As per Treasury Regulation § 1.671-4(b), no separate Tax Identification Number is required for this type of trust.If you have any questions regarding this transfer, please contact [me/us] using the information listed below. Thank you for your assistance and prompt attention to this matter.

Letter to Client 
[name of law firm][law firm address][law firm phone number][month] [day], [year][name(s) of client(s)][address of client(s)]RE: Estate Planning Document DraftsDear [name(s) of client(s)]:I am pleased to enclose for your review and approval drafts of the following documents:Last Will and Testament. Your Last Will(s) and Testament(s) govern(s) the disposition of any property held in your individual name(s) at the time of your death. [It/They] also provide(s) for the administration of that property, the payment of your debts, expenses of administration, and the appointment of an executor, who carries out the administration of your estate.Trust Agreement. The Trust Agreement(s) create(s) a revocable trust, sometimes referred to as a Living Trust or Revocable Living Trust. The Trust(s) [is/are] entirely revocable and amendable by you during your lifetime(s), but the terms become irrevocable at death. Any property that is transferred to the Trust(s) during your lifetime(s) or upon your death(s) will be disposed of in accordance with the terms of the Trust Agreement(s) upon your death(s).Certification. The Certification, also known as Extract of Trust, is an alternative to providing a full copy of your Trust Agreement(s) to a requesting third party. It contains only the minimum amount of information as required by Section 9:2092 of the Louisiana Revised Statutes and allows you to keep sensitive portions of your Trust Agreement confidential, such as the disposition of property upon your death.Power(s) of Attorney. Your Power(s) of Attorney nominate(s) an individual to serve as your agent. Your agent is given the power to act on your behalf, as if you were present and acting, with respect to your property. When executing this document, you may specify the types of transactions that your agent can engage in on your behalf. Your Power(s) of Attorney are effective [immediately upon execution/upon your disability, as certified by the individuals stated in the document/upon your disability or incapacity, as certified by a physician].Advance Directive (Health Care Power of Attorney). Your Health Care Power(s) of Attorney allow(s) you to nominate an individual to serve as your health care agent to make medical care decisions on your behalf. When signing this document, you must specify when the agent's authority is to become effective and whether you wish to limit the agent's decision-making authority. Further, this document enables you to state your preferences concerning life-sustaining treatment. Additionally, the Health Care Power(s) of Attorney include(s) language related to the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996 (HIPAA) and authorize(s) your health care providers to disclose your medical records to your agent.Please review your documents thoroughly, taking note of the spelling of names in the documents so that any typographical errors can be corrected prior to the signing ceremony. After you have had the opportunity to review, please contact me to discuss any questions or concerns and any revisions that you would like made to the documents prior to signing. Once you have approved the drafts of your documents, we can schedule a time to sign the final documents. I look forward to assisting you [both] through the remainder of the estate planning process.
Sincerely yours,[firm name]____________________[attorney name][attorney's email address][attorney's phone number]Enclosures: Estate Planning Document Drafts for [client name(s)]End of Document

Revocation, Amendment, and Termination of Trusts (GA)
Go to: Amending, Revoking, or Terminating a TrustMaintainedReviewed by Elise MyersThis practice note provides guidance on how to revoke, amend, or terminate a trust under Georgia law, distinguishing between the procedures for revocable and irrevocable trusts. It discusses amendment, reformation, modification, division, consolidation, revocation, and termination under the Revised Georgia Trust Code of 2010. In addition, the note sets forth practice considerations specifically pertaining to revocation, amendment, and termination of trusts.For a full listing of key content on creating an estate plan for an individual with a spouse or partner residing in Georgia, see Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (GA).For an in-depth discussion of different types of trusts and the purposes of a trust, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (GA). For more information on drafting and administering a trust in Georgia, see Requirements and Restrictions on Trust Purposes and Administration (GA).Amending, Revoking, or Terminating a TrustThe need for amendment, revocation, and termination usually arises in association with changed circumstances surrounding a trust, including changes to applicable law. As it is impossible to foresee every situation that may come to effect a trust, the estate planning practitioner must address the issue of flexibility with the client and provide a mechanism by which changes can be made. The settlor, beneficiary, or trustee's ability to amend, revoke, or terminate a trust, absent court intervention, depends entirely upon the terms of the trust instrument. Explicit reservation of these powers provides the settlor and other interested parties with the flexibility essential to address future, unknown situations.Too little flexibility can restrict the trust's ability to accomplish its goals. Too much flexibility may give certain individuals an opportunity to take action that contradicts the settlor's intentions. In addition, many settlors fear that granting too much flexibility to amend, revoke, or terminate the trust will result in their intentions and goals for the trust being discarded. When drafting workable trust provisions, the estate planning practitioner must balance the level of flexibility, for example by:• Prohibiting a beneficiary who is also the sole trustee from unilaterally amending, revoking, or terminating a trust• In the case of co-trustees, requiring permitted amendments, revocations, or terminations to be by unanimous consent or by the will of the majority• Requiring amendment, revocation, or termination to be predicated on good causeFor more information on drafting workable yet flexible trust instruments, see 1 Georgia Estate Planning § 8.12.Amending a TrustAlthough a trust may have been planned to take into account every conceivable contingency, circumstances nevertheless occur that were not present or contemplated when the trust was drafted. Such circumstances may lead the settlor to want to amend the trust. The extent to which a trust may be amended and the procedures for doing so depend largely on whether the trust, as drafted, is revocable or irrevocable.Revocable TrustsA settlor does not have power to amend a trust in the absence of an express reservation of that power within the trust instrument. See Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-40(a). In Georgia, the power of revocation includes the power to modify and an unrestricted power to modify includes the power to revoke. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-40(b). Settlors should be made aware that a trust is not considered revocable simply because a life beneficiary has a revision in or a power of appointment over assets of the trust, or because a life beneficiary's heirs or estate have a remainder interest in it. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-44. See also 1 Georgia Estate Planning § 9.2Modification of an express trust must be in writing and signed by the settlor. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-40(c). Further, the settlor must give notice of the amendment to the trustee, otherwise the trustee is not liable for failing to act in accordance with the amended terms and conditions of the trust agreement. See Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-42. As a best practice, any amendment should be executed with the same formalities as the original trust instrument and the amendment should refer specifically to the trust and to the particular provision of the trust being amended.The trust instrument ultimately controls the settlor's ability to amend the trust. If this provision is carefully drafted, the settlor can retain an enormous amount of flexibility as to how the trust is amended. For example, courts have allowed a trust provision providing for alteration by "execution of a written instrument" to allow modification of the trust by power of attorney. While executing a proper amendment is ideal, flexibility provided to a settlor may be necessary and advantageous in certain circumstances. Strange v. Towns, 769 S.E.2d 604 (Ga. Ct. App. 2015). A settlor's powers with respect to amendment of a trust may be exercised by an agent under a power of attorney, but only to the extent expressly authorized by the terms of the trust and the power of attorney instrument. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-43(a). The ability of the settlor's conservator to amend the trust is constrained by Ga. Code Ann. § 29-5-23. See Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-43(b).Counsel should be aware that trustee consent is required for certain modifications. In exercising a power to modify the trust instrument, the settlor may not enlarge the duties or liabilities of the trustee without the trustee's express consent. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-41.Irrevocable TrustsAn irrevocable trust may not be amended except in limited situations permitted by statute. The only way to effect a change in an irrevocable trust is to petition the court for modification of the trust under the provisions of Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61. In addition, the court may in some circumstances reform the terms of a trust to correct a mistake. See Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-60.A proceeding to approve a proposed modification of a trust under Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61 may be commenced by either a trustee, trust director, or a beneficiary. A proceeding may also be commenced by the settlor during his or her lifetime. In the case of an unfunded testamentary trust, the petition may be brought by the personal representative of the settlor's estate. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(e).Modification of an irrevocable trust may be made upon petition to the court in the following circumstances:• Modification with settlor. If the court finds that the settlor and all beneficiaries' consent to the modification or termination of an irrevocable trust, the court shall approve the modification or termination even if the change is inconsistent with a material purpose of the trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(b).• Modification by consent. After the death of the settlor, the court may, on petition, modify the terms of an irrevocable trust only if (1) all of the beneficiaries' consent, (2) the trustee receives notice, and (3) the court finds that the modification is not inconsistent with any material purpose of the trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(c)(1). After the death of the settlor the court may, on petition, terminate the irrevocable trust. See Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(c)(2).• Unanticipated circumstances. The court may modify an irrevocable trust when there are circumstances not anticipated by the settlor and if such modification would further the purposes of such trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(1).• Inability to administer the trust effectively. The court may modify the trust's administrative terms if continuing the trust on its existing terms would be impracticable and would impair the trust's administration. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(2).• Appointment of additional trustee or special fiduciary. The court may modify the terms of the trust by the appointment of an additional trustee or special fiduciary if such appointment is necessary or helpful to the administration of the trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(3).• Tax objectives. The court may modify the trust to achieve the settlor's tax objectives. Such a modification can be either prospective or retroactive. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(4).• Division and consolidation. The court may order the division of a single trust into two or more trusts. It may also order the consolidation of two or more trusts—whether created by the same or different trust instruments or by the same or different persons—into a single trust. This modification will be effectuated only if the division or consolidation would be helpful to the administration of the trust or trusts. Counsel should be cognizant that the trust instrument itself may confer upon the trustee, or another person, the power to consolidate or divide the trust or trusts without court approval. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(a). Absent the grant of power to combine or consolidate, however, the trustee must petition the court for combination or division of trusts pursuant to Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(5).• Uneconomical trust. The court may modify or terminate a trust and order the distribution of the trust property if:o The costs of administration are such that the continuance of such trust, the establishment of such trust if it is to be established, or the distribution from a probate estate would defeat or substantially impair the purposes of such trusto The purpose of such trust has been fulfilled or become illegal or impossible to fulfill –or–o Continuance of such trust would impair the accomplishment of the purposes of the trustGa. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(6)Reformation to Correct MistakesA trust can be reformed to correct mistakes. If it is proved by clear and convincing evidence that the settlor's intent or the terms of the trust were affected by a mistake of fact or law (whether in expression or inducement), the court may reform the terms of a trust to conform to the settlor's intention. The court may reform the trust terms even if such terms are unambiguous. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-60(a).Reformation of a trust by the court must be upon petition by the trustee or a beneficiary. If the trust is an unfunded testamentary trust, the personal representative of the settlor's estate has standing to petition for reformation. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-60(b). All trustees and beneficiaries are entitled to notice of a petition for reformation of a trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-60(c).Revoking a TrustA settlor may not revoke a trust unless he or she has expressly reserved that power in the trust instrument. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-40(a). Further, the power to revoke the trust includes the power to modify it; and an unrestricted power to modify is deemed to include the power of revocation. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-40(b). Revocation of the trust must be in writing and signed by the settlor. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-40(c). See also 1 Georgia Estate Planning § 9.2.The settlor should inform the trustee of the revocation. A trustee who remains unaware that a trust has been revoked is not liable for failing to act in accordance with the revocation. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-42. Lack of notice of termination to a trustee could result in distributions, incursion of liability, and other acts not contemplated by the settlor when he or she revoked the trust. The best practice is for the settlor to send the trustee a copy of the revoking instrument by a traceable method such as certified or registered mail, or another parcel delivery service that provides tracking. A settlor may also wish to record a revocation into the real property records of any county where real estate assets of the trust are located. This recordation, while not required, will assist title companies in preventing fraudulent transfers of trust property after a revocation has been made but before title of the real estate has reverted to the settlor.A settlor's powers with respect to revocation of a trust may be exercised by an agent under a power of attorney, but only to the extent expressly authorized by the terms of the trust and the power of attorney. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-43(a). The ability of the settlor's conservator to revoke the trust is constrained by Ga. Code Ann. § 29-5-23 which codifies the authority of a conservator and enumerates his or her powers. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-43(b).Terminating a TrustTermination by ExpirationA trust terminates if it expires under its own terms. Expiration under the terms of the trust typically occurs when a terminating event defined in the instrument transpires. Common terminating events include:• Age. Termination may occur when one or all of the beneficiaries attain a specified age. Age-based termination is particularly useful for parents and older relatives who wish to leave bequests to minor children or beneficiaries who are too young to manage a bequest prudently. An age-based termination protects minors from outright inheritance being subjected to guardianship provisions and allows time for beneficiaries to mature.• Death. Termination may occur when all or a specified beneficiary dies. This type of termination is useful in situations involving marital trusts, where upon a surviving spouse's death, a trust terminates with distributions to the couple's descendants. In the case of pet trusts under Section 53-12-28 of the Georgia Code, the trust terminates upon the death of the last surviving animal-beneficiary. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-28(a).• Contingent term completed. Termination may occur upon completion of a specified trust term upon which termination is contingent. As a trust may be established for any legal purpose, the settlor has a broad range of contingent termination events available. Completion of college or vocational school is one common contingent termination event employed by parents establishing trusts for children or grandchildren. Another increasingly common contingent termination event is a beneficiary's sobriety. Settlors may wish to include contingent termination language based on the beneficiary becoming sober. The practitioner should be careful to address all aspects of this issue, such as the potential for relapse, and how and by whom sobriety is determined.Counsel is advised to draft thorough termination provisions to account for a wide array of circumstances.Termination of Irrevocable Trust by ConsentDuring the settlor's lifetime, a court may, upon petition, terminate an irrevocable trust under Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(b). If the court finds that the settlor and all beneficiaries consent and proper notice was given to the trustee, the court may approve the termination even if it is inconsistent with a material purpose of the trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(b). In the event that the settlor is still living but unable to consent to such termination, the settlor's power to consent to termination may be exercised by:• An agent under a power of attorney, only to the extent expressly authorized by the power of attorney and the terms of the trust• The settlor's conservator, with the approval of the court supervising the conservatorship if an agent is not so authorized• The settlor's guardian, with the approval of the court supervising the guardianship if an agent is not so authorized and a conservator has not been appointedGa. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(b)(1)–(3).Following the settlor's death, the court may terminate an irrevocable trust under Section 53-12-61(c). The trust may be terminated if the court finds that:• All of the beneficiaries' consent to the termination• The trustee has received notice of the proposed termination –and–• The court concludes that continuance of such trust is not necessary to achieve any material purpose of such trustGa. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(c)(2). If the client anticipates his or her power of attorney agent exercising the settlor's powers under the trust instrument, counsel must ensure that the authorization for such exercise of power is present in the power of attorney instrument as well as the trust instrument. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(b)(1).Termination Due to Cost of AdministrationUpon petition, the court may terminate a trust and order the distribution of the trust property if the costs of administration are such that the continuance of the trust, the establishment of the trust if it is to be established, or the distribution from a probate estate would defeat or substantially impair the purposes of the trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(6)(A).Termination Based on PurposeUpon petition, the court may terminate a trust and order the distribution of the trust property if the purpose of the trust has:• Been fulfilled• Become illegal to fulfill –or–• Become impossible to fulfillGa. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(d)(6)(B).Termination Due to ImpairmentUpon petition, the court may terminate a trust and order the distribution of the trust property if the continuance of such trust would impair the accomplishments of the trust's purposes. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61(c)(6)(C).Termination of Uneconomical TrustWhen the value of the trust property is under $100,000 or the trustee's annual fee exceeds 5% of the market value of the principal assets, the trustee can terminate the trust if he or she concludes that the value of the trust property is insufficient to justify the cost of administration. The trustee must notify the qualified beneficiaries prior to termination. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-65(a). The court may also terminate a trust—or remove the trustee and appoint a different one—if the court determines that the value of the trust property is insufficient to justify the administration cost. Ga Code Ann. § 53-12-65(b). If a trust is terminated under Section 53-12-65(a), the trustee must distribute the trust property in a manner consistent with the purposes of the trust. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-65(c).Sections 53-12-65(a)–(b) do not apply to an easement for conservation or to trusts governed by Chapter 14 of Title 10 of the Georgia Code pertaining to cemetery and funeral services. Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-65(d) and 65(e).Practice ConsiderationsAmendment, revocation, and termination must also be considered in the context of practice. Estate planning practitioners are frequently faced with conflicts of interest, undue influence, and issues concerning capacity. These situations are especially common for the estate planner who drafts trusts and who may be asked to represent multiple parties with different roles pertaining to the instrument.The estate planner will in all likelihood be asked to represent settlors, beneficiaries, trustees, or any combination of individuals involved with the trust. When undertaking representation of these multiple parties, the practitioner must be aware of potential ethical pitfalls.Duty of LoyaltyLoyalty and independent judgment are essential elements of the lawyer's relationship to a client. See Ga. R. Prof Conduct 1.7 Cmt. 1. Loyalty to a client encompasses prohibitions against representing clients who have directly adverse interests. Within the meaning of Rule 1.7(a), the representation of one client is impaired by the representation of another client "when a lawyer cannot consider, recommend, or carry out an appropriate course of action for the client because of the lawyer's other competing responsibilities or interests." See Ga. R. Prof Conduct 1.7 Cmt. 2. Consider the following examples of the role of the duty of loyalty in estate planning practice:• Example 1. The estate planner is approached by the remainder beneficiary, who wishes to retain the estate planner to seek to terminate the trust under Ga. Code Ann. § 53-12-61. May the estate planner represent the remainder beneficiary? In all likelihood, no. If the remainder beneficiary is seeking termination in contradiction of the trust instrument or the settlor's interests, a conflict of interest violating the duty of loyalty would likely be created.• Example 2. The estate planner drafted an irrevocable trust for the settlor. The estate planner is later approached by the trustee, who requests the estate planner draft a Certification of Trust for the trustee's use. May the estate planner draft the requested document for the trustee without creating a conflict of interest that violates the duty of loyalty to the settlor? In all likelihood, yes. The trustee's request for a Certification of Trust does not conflict with the settlor's interests and likely aids the trustee in fulfilling the settlor's intentions under the trust.As a best practice, the estate planner should not undertake the type of representation in either example until the settlor or the settlor's agent has provided consent under Ga. R. Prof Conduct 1.7(b).Incapacity and Undue InfluenceEqually important to the estate planning practitioner are the issues of client incapacity and undue influence. Settlors, trustees, and beneficiaries are all subject to possible legal incapacity or lack of free will. For example, an incapacitated settlor may wish to amend the terms of a trust to conform with a delusion caused by the incapacity. Or the assertion of undue influence may persuade a settlor to revoke a trust against the settlor's own best interests. The estate planner must proceed cautiously when presented with a situation involving a potentially compromised individual in order to adhere to standards of professional conduct, and to best advise the parties with regard to the settlor's intentions and the trust purposes.For an in-depth discussion of the ethical issues involved in estate planning, see Ethical Issues in Estate Planning (GA), 2 Georgia Estate Planning § 14.1, 1 Tax, Estate & Financial Planning for the Elderly § 18.04, and 1 Tax, Estate & Financial Planning for the Elderly § 18.06.Current as of: 09/18/2024End of Document

Your Power(s) of Attorney for Health Care allow(s) you to nominate an individual to serve as your health care agent to make medical care decisions on your behalf. When signing this document, you must specify when the agent's authority is to become effective and whether you wish to limit the agent's decision-making authority. Further, this document enables you to state your preference concerning life-sustaining treatment. Additionally, the Medical Power(s) of Attorney include(s) language related to the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996 (HIPAA) and authorize(s) your health care providers to disclose your medical records to your agent.Please review your documents thoroughly, taking note of the spelling of names in the documents so that any typographical errors can be corrected prior to the signing ceremony. After you have had the opportunity to review, please contact me to discuss any questions or concerns you may have and any revisions that you would like made to the documents prior to signing. Once you have approved the drafts of your documents, we can schedule a time to sign the final documents. I look forward to assisting you [both] through the remainder of the estate planning process.

Letter to Client 
[Month] [Day], [Year][name of client][address of client]RE: Funding of Revocable TrustDear [name of client]:Now that you have established your Revocable Trust (the "Trust"), you should fund it by transferring ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. An unfunded or partially funded trust will not avoid probate. While your pour-over will provides that assets held in your individual name at your death will be added to the Trust and distributed as provided therein, those assets will be subject to probate before they are added to the Trust. To avoid that result and any attendant delays, you may wish to retitle all assets in the name of your Trust as further outlined in this letter.Please review your assets to determine which to transfer into your Trust. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property located in any state or country, tangible personal property, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you may hold. Generally, certain assets should not be transferred to the trust during your lifetime, for example, IRAs and certain types of annuities.In general, to transfer assets to your Trust, you will need to change the title for each asset from your name individually to the Revocable Trust. This may require you to execute new documents of title, deeds to real property, and signature cards for your bank accounts. We prefer the following format for titling assets in the name of your Trust:[Name of client], as Trustee of the [name of revocable trust] dated [month] [day], [year], and any amendments thereto.We recommend you keep copies of all written documents which evidence the ownership interest of your Trust. Additionally, it is good practice to keep a list of all assets transferred to the Trust, along with the date of transfer, on Schedule A of the Trust.You should carefully and continually review the assets held in your own name (both assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this is consistent with your estate planning goals. I will be glad to discuss the advisability of retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, holding assets in joint names with another person, or transferring assets to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust. On the following pages, I offer some general guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your Revocable Trust. These comments are general in nature, and if you require specific guidance, or if you would like assistance with any of these transfers, please contact my office.Instructions for Transferring Specific AssetsCash AccountsYou should sign new signature and ownership cards to retitle any sizeable bank accounts or cash equivalents, including treasury bills, money market accounts, and certificates of deposit, to name yourself as Trustee of those accounts. You need not change any small joint or other checking accounts used primarily for household expenses.Before you retitle your certificates of deposit, consult with a bank officer to make sure that the institution does not consider the change in account name to be an early withdrawal that incurs a penalty. Generally, this should not be a problem because your tax identification number for the account will remain the same.Instruct your financial institution by letter or in person to change the title to your Trust. The tax identification number (your Social Security number) on the account for withholding and reporting purposes will remain the same. Sign the new signature cards as Trustee.Investment AccountsIf you hold publicly traded stocks and bonds through brokerage or investment accounts, contact your brokers or custodians and direct them to change the title of the accounts to the name of your Trust. The procedure for doing so is the same as the procedure for retitling cash accounts explained above. You may have to complete new account applications and present a copy of your Trust Agreement or Certification of Trust in order to change the title. Title to the accounts should be in the trust name.Stocks and Bonds Not Held in Investment AccountsIf you possess original stock or bond certificates, new certificates may be issued to reflect the Trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. Alternatively, you may open a brokerage or investment account in the name of your Revocable Trust and deposit your original certificates in the account.Personal EffectsTangible personal property refers to such items as household furnishings, appliances and fixtures, works of art, motor vehicles, pictures, collectibles, personal wearing apparel and jewelry, books, sporting goods, and hobby paraphernalia. You can transfer your tangible personal property by executing an Assignment of Personal Property.Your personal vehicles can be titled in the name of your Revocable Trust, but we find that most of our clients prefer to leave their vehicles outside their trust. However, should you decide to title your vehicle in the name of the Trust, consult your casualty insurance agent and advise them of the change.Retirement Plans, Life Insurance Policies, and AnnuitiesGenerally speaking, ownership of these accounts should not be transferred to your Trust, but the death benefits can be made payable to the Trustee for distribution under the terms of the Trust. Although life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate, you may wish to transfer ownership to your Trust to give the Trustee more control over the policies in case you become disabled. Alternatively, you may want the proceeds from the policies paid directly to your Revocable Trust. In order to designate the Trust as the owner and/or beneficiary, you will need to obtain and complete Change of Ownership and/or Change of Beneficiary forms. Your agent should be able to assist you with naming the Trustee of your Revocable Trust as the beneficiary of the policy, or if not, please contact me and I will be happy to assist you. You should receive a letter confirming the change of beneficiary from the insurance company to keep with your trust records.Ownership of IRAs should not be transferred to the trust. Designating the Trust as beneficiary of an annuity or retirement account may have unintended income tax consequences. Please contact me or your tax professional prior to making any such designation.Real PropertyTransferring real property to your trust will require attention to ownership and tax issues based on the nature of the current title to the property. You will need to execute new deeds for each property. At your request, we will assist you with the transfer of the property located in New Jersey.Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer and whether the proposed transfer will trigger a "due on sale" clause in the instrument. If there is an open mortgage of record, you should obtain the bank's consent prior to executing and recording the deed.You should contact your homeowner's insurance provider to discuss adding your Trust as an additional insured on your policy. Further, you may wish to consult with a title agent to ensure that the transfer to the Trust will not void the title policy.Administering the TrustIf a bank, financial institution, or other governing body requires evidence of the Revocable Trust to complete the transfer, use the Certification of Trust rather than producing a copy of the entire trust agreement. The Certification of Trust provides only the necessary information without disclosing confidential details.Once an asset has been transferred to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust, all transactions regarding that asset must be handled by the Trustee, rather than by you individually. For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the Trustee, it is the Trustee who has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting. Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the Trustee.As long as you are living, you do not need to obtain a separate tax identification number for your trust or to file a separate trust tax return. The Internal Revenue Service (IRS) prefers that you use your own Social Security number. You should report all of the income generated by trust assets on your personal IRS Form 1040.When you die, your trust becomes irrevocable and the successor trustee will need to obtain a tax identification number. We will work with your successor trustee and your accountant to see that your heirs get the full benefit of your estate planning upon your death.You should review your estate plan with an attorney every two to three years because all estate plans require on-going maintenance. In particular, a change in your family circumstances, an increase in your net worth, or a change in the tax law could significantly impact the effectiveness of your plan.I hope that this letter answers most, if not all, of your immediate questions. Please call me if you have any additional questions about the transfer of property to your Trust or about the operation of the Trust in the future. At death, certain trust administration procedures will be required, and it is advisable for the successor trustee to consult with an attorney at that time. This letter is for your guidance only and does not discuss all possible situations. Please keep it for future reference.____________________[name of attorney]End of Document

When signing this document, you must specify when the agent's authority is to become effective and whether you wish to limit his or her decision-making authority. Further, this document enables you to state your preference concerning life-sustaining treatment. Additionally, the Health Care Power(s) of Attorney include(s) language related to the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996 (HIPAA) and authorize(s) your health care providers to disclose your medical records to your agent.Please review your documents thoroughly, taking note of the spelling of names in the documents so that any typographical errors can be corrected prior to the signing ceremony. After you have had the opportunity to review, please contact me to discuss any questions or concerns you may have and any revisions that you would like made to the documents prior to signing. Once you have approved the drafts of your documents, we can schedule a time to sign the final documents. I look forward to assisting you [both] through the remainder of the estate planning process.
Sincerely yours,[firm name]____________________[attorney name][attorney's email address][attorney's phone number]Enclosures: Estate Planning Document Drafts for [client name(s)]End of Document

Funding Revocable Trusts
Go to: How to Transfer or Place Specific Assets into a Revocable Living Trust   |   Other Funding IssuesMaintainedThis practice note contains information and instructions on how to fund a revocable trust. It is intended to be a general overview and is not tailored to any particular state law. You should review your applicable state's law for particular information and instructions as it may differ from these general instructions.One of the primary benefits of a revocable living trust is that, if fully and properly funded during the grantor's lifetime, probate can be avoided. Therefore, it is vitally important to transfer all of a grantor's assets into the trust, ideally soon after the creation of the trust, and then on an ongoing basis as needed throughout the grantor's lifetime. This practice note offers guidance to you when funding a revocable living trust for your clients, as well as when advising your clients as to how they should fund it.Trust funding must be taken seriously and professionally, as errors can result in an ineffective estate plan, client complaints, and malpractice claims. Some attorneys choose not to involve themselves in the funding process. Others assume responsibility for certain tasks and not for others. To the extent possible, you should determine with your client in advance who will be responsible for which tasks and clearly explain that all tasks other than those you expressly agree to undertake will be the responsibility of the client unless you subsequently agree otherwise (ideally in writing). In situations where the client is to perform some or all funding tasks, you may wish to send a follow-up/reminder letter approximately 60 days after the trust is created to reiterate the importance of funding the trust and to see if they wish to involve your help with any remaining tasks. If you choose not to take on the responsibility of funding the trust for your client, or if the client declines your offer to help, you should at least provide explicit instructions about the process to the client.A grantor's assets must be transferred into the trust if the trust is to achieve its objectives. An essential objective of the trust is to simplify the transfer and administration of assets upon the grantor's incapacity and after the grantor's death. Funding is necessary because the trustee only has control of the property if it is held in the trust. If the grantor becomes incapacitated, the chosen successor trustee(s) will be able to continue managing trust assets without court involvement or other difficulty if the grantor's assets are in the trust at that time.To the extent that the grantor does not transfer assets into the trust or otherwise provide for their transfer upon death through beneficiary designation or similar forms, those assets will not escape probate. That is why a pour-over will should always be prepared along with the trust agreement to direct that any assets not held by the trust at the time of the grantor's death be transferred into the trust so that they will be distributed in accordance with the provisions of the trust. However, a probate proceeding will be necessary to accomplish this. See Modern Estate Planning § 37.26 for additional information.For a full listing of key content related to estate plans, see Estate Planning Essentials Resource Kit.For related practice notes, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts and Requirements and Restrictions on Trust Purposes and Administration.For more discussion of funding of revocable trusts, see Modern Estate Planning §§ 37.25, 26, and 28, and Living Trusts: Forms and Practice § 7.02–7.07.How to Transfer or Place Specific Assets into a Revocable Living TrustThe grantor must assign each titled asset separately into the name of the revocable trust to ensure that the transfer of those assets has legal effect. Certain assets may need special consideration as to whether they should be transferred to the trust. Funding a trust is a multistep process, as summarized in the below image.Visualization of Revocable Trust Drafting and Funding Flowchart.The assets discussed below are commonly held by a grantor. You should be mindful to get a clear and accurate picture of the types of assets your client owns so you can provide appropriate advice and try to ensure everything that must be transferred to the trust gets transferred.Personal ResidenceIn most cases, the grantor's interest in their home should be transferred from their own name to the name of the trustee (usually, the client is the trustee). Recording a deed and filing any necessary transfer tax forms (if applicable in the client's jurisdiction) will achieve this purpose. For a modest fee, a title insurance company or attorney can help make sure everything is done properly and efficiently. If the client undertakes this task, you should ask to be sent a copy of the recorded deed.A few special points about the transfer of a residence or any other real property into a revocable living trust:• There should be no reassessment of property taxes because the transfer of real property to a revocable trust is not a "change in ownership" for property tax purposes. The beneficial owner is still the grantor. However, you should confirm this is the case in the applicable state.• There should be no transfer tax because there has been no transfer of beneficial interest. Typically, for transfer tax to apply, there needs to be a transfer of beneficial interest. However, depending on your jurisdiction, appropriate transfer tax forms may need to be filed, indicating that it is a transfer for no consideration to a revocable trust.• While it is not legally required that the new deed be recorded right away, it is highly advisable to do so unless the terms of any mortgage contain language that makes it unwise. For example, most mortgage agreements have what is called an acceleration or "due on sale" clause, which means that the full balance on the mortgage becomes immediately due if there is a change in ownership. Since transferring the home into the trust is not a change of ownership of the type a due on sale clause is meant to address, you or the client should contact the lender, explain what you are trying to do, and confirm that the due on sale clause will not be triggered. See 12 U.S.C. § 1701j-3(d)(8). Whenever possible, you or the client should get this in writing from the lender.Cooperative or Condominium ApartmentsIf the client has such an ownership interest in a cooperative or condominium, you or the client should ask the manager of the cooperative or condominium about how it should be transferred into the trust. It is likely the management will have various formalities and fees associated with the transfer, but it is nevertheless important that the transfer be made. You should try to explain that this is not an actual transfer of beneficial ownership and that fees or certain formalities need not or should not apply, but the ultimate decision will rest with the cooperative or condominium.Please also note that if the client chooses to refinance their mortgage after transferring the cooperative apartment or condominium into the trust, they may have to transfer it out of the trust first in order to refinance due to the mortgage company's requirement, and then transfer it back into the trust after the refinance is complete. You or the client should always consult with the lender when making these types of changes to ensure compliance with the terms of the loan agreement.Other Real PropertyA new deed is required for every other piece of real property that the client owns. As noted above, it may be necessary to obtain the permission of the lender to transfer the property into the trust. If the other real property is located in another state, you or the client should consult an attorney in that state to assist in transferring that property, as different states have different rules and customs, especially as it pertains to deed formalities and requirements. If your client purchases real estate in the future, they should generally take title in their trustee capacity from the beginning. Often, clients obtain the real estate in their name and then ask the attorney to help them transfer it to their trust. If the client fails to transfer such new real property into the trust before death, the new real property must go through the probate process.Savings Accounts and Certificates of DepositThese accounts should be held by the trustee as trustee of the revocable trust. Banks and similar institutions commonly see revocable trusts and are used to helping their customers with necessary changes. A copy of the trust instrument or a Certificate of Trust (also referred to as Certification of Trust in some states) is usually required as proof that the trust exists. The Certificate of Trust provides the information necessary to identify the trust, including the grantor's name, the trustee's name, the trust's address, the date the trust was created, and the trustee's powers. See Certificate of Trust (NY) for a sample certificate of trust template. Suggest that the bank make a copy if they require it for their records. The client should keep a copy of any new card or document that they sign to effect the change. If a particular savings account cannot be transferred before a set date without losing interest, the client may decide to wait. It may be necessary to wait until a Certificate of Deposit's maturity date before it can be retitled to avoid any penalties. For a non-jurisdictional letter directing the transfer of accounts to a revocable trust, see Letter to Financial Institution (Transfer of Accounts to Revocable Trust).Checking AccountsWhile such accounts can and, in almost all cases, should be put into the name of the trust, the client may want, for a special reason, to leave one account in their name individually. One such reason could be that the client pays all of their bills using that account and transferring it to the trust would require new checkbooks and notifying each accountholder to update their records. However, any such account should maintain a low balance to the extent possible. Bank records must indicate that accounts are held in the name of the trust, but personal checks can be printed with the client's name only, without reference to the trust.Life Insurance PoliciesYou or the client must contact their insurance agent or the company itself to obtain their "Change of Beneficiary" and "Change of Ownership" forms. In many cases, the owner and the beneficiary should be the trust. You could make a trust the owner of an existing policy. To transfer an existing policy, you would have to contact the insurance company and comply by its requirements. Most often, in the case of an existing policy, the grantor makes the trust the beneficiary upon the grantor's death. If the grantor desires the insurance proceeds to be managed a certain way or to be distributed to specific beneficiaries unlike the other assets held in the trust, then the specifics in regard to such insurance policy should be clearly spelled out in the trust document. You or the client should take care to make the transfer of the life insurance policy or to designate the trust as its beneficiary as soon as possible. Be sure to receive written confirmation from the insurance company that the desired changes have been effected in company records and by endorsement on the policy. If not, you or the client should follow up periodically until the company sends confirmation of such change. See Modern Estate Planning § 37.17 for additional information.If the client has personal reasons for naming a particular individual as beneficiary instead of the trust, they certainly may do so. This means that the proceeds of the policy will be distributed directly to the named beneficiaries, and not according to the terms of the trust. There are differing opinions and considerations on whether to name individuals or the trust as the primary beneficiary of life insurance policies, and these opinions and considerations should be discussed with the client to determine the primary beneficiary of such policies. For example, if the client wants an outright distribution of insurance proceeds to a named beneficiary who is an adult and feels that person should have access to those proceeds quickly, then naming the individual as a beneficiary instead of the trust might be advisable. Also, while creditors often can go after insurance proceeds to satisfy any debts of the deceased grantor, it is perhaps less likely that will happen if the proceeds pay out directly to a named beneficiary as opposed to them being paid out to the trust.Insurance on Property Held by the TrustInsurance contracts that provide for payments to be made if there is loss or damage to assets held in trust (e.g., title insurance and homeowner's insurance) should be amended. You or the client should ask their insurance agent to have the policy or policies amended. The trustee(s) and the trust should be named as additional insured parties, using the "name" suggested under Other Funding Issues below.The grantor, as an individual, should continue to be named as an insured party even though the trust and trustee are added as additional insured parties.Stock/SecuritiesThe transfer of stock to the trust must be accomplished by changing title on the stock certificates themselves. The client's broker can help make this change for free or for a small charge per certificate. If your client holds shares through a brokerage firm, the client will need to change the title on only the brokerage accounts, rather than on each share certificate.The stockbroker may request a copy of the signed trust instrument for examination by the firm's legal department. A copy of the signature page and the trustee powers should be sufficient to demonstrate that the trust is properly executed and the trustee has the necessary power to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds, and options, trade on margin, and conduct other trust business. Alternatively, you or the client may be able to simply provide a copy of the Certificate of Trust or equivalent document.If the client holds shares in a closely held corporation, the corporation's secretary should issue new certificates in the name of the trust. The existing (old) certificates should then be canceled. Before this is done, however, it should be determined whether there is a stock restriction agreement, whether an assignment might cause some sort of forfeiture, and whether the board must approve the transfer. It would be wise to consult with corporate counsel in this regard.You or the client should send any stock certificates via registered mail, return receipt requested, with enough insurance to cover the replacement bond, in case the certificates are lost in the mail. The amount of this bond can be obtained from the brokerage, and it guards against someone else trying to transfer the certificates if they somehow fall into someone else's hands.PartnershipsPartnership interests can be complicated. A limited partnership agreement may indicate what is needed to transfer the interest. Such an agreement should be reviewed to determine (1) whether or not the client can transfer their interest into the trust and, if so, (2) how to achieve this result. See Modern Estate Planning § 37.20 for additional information.Limited Liability CompaniesLimited liability companies are similar to closely held corporations and partnerships. Instead of ''shares'' or ''stock,'' the owners of a limited liability company own ''membership units'' or ''membership interests.'' Different states may refer to such ownership using different terms. They can involve certificates, which would have to be transferred in the same way as a corporation's certificate. They can also have a membership interest restriction agreement in place, which can restrict the transfer of membership units similar to a stock restriction agreement. A limited liability company's operating agreement, which is similar to the partnership agreement, could also provide for restrictions on the transfer of membership units.Promissory NotesThe client should assign each promissory note owned to the trust. The client can do so by typing on the back of each note the following endorsement:Pay to the order of ______________________, Trustee under ______________________ Trust Agreement Dated ______________________ ______________________.Go to table1The client should also assign the deeds of trust securing payment of the notes to the trust. You or the client need not record the assignment, but it should be prepared in the same manner as deeds to real property and should be retained in a safe place. If the trustee or a successor trustee needs to commence any action to obtain title to the property given as the security for payment of the note, the assignment can be recorded. For a non-jurisdictional promissory note assignment template, see Assignment of Unsecured Promissory Note to Revocable Trust.Alternatively, the client could sign an ''Allonge'' to the promissory note if allowed under the applicable jurisdiction, which is a simple declaration of assignment.IRAsIRAs must be held in the name of the individual who created the account and their ownership cannot be changed. The client can only name beneficiaries on a "designation of beneficiary" form provided by the financial institution. Normally, a married person will name their spouse as the first beneficiary (because the spouse can roll over the IRA into their own IRA on the client's death), and, as alternate beneficiary, either the trust or individuals. Individuals as beneficiaries may have a few more tax elections than the trust will have. Naming the trust as beneficiary will have less such options, but would ensure that the proceeds of the account pay to beneficiaries in accordance with the terms of the trust.Retirement BenefitsRetirement benefits require special consideration since both the form of benefit and the choice of beneficiary affect the client's estate plan and can have significant tax consequences. These choices also have a major impact on married persons' lives in the event one spouse enters a nursing home. The client should consult with the plan administrator about the options available now and in the future. The necessary forms may require specific wording in order for your married clients to be eligible for the marital deduction and other benefits.If your client's employment terminated before 1985, they may be entitled to certain important tax advantages. The client should review their benefits with a pension specialist prior to making any changes to the retirement benefits.Normally a married client should first designate their spouse as the beneficiary of qualified plan benefits.AutomobilesIt may not be advisable to transfer automobiles to the trust. If, however, your client has a number of automobiles, or ones of particularly high value, these should be transferred to the trust. Automobiles should not be transferred to the trust unless the client is certain that adequate liability insurance is available to cover substantial claims, as the trust may be made a party to any lawsuits following an accident involving an automobile that the trust holds as the owner. If automobiles are to be transferred to the trust, your client should contact the appropriate state licensing agency for the vehicle and seek instructions on how to effectuate the transfer of title to the trust. Further, your client should also contact the insurance carrier for the vehicle to ensure the vehicle will continue to be covered once it is placed in the trust.Lease AgreementsThe lease under which your client is the lessor should be assigned to the trust using an Assignment of Lease or similar document that effectuates the assignment. Your client should notify the lessee in writing that all future rental checks should be made payable directly to the trust, not to your client individually.Cemetery PlotsYou or the client should transfer their cemetery plot to the trust. This is typically done through executing an Assignment of Burial Plots & Mausoleum or similar document that effectuates the transfer. The director of the cemetery should receive a copy of this Assignment. Perhaps the organization that sold the client the cemetery plot also has assignment forms that they could provide.Safe Deposit BoxYour client can transfer their safe deposit box by assigning the rights under the existing rental agreement to the trust or by having the trust itself enter into a new rental agreement with the bank. It is also a good idea for your client to give the successor trustee authority to access the box so that if your client should become incapacitated or even deceased, the successor trustee can get into the box.U.S. Savings BondsThe federal government requires the use of Form PD 1851 for transferring U.S. Savings Bonds. The back of the form has instructions. After you or the client have filled out the form, the client's signature must be "guaranteed" (medallion stamped) by an officer at their bank and forwarded along with the bonds to the following. For EE or I savings bonds: Treasury Retail Securities Services, PO Box 214, Minneapolis, MN 55480-0214; for HH savings bonds: Treasury Retail Securities Services, PO Box 2186, Minneapolis, MN 55480-2186.Other Personal PropertyProperty such as airplanes, yachts, horses, artwork, copyrights, patents, or royalty contracts, a family business, stock options, mobile homes, partnership interests, and other rare types of property have special considerations that should be researched to determine if and how to transfer such property into the trust.Personal property items (household furniture and furnishings, books, pictures, jewelry, hobby equipment and collections, wearing apparel, and other articles of household or personal use or ornament, etc.) can be transferred by a general assignment with particular descriptions of valuable items and a provision to cover after-acquired items.Other Funding IssuesIn What "Name" Should Trust Assets Be Held?Generally speaking, assets will be retitled in substantially the following format:[Trustee name], Trustee under [trust name] Trust Agreement Dated [month day, year].This ensures that the appropriate trust is the owner of the property. Not using all these identification markers can lead to ambiguities. There could be two or more trusts with the same name, or on the same date, but it is very unlikely that two trusts exist with the same trustee, name, and date of signing. It presents no problems if a bank or any other financial institution wants to hold an asset in a slightly different name. The important thing is to be sure that the trust is not confused with any other trust and that the assets are clearly held in the trust.Income Tax ReturnsAt the time the trust is established, your client should inform their accountant.As long as the grantor serves as the trustee (which is usually the case), they will use their social security number as the number for the trust. Your client will file the same income tax return they have always filed. If a successor trustee takes over, a tax identification number must be obtained so that the trust can file an income tax return. The trust will, in effect, file a return with the same information and pay no more taxes than your client pays now, but the information will be reported on an IRS form designed for trustees and fiduciaries.The successor trustee will use IRS Form SS-4 to obtain the new tax identification number. When the number is received, it should be communicated to the client's accountant or tax preparer, as it will be used on the tax return to be signed by the trustee.RecordkeepingNo extraordinary recordkeeping is necessary beyond that which your client would ordinarily maintain. It is important, however, that the client keep records of trust property separate from property that may not be in the trust. Once changes in ownership to the trust are made, your client will receive monthly and other account statements in the name of the trust. Information your client receives from their stockbroker or other sources of financial information should also be adequate.Preparation of Schedule AYou or your client should prepare a Schedule A listing all assets transferred to the trust. Examples of these listings are:• 100 shares, P, G & E, common• Real property at 0000 High Street, Palo Alto, California –and–• Limited partnership interest in XXX Fund 1 unitFuture Changes in Trust AssetsIf the trust should subsequently dispose of a listed asset, it may be removed from the schedule by keeping a separate schedule and indicating its disposition and the date it was disposed of. Schedule A as originally prepared need not be changed, as it only indicates assets as of a particular day. As assets are removed, your client should simply keep a record of the change. Likewise, if a new asset is acquired by the trust, it may be added to a new schedule.The client should review the schedule of trust assets each year when income tax returns are prepared. Delete what has been sold. Add what has been acquired and confirm that it is held in the name of the trust.Future Trust AdministrationThe focus at the time the trust is created is funding the trust and administering it during your client's lifetime. At death, certain trust administrative procedures are required, and consultation by the successor trustee with an attorney will likely be necessary. If the trust has been properly funded, the cost of trust administration will be significantly less than probate. Furthermore, if the trust contains provisions for an A/B trust, significant tax savings and other benefits are likely. You may also wish to suggest that your client create a list of their professional advisers (e.g., you, their financial planner, their CPA/tax preparer, etc.), as well as the institutions holding the grantor's assets—along with contact information for each—and keep it with their trust records. This can be of great benefit to successor trustees who may not be familiar with the grantor's assets and who to contact about those assets.Current as of: 01/10/2025

Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (MI)
Go to: What Is a Trust?   |   Characteristics of a TrustMaintainedby Dustin S. Foster and Sara A. SchimkeThis practice note discusses characteristics of trusts in Michigan. It provides a definition of the term and identifies certain distinctions in terminology used, as well as types of trusts and certain characteristics of trusts. It addresses the types of trusts and certain characteristics of trusts, including bifurcation of property ownership, trustee’s powers and duties, trust interpretation, and revocable versus irrevocable trusts.For more information on revoking, amending, or termination a trust, see Revocation, Amendment, and Termination of Trusts (MI).What Is a Trust?Effective April 1, 2010, Article VII of Estates and Protected Individuals Code (EPIC) became the Michigan Trust Code (MTC). With the exception of a few sections, the MTC applies to all trusts created either before or after the effective date. The code provides a new level of consistency when it comes to the creation, administration, and termination of trusts in Michigan. The following is a discussion and analysis of the major portions of the MTC.While the MTC is new, the definition of a trust has not changed.“Trust” includes, but is not limited to, an express trust, private or charitable, with additions to the trust, wherever and however created …. Trust does not include a constructive trust or a resulting trust, conservatorship, personal representative, custodial arrangement under the Michigan uniform transfers to minors act, 1998 PA 433, MCL 554.521 to 554.552, business trust providing for a certificate to be issued to a beneficiary, Yu trust fund, voting trust, security arrangement, liquidation trust, or trust for the primary purpose of paying debts, dividends, interest, … or another arrangement under which a person is a nominee or escrowee for another.MCL 700.1107(n).The MTC has added a few new terms to the trust vocabulary in Michigan. Namely, the MTC now defines charitable trust (MCL 700.7103 (c)), discretionary trust (MCL 700.7103(d)), interests of the beneficiaries (MCL 700.7103(e)), qualified trust beneficiary (MCL 700.7103 (g)), revocable (MCL 700.7103 (h)), support provision (MCL 700.7103 (k)), and trust director (MCL 700.7103(m) and MCL 700.7703a). Almost all other terms dealing with trust can be found at the beginning of EPIC, MCL 700.1103 through MCL 700.1108.Bifurcation of Property OwnershipMost trusts created for estate planning purposes are express trusts governed by written declarations of trust (or, in the case of a testamentary trust, the terms of the trust are contained in the terms of the testator’s will); however, the MTC recognizes oral trusts under MCL 700.7407. The fundamental principles of a trust remain the same: when a trust is created, the settlor splits legal and equitable title between a trustee and a beneficiary respectively. See MCL 700.7401(1). Without a split in the title, there is no trust. The MTC has codified common law with regard to the requirements for the creation of a valid trust: the settlor must have capacity to create the trust (MCL 700.7402(1)(a)), intent to create a trust (MCL 700.7402(1)(b)), and an ascertainable beneficiary (MCL 700.7402(1)(c) and (2)); the trustee must have duties to perform (MCL 700.7402(1)(d)); and the sole beneficiary cannot be the sole trustee (MCL 700.7402). Contrary to common law, the trust does not require the transfer of res or trust property to the trustee concurrently with the signing of the trust instrument to be valid (see MCL 700.7401(2)). Furthermore, the MTC recognizes the existence of oral trusts established by “clear and convincing evidence.” See MCL 700.7407.The requisite mental capacity to create a trust is the same as that to create a valid will (MCL 700.7601). Therefore, in order to create a valid trust, the settlor must understand that the trust will distribute property after his or her death (MCL 700.2501(2)(a)), understand the “nature and extent” of his or her property (MCL 700.2501(2)(b)), know the “natural objects of his or her bounty” (MCL 700.2501(2)(c)), and have “the ability to understand in a reasonable matter the general nature and effect of his or her … signing the [trust]” (MCL 700.2501(2)(d)). The lack of mental capacity does not preclude the creation of a trust so long as a properly appointed agent is given the power to create a trust on behalf of an incapacitated settlor. See Reporter’s Comment to MCL 700.7402. All trusts created after April 1, 2010, are presumed to be revocable unless stated otherwise by the settlor. MCL 700.7602(1).Trust and Trust AgreementTrusteesTrustees’ powers and duties are detailed in Part 7 of the MTC. As with most sections in EPIC and the MTC, the rules set forth for the trustee are default provisions which can be modified by the declaration of trust with the exception of the following: the trust document cannot modify a trustee’s duty of loyalty and care as defined in Section?7801 (MCL 700.7105(2)(b)), the court still has the power to determine the necessity of bond (MCL 700.7105(2)(f)) and determine the reasonableness of the trustee’s compensation and make required adjustments (MCL 700.7105(2)(g)), the trustee is still required to “provide beneficiaries with the terms of the trust” and other necessary information as stated in Section?7814 (MCL 700.7105(2)(i)), and the trust document cannot eliminate the power of a court to order trustee accountings (MCL 700.7105(2)(j)).A cotrustee, qualified beneficiary, or the settlor may petition for the removal of a trustee (MCL 700.7706(1)). And the court may remove a trustee for various acts or failure to act, including a “lack of cooperation” between cotrustees (MCL 700.7706(2)(b)), “persistent failure” to perform his duties (MCL 700.7706(2)(c)), or a “substantial change of circumstances” (MCL 700.7706(2)(d)). A trustee is entitled to reasonable compensation (MCL 700.7708(1)), and reimbursement of expenses “properly incurred in the administration of the trust” (MCL 700.7709(1)(a)).The trustee must “administer the trust in good faith … in accordance with its terms … for the benefit of the trust beneficiaries.” See MCL 700.7801. The trustee’s duty of loyalty is set forth in MCL 700.7802 which codifies the traditional sense of a fiduciary’s duty of loyalty: the trustee must administer the trust for the benefit of the trust beneficiaries and no one else (MCL 700.7802(1)). With regard to the duty of care that must be followed by a trustee, the MTC refers to the Michigan prudent investor rule and follows a prudent person standard. Furthermore, the code states that if a trustee has special skills and was named trustee “on the basis of representation of [those] skills,” the trustee is expected to use those special skills (MCL 700.7803). Therefore, if your client names her nephew to act as her trustee because her nephew is an attorney, the nephew is expected to exercise a level of care normally associated with an attorney.Under MCL 700.7401(2), until property or an interest in property is transferred to the trustee or made subject to the terms of the trust, the person nominated as trustee has no fiduciary or other obligations under the instrument establishing the terms of the trust except as may have been specifically agreed by the settlor and the nominated trustee.Effective March 29, 2019, Michigan adopted the Michigan’s directed trust and divided trustee laws. Codified at MCL 700.7703a and MCL 700.7703b, the laws are based on the Uniform Directed Trust Act.Administration of the trust generally rests in the settlor and/or trustee, or another person delegated by the trustee. In 2019, Michigan law adopted the concept of the role of the trust director, which is someone other than the settlor or trustee who has the power to make decisions regarding some part of the administration of the trust (MCL 700.7703a). A trust director must be either a natural person or an organization permitted to exercise trust powers. See MCL 700.7703a(23)(f). The extent to which a trust director can determine the administration of the trust is set forth by the settlor. The settlor and drafter must carefully consider the powers and responsibilities given to the trust director as this individual is a fiduciary and could face liability if his or her role and responsibilities are not carefully described. Even if the settlor does not use the title trust director in his or her declaration of trust, a court may in fact determine that an individual is a trust director by virtue of his or her responsibilities to the trust.An unpublished 2023 decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals presents an interesting situation involving the administration of a trust by a future trustee and beneficiary while the settlor/trustee was still alive. In In re Esther Kratzer Revocable Trust & In re Wendell Kratzer Revocable Trust, Michigan Court of Appeals, Dkt. Nos. 357860, 357861 (Mar 23, 2023) (unpublished), the settlor and trustee of a trust and successor trustee of his wife’s trust. The purpose of the trusts was the administration of the family farm and farm-related assets. The trustee nominated one of his four children to become the trustee of the trusts after his death. During the father’s lifetime, the son who was to become the trustee on the father’s death and who lived on the farm with his wife, made noticeable improvements to the farm property during his father’s lifetime. The father was in an assisted-living facility, but was not mentally incapacitated. Although the son made the decisions, all decisions were made in consultation with the father.In the original proceedings, the probate court held that the son and his wife were the only beneficiaries of the improvements that were made with the trust funds, and that he was required to pay the trust back for the cost of the improvements. Reversing the decision of the probate court, the appeals court held that because the father, who was mentally capable, was still the trustee when the improvements were made and because the father consented to the improvements, the improvements that were made were not a breach of the son’s fiduciary duty. The court found that the son owed these duties only to the father, and that, because the father had created the revocable trust, he could override the terms of the trust and give the son permission to make the improvements.The above case is, however, a cautionary tale as to the administration of a trust by someone other than the trustee. Although the son was going to become the trustee on his father’s death, he was, during the father’s lifetime, not able to act on his own. The appeals court based its decision largely on the competency of the settlor/trustee and the action of the son as his agent. The appeals court found that the son, as agent of his father, owed a fiduciary duty to his father, and not to the other beneficiaries.Interpretation of TrustsWith regard to interpretation of specific trust provisions, the MTC has again codified long-held common-law principals and added a few clarifications. This section will review some notable provisions in the area of trust interpretation.Spendthrift, support, and discretionary trusts receive essentially the same treatment under the code as they did in common law. Spendthrift provisions are valid and enforceable and they prevent “both voluntary and involuntary transfer of the trust beneficiary’s interest.” See MCL 700.7502(1) and (2). Support language will protect trust assets from creditors until it is distributed to the beneficiary. Once income or principal is distributed to a beneficiary, the beneficiary’s creditors may only attach to those amounts “not necessary for the health, education, support, or maintenance of the trust beneficiary.” Discretionary trusts are still the strongest protection against a beneficiary’s creditors in that no amount is available to creditors until the income or principal is distributed to the beneficiary (MCL 700.7505). In the event the settlor has created a hybrid trust such that the trustee has discretion to distribute income or principal for the beneficiary’s support, the trust will be treated as a discretionary trust and not a support trust (MCL 700.7103(k)).The MTC codifies the common-law treatment of the three special creditors: alimony and child support, support services creditors, and the state of Michigan or the United States. MCL 700.7504 specifically exempts these three categories of creditors when it comes to the application of spendthrift and support provisions in a trust. Therefore, a creditor of the beneficiary falling into one of the three mentioned categories can attach to the beneficiary’s interest in a trust, even though it is protected by a spendthrift or support provision, without having to wait for the income or principal to be distributed directly to the beneficiary.The rules of construction as applied to accessions, the presumption of non-ademption by extinction, ademption by satisfaction, and the presumption of non-exoneration are all applied to trusts in the same manner as they are applied to wills. MCL 700.2605 et seq. In terrorem clauses in trusts now have the same treatment as with wills. Therefore, a penalty clause in a trust will not be given effect if there is probable cause to bring the suit. MCL 700.7113.The doctrine of Cy pres is now codified in MCL 700.7413. Therefore, if a charitable trust becomes illegal or impossible to achieve and the court finds that the settlor had a general charitable intent, the court can modify the terms of the trust “in a manner consistent with the settlor’s general charitable intent.” MCL 700.7413. This version is entirely consistent with established common law. For more on this, see LaFond v City of Detroit, 357 Mich 362, 98 NW2d 530 (1959); In re Estate of Rood, 41 Mich App 405, 200 NW2d 728 (1972); Central United Methodist Church v Estes-Palmer Found (In re Estes Estate), 207 Mich App 194, 523 NW2d 863 (1994).Characteristics of a TrustRevocable or IrrevocableA revocable living trust arrangement offers several advantages during life and after death that are not available under a will alone:• During life, assets held under a revocable living trust agreement may be used for the benefit of an incapacitated settlor or his or her dependents. Assets may be deposited to the trust pursuant to the terms of a durable power of attorney or pursuant to court order. Thus, an ongoing, court-supervised conservatorship may be avoided.• After death, assets held in trust, payable to a trust, or “pouring over” to a trust pursuant to a will can be administered and distributed promptly, privately, and efficiently. To the extent, assets are held in trust or are payable directly to the trust at the settlor’s death, they are not subject to probate in Michigan or, generally, in other jurisdictions where they are situated. For example, insurance payable to the trustees of a living trust usually can be collected quickly upon presentation of a copy of the death certificate, the policy, and the trust agreement, and upon the signature of the trustee. If the same insurance were payable to a personal representative of a probate estate or to a testamentary trustee, before payment, the insurance company would require certified copies of letters of authority as evidence of a court’s appointment of the fiduciary.
