If the insurance were payable to a decedent’s estate, it would be subject to a probate court inventory fee; if payable to the trustee of a living trust, the probate fee is avoided. The trustee of a living trust, by contrast, generally may administer a trust without court supervision unless the trustee or a beneficiary requests probate court review. Indeed, a living trust arrangement may thwart court proceedings, such as a will contest or a spouse’s statutory election against a will.A revocable living trust can be explained to clients as an alternative means of holding title to property. Clients are familiar with holding assets in sole name or jointly with someone else. Assets titled in the name of a trustee are held according to the terms of a trust agreement which the client writes. If a client establishes a trust as settlor and trustee, the client retains full control over the trust terms and over any assets in the trust. If a client wishes to designate a third-party trustee or cotrustee, the client can have the benefit of the third party’s advice about the management of trust assets but can still retain control over trust terms and the trustee through powers of amendment, withdrawal, or removal of the third-party trustee.When should a trust be funded? A revocable living trust may be funded (1) during the Settlor’s lifetime, (2) by will, or (3) by other disposition taking effect upon the Settlor’s death. See MCL 700.7401(1)(a). Under Michigan law, an unfunded living trust agreement may be funded at death by assets pouring over from a decedent’s estate. MCL 700.2511. There should be no need to transfer funds to a trust when a trust agreement is signed unless the settlor derives some psychological comfort from establishing a trust “corpus” or unless a contest about settlor’s intent may be expected. The settlor may, however, wish to fund a trust during life to obtain professional investment assistance, to prepare for ease of administration if disability occurs, or to give future beneficiaries the comfort of knowing the arrangement for them is in place. Funding during life avoids the need to have assets probated even if settlor has a pour over will. If the assets are in the settlor’s name alone at his or her death, the property must go through the probate process to transfer title to the trustee.A living trust arrangement should not be oversold. A revocable trust does not afford protection against the settlor’s creditors, and trust assets are subject to federal estate tax.Current as of: 09/13/2024End of Document

Letter to Client 
Dear [name]:I am writing regarding the [name of beneficiary] Special Needs Trust Agreement dated [month] [day], [year], which we have prepared for you as [settlor of the trust/trustee of the trust/both settlor and trustee of the trust].The purpose of this letter is to (1) describe the process of transferring assets into the trust, and (2) address what you, as [settlor/trustee/both settlor and trustee] of the trust, need to know and think about while administering the trust. Therefore, I advise you to keep this letter for future reference.To accomplish the long-term goals for which the trust was created, the settlor and trustee must adhere to the terms of the trust agreement and the tax laws relating to trusts. If you have any questions about the tax laws relating to this trust, please do not hesitate to ask me or a tax professional.The trust describes the responsibilities and the powers of the trustee(s). Since all trust documents (also called trust instruments) do not contain the same powers, the trustee must pay special attention to the language in the trust to be certain of the trustee's authority and options.This trust is a third-party special needs trust. It is designed so that the family and friends of [name of beneficiary], the beneficiary of the trust, can make gifts to benefit [name of beneficiary] while preserving the right to receive government benefits. There can be only one third-party special needs trust per beneficiary. It will be necessary that all friends and family wishing to make gifts to the beneficiary have the relevant information for this trust. The gifts made to the trust can take many forms: cash, bonds, shares of stock, real property, or even corporeal moveable property. Under no circumstances should any property or assets belonging to the beneficiary be transferred into the trust. If this is done, it could jeopardize the beneficiary's eligibility for government benefits. Also, the beneficiary has no right to demand or withdraw any assets or property from the trust.Irrevocability of Trust. The trust is an irrevocable trust. That means it is fixed and cannot be amended or terminated, except under very limited circumstances. Also, gifts made to the trust cannot be returned. Please call me if you need to make any changes to the trust.Federal Tax Identification Number. Whether or not the trust needs its own federal tax identification number (TIN) depends on whether the trust is a grantor trust or not. A grantor trust is a trust over which the grantor or other owner retains the power to control or direct the trust's income or assets. If the trust is a grantor trust, the trustee has the choice of reporting the annual income for federal tax purposes on either IRS Form 1041 or on the income tax return of the grantor. A grantor trust does not need its own TIN. If the trust is not a grantor trust, either the trustee or the attorney for the trust must obtain a TIN for the trust by filing IRS Form SS4. The TIN will be used to identify the trust in bank accounts and tax returns.Funding the Trust. The ownership of one or more assets must be transferred to the trustee for the trust to be in effect. The bank or brokerage account or the asset itself, such as shares of stock, should be titled in the name of the trust, as follows: "[name of trustee], Trustee of The [name of beneficiary] Special Needs Trust dated [month] [day], [year]." (Your bank may want to use a slightly different name.) If the trust is not a grantor trust, always use the trust's TIN as the identification number for bank accounts.Your stockbroker may request a copy of the signed trust instrument for examination by the firm's legal department. We generally do not provide a copy of the entire agreement. A copy of the Certification of Trust/Extract of Trustor the signature page and the trustee powers will demonstrate that the trust has been properly executed and the trustee has the necessary power to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds, and options, trade on margin, and conduct other trust business.To transfer real estate into the trust, a deed must be executed and recorded in the parish in which the real estate is situated granting the property to the trust. If there is more than one piece of real estate being transferred, it will be necessary to record the deed and Extract or Certification of Trust in each parish where the real estate is situated.Gift Tax Return. If any gift to the trust exceeds $19,000 in value, the donor should be instructed to complete a gift tax return (IRS Form 709) by April 15 of the following year. Please consult me concerning allocating some or all of your generation-skipping tax exemption to this trust.Taxation of Trust Income. The trustee is required to file IRS Form 1041 and Louisiana Form IT-541 to report the income for any year in which the trust has taxable income. These forms are due on April 15 of the year following the taxable year. Although tax software is widely available for this purpose, we strongly recommend having these forms prepared by an accountant because of their complexity.Distributions from the Trust. The types of distributions that can be made to the disabled beneficiary are limited to those which will not jeopardize the benefits received from any county, state, federal, or other government agency. In general, distributions should not be made for basic support (e.g., housing, food, clothing, and medical treatment for which public or Medicaid funds are available). Here are some examples of permissible distributions: automobile, computer equipment, subscriptions, travel, vacations, furniture, education (tuition, books, supplies), health insurance, life insurance, sports equipment, tickets to events, medical equipment not provided by public or Medicaid funds, equipment, devices, software that assist the beneficiary with managing their disability and day to day tasks (tablet with speech software, vehicles equipped with ramps or lifts to assist entering and exiting the vehicle, etc.). Cash or cash equivalents should not be distributed directly to the beneficiary.If you have any questions about permissible distributions, please do not hesitate to contact me.Termination of the Trust. The trust will terminate if any of the following events occur:• There are no more assets in the trust.• The disabled beneficiary dies, and the assets of the trust are distributed to the beneficiary's descendants or to the other contingent beneficiaries named in Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust.• The beneficiary has become ineligible for public benefits, per Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust.Trustee Powers. A detailed list of the trustee's powers can be found in Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust. Please read them and make sure you understand them. I will be happy to answer any questions you may have about them.It is important that the trustee keep records of trust property separate from property that may not be in the trust. The settlor and the trustee will receive monthly and other account statements in the name of the trust.I hope this letter answered your immediate questions. If you would like further explanation of any aspect of administering the trust, please do not hesitate to contact me.
Sincerely, ____________________[name of attorney]End of Document

Requirements and Restrictions on Trust Purposes and Administration (TX)
Go to: Formal Requirements   |   Restrictions on Trust Purposes   |   Trustees and Trust Administration   |   Related ContentMaintainedMichael Sneeringer; Anna E. Els; maintained by Pamela C. LyleThis practice note discusses the formal requirements for creating a valid trust in Texas, including intent, identifiable property, and ascertainable beneficiaries. It also covers restrictions on trust purposes, such as illegality and provisions in contravention of public policy. Finally, the note addresses the appointment and succession of trustees, as well as trustee duties, responsibilities, and liability. Settlors often create trusts to avoid a lengthy probate process and to ensure protection of assets from creditors. In order to carry out the intentions of the settlor, estate planners must understand the permissible purposes for which a trust may be established, and the attendant requirements for doing so.For a full listing of key content related to estate plans for individuals in Texas, see Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (TX) and Estate Plan for Single Individual Resource Kit (TX).For more information on creating a trust in Texas, see 4 Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 80.04.For a list of relevant statutes on trust creation in Texas, see 9 Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 48.02.For further discussion of trusts, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (TX) and Revocation, Amendment, and Termination of Trusts (TX).Formal RequirementsTrusts created in Texas must comport with specific requirements to be valid. Under the Texas Trust Code, intent, identifiable property, and ascertainable beneficiaries all must be present in order for a trust to come into existence.IntentIt is axiomatic that a settlor cannot create an express trust without the intent to do so. Tex. Prop. Code § 112.002. See also Texas Estate Planning § 32.02[2]. Issues of intent generally boil down to one of the following:• Whether the settlor lacked capacity to enter into a trust• Whether the creation of the trust was induced by fraud, duress, or undue influence• Whether the trust was created by mistakeThe capacity required of the settlor is the same as the capacity required to make a will. See Tex. Prop. Code § 112.007. See also Texas Estate Planning § 32.02[4] and Texas Estate Planning § 20:42. If the settlor lacks capacity to convey property, then no trust arises. See also Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 45.04 ; see also Tex Jur Duress and Undue Influence § 16. Be advised, however, that there are very few cases in which trusts have been contested successfully in Texas.In Texas, a trust is enforceable only if there is written evidence of the trust's terms bearing the signature of the settlor or the settlor's authorized agent. However, if a trust consists only of personal property, it trust may be established orally or by a less formal written instrument. Tex. Prop. Code § 112.004.Identifiable PropertyAccording to common law, for a valid trust to exist, it must hold assets (i.e., there must be a trust res or property delivered to the trustee). Tex. Prop. Code § 112.005. See also 1-30 Texas Estate Planning § 30.06. Unfunded revocable trusts are increasingly common. There is some concern that a trust that is not yet valid could not be effectively named as a beneficiary under a pour-over will. However, so long as the trust instrument is validly executed and nominally funded, the trust is considered legally in existence. In re Estate of Canales, 837 S.W.2d 662 (Tex. App. 1992). In practice, the res requirement is highly unlikely to be an issue, but if a contest is anticipated, you should take steps to reduce the risk of contest by funding the trust to a degree that is more than nominal promptly after creation.Ascertainable BeneficiariesAnother old common-law rule is that to be valid a trust must have ascertainable beneficiaries. Be aware that this does not mean that a trust must have current beneficiaries. A trust is valid if there are no current beneficiaries for a period of time, and income must be accumulated for that period. However, there must be some party in a position to enforce the trust. The party enforcing the trust need not be an individual; nonnatural persons such as charities or other trusts, or in theory, other legal entities, can be beneficiaries. Additionally, animals can be the sole current beneficiaries of trusts. Tex. Prop. Code § 112.037.In contrast, there is no requirement for a trustee to be specified in a trust instrument. A trustee can always be appointed, just like a vacancy in the office of trustee can always be filled, via a court proceeding or by a mechanism provided for in the agreement.Restrictions on Trust PurposesIn general, a trust may be created for any purpose that is not illegal. Although historically, many trusts in Texas have successfully withstood trust contests, there do exist restrictions on the purpose for which a trust may be created:• Illegality. A Texas trust may be created for any purpose that is not illegal if it does not require the trustee to commit an act that is criminal, tortious, or contrary to public policy. Tex. Prop. Code § 112.031. A trust may fail if it was settled to avoid the settlor's creditors. See also Texas Estate Planning § 32.03.• Provisions contrary to public policy. While it is rare to trip over an illegality of purpose issue, it is not as uncommon to encounter provisions that are contrary to public policy. Purposes that violate public policy include those that tend to encourage criminal or tortious conduct.Trustees and Trust AdministrationThe selection of trustees and the administration of the trust are critical components of manifesting the intent of the settlor. Bifurcation of property ownership is the defining characteristic of a trust and as such, the trustee, as the individual holding title to the assets of the trust, plays an active role in its administration.TrusteesIn general, any individual, or a corporate trustee such as a bank or trust company, may serve as trustee. The selection of a trustee depends on a number of factors, including:• The settlor's preference• The time commitment that will be required of a prospective trustee• Conflicts that may exist between a potential trustee and one or more beneficiaries of the trust• The size, complexity, and potential risk of the assets of the trustAppointmentWhile the settlor may desire an independent trustee (e.g., a corporate trustee), the prospective trustee may be reluctant to serve, for example, where the trust assets consist of real estate holdings which have not been evaluated for potential risks, such as environmental hazards, and the undertaking would be cost prohibitive. More often, the size of the trust estate may not justify the fee structure of an institutional trustee. If the trust instrument nominates as trustee a bank that no longer exists, no longer accepts trust business, or refuses to administer the particular trust, and no other mechanism of filling the vacancy in the office of trustee is provided, the only solution may be to seek court appointment of a trustee. To avoid this scenario, the trust instrument should either appoint a successor trustee or provide a means for the last trustee, beneficiaries, or another third party to appoint a new trustee. See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.20.Be aware that designating a trustee does not compel that person or entity to serve as such. The trustee must voluntarily qualify by formally accepting appointment, before actually commencing to serve. The initial trustees of an inter vivos trust qualify by signing the trust instrument. Successor trustees of an inter vivos trust accept by signing the instrument under which they are appointed, or signing a separate instrument drafted for that specific purpose (an acceptance of trust). In all cases, proper documentation is essential to forestall any claims that a trustee is acting without due authority. Best practices further suggest preparing documentation for the acceptance of trusteeship under testamentary trusts, including any sub-trusts that may spring into existence under the trust instrument after the death of the settlor. See also Texas Estate Planning § 120.06.SuccessionIf a trust instrument names a successor trustee, or provides for a process of naming a successor trustee, there is generally no problem with succession. However, even when a successor is not named in the trust instrument, the Court shall appoint a successor trustee upon the petition of an interested individual.. Tex. Prop. Code § 113.083.When drafting a Texas trust, it is recommended that estate planners incorporate multiple layers of trustee succession provisions. To be thorough, the practitioner may, in sequence: 1 Empower the settlor to name successors2 Authorize the settlor's spouse to designate successors3 Nominate a series of successor trustees, in order of priority listed, to serve as trustee4 Provide that the last acting trustee may appoint a further successor5 Allow a majority of the adult beneficiaries of the trust to fill any vacancy6 Identify a corporate trusteeTexas Estate Planning § 35.04; Texas Estate Planning § 170.05. While estate planners do not typically provide for all of these fallback options, some subset of them is advisable. Identification of a corporate trustee is a last resort.Carefully consider that the unrestricted power of a settlor or beneficiary to remove and replace trustees presents a trap for the unwary, as such a power may present tax complications. See Rev. Rul. 95-58, 1995-2 C.B. 191. However, the mere power to name successors should not be problematic. If the trust should not be included in the settlor's estate for estate tax purposes, the attorney will generally expressly provide in the trust instrument that the settlor cannot under any circumstances become a trustee.Trust AdministrationEffective administration of a trust requires knowledge and understanding of the trustee's fiduciary and other duties associated with administering the trust, as well as the responsibilities and liabilities inherent in the trustee's position.Duties of a Trustee – GeneralSection 113.051 of the Texas Trust Code articulates a general duty of good faith administration in accordance with the trust terms and with the Code. Unless otherwise contrary to the provisions of the Trust Code, the trustee must perform all the duties imposed by common law.Fiduciary DutyOne of the fundamental characteristics of a trust is the trustee's fiduciary duty to the beneficiaries. In its most basic translation, a fiduciary duty means that the trustee must act in the best interests of the beneficiaries. Arguably, the most famous statement on fiduciary duty in American jurisprudence comes from a New York Court of Appeals case, Meinhard v. Salmon, 249 N.Y. 458 (1928), in which Judge Cardozo eloquently stated, "A trustee is held to something stricter than the morals of the market place. Not honesty alone, but the punctilio of an honor the most sensitive, is then the standard of behavior." Meinhard, 249 N.Y. at 464.The broad concept of a trustee's fiduciary duty has been broken down into various individual duties, which will be described in more detail below. It is important for counsel to understand one point that often goes overlooked in discussions about fiduciary duties: not all fiduciaries are created equal. For example, an attorney-in-fact under a power of attorney is a fiduciary, but an attorney-in-fact has no affirmative obligation to act on behalf of the principal, whereas the fiduciary who has accepted the office of trustee has signed up for such a commitment. The fiduciary duties of attorneys-in-fact stem from the law of agency, not the law of trusts, and though this has not been written about extensively, less is expected of agents than of trustees. In particular, though this issue arises more often in other contexts than in estate planning, breaches of fiduciary duty in the agency context can generally be excused if they are properly disclosed, but in the trust context, disclosure alone will not suffice; informed written consent is required.Duty of LoyaltyOf the various individual duties owed by trustees, the most central to the fiduciary concept is the duty of loyalty. See Tex. Prop. Code § 117.007. See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.23 .The trustee is, however, entitled to be paid reasonable compensation. Tex. Prop. Code § 114.061. See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.26.A trustee is typically prohibited from engaging in self-dealing (i.e., transactions between the trustee and the trust) for the obvious reason that the trustee cannot be relied upon to seek the best result for the trust and its beneficiaries when the trustee stands on the other side of the transaction. Self-dealing falls into the general category of conflicts of interest, which a trustee is honor bound to avoid. See also Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 81.23. If, for example, a trust owns 30% of the stock in a company, and the trustee of that trust individually owns 30% of the stock, the very potential for a biased vote creates a conflict of interest. Where the trustee is permitted to vote the trust's stock, the trustee may not vote those shares in a manner that will benefit the trustee personally unless it just so happens that such vote is also in the best interests of the trust and its beneficiaries.Interestingly, the evolution of modern financial institutions has led to a softening of the prohibition on self-dealing and conflicts of interest. For example, a corporate trustee may want to invest the trust's assets in mutual funds of an affiliate of the trustee. Under traditional trust law, this would be considered prohibited self-dealing. Whether because of a recognition that it could well be in the best interests of the trust to permit such investments or lobbying from the financial services industry, such investments are now specifically authorized by statute. See Tex. Prop. Code § 113.052. However, unless the trust provides otherwise, the trustee must choose between earning commissions on such investments or its affiliate earning its standard fees; the statute does not condone double-dipping. Slay v. Burnett Tr., 143 Tex. 621, 187 S.W.2d 377 (1945). Accordingly, many corporate fiduciaries will insist on inclusion of trust language overriding the proscription against double-dipping and authorizing a range of other self-dealing transactions on the theory that the settlor is choosing the trustee at least in part because of the range of investments that will be made available to the trust. When drafting a trust that will name a corporate trustee, you should obtain from the trustee its preferred language early on in the drafting process in order to negotiate the self-dealing provisions as needed and ultimately incorporate them into the trust document. See also Texas Estate Planning § 170.05.Duty of CareThe next most basic individual duty owed by a trustee is the duty of care, or the responsibility to administer the trust with the care and skill of an ordinary prudent person. See 72 Tex. Jur. Trusts § 107; see also Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 47.01[3][d]. A trustee who is an accountant will be expected to know the tax law better than a trustee who is not a tax professional, and a corporate trustee will be expected to keep impeccable records whereas an individual trustee might be excused for minor lapses. You should be aware of two things regarding prudence. First, prudence does not mean perfection. If a trustee makes prudent investments that do not happen to work out well, the trustee has not breached its duty of care even though the trust may have lost money. Second, prudence is process-driven rather than a results-driven. If the decision-making process is sound, the trustee has fulfilled the duty of care, regardless of the result. See Tex. Prop. Code § 114.001(b).Still, it may be challenging to establish a sound process; therefore, you should encourage trustee clients to document their decision-making as thoroughly as possible. Detailed minutes of trustee meetings can save the trustee from liability. Non-trust lawyers sometimes have a bias against detailed minutes because they feel that any more detail than absolutely necessary gives potential litigants fodder for lawsuits. Whether or not that is true in other contexts, it is clearly incorrect in the trust context. You should be highly aware that the most important criterion for avoiding liability is the degree of attention to the decision-making process demonstrated by the trustee.Duty of ImpartialityAnother individual duty is that of impartiality: the trustee must treat all beneficiaries fairly. Fairly does not mean equally, though, as different beneficiaries will have different needs. The trustee must give due regard to the respective interests of the current beneficiaries and the remainder beneficiaries. For example, a current beneficiary may ask the trustee for a large distribution, justifying that the distribution is prudent (e.g., it is needed to pay for the beneficiary to go to law school and enhance her earning potential), but if the distribution constitutes a large percentage of the trust, it may unduly favor the current beneficiaries over the remaindermen. See also 72 Tex. Jur. Trusts § 64.A client may wish to favor all current beneficiaries over the remainder beneficiaries or one current beneficiaries over the other current beneficiaries. The estate planner may incorporate specific language in the trust instrument to denote such preference. For example:Without limiting the trustee's discretion, the trustee may consider the needs of the settlor's daughter as more important than the needs of other descendants of the settlor, and the settlor encourages the trustee to be liberal in making distributions to the settlor's daughter, even to the extent of distributing the entire trust principal.In a traditional trust that requires the trustee to distribute all of the trust income, the current beneficiaries may request that the trustee invest in assets that generate a great deal of income, whereas the remainder beneficiaries would rather the trustee invest in assets that have more potential for appreciation. Historically, trustees have addressed the need to balance these competing interests by investing in an even mix of bonds yielding income and stocks generating growth. However, modern portfolio theory deems this balanced approach inefficient and would instead spur the trustee to maximize or minimize expected return consistent with risk tolerance.Nevertheless, in a point that is widely misunderstood by beneficiaries and trustees alike, the results of asset allocation do not dictate the trustee's distribution decisions. In other words, if a hypothetical balanced portfolio of 50% bonds and 50% stocks generates 2% income, but the trust's actual investment portfolio produces only 1.5%, the trustee could justifiably use the power of adjustment to boost distributions to the current beneficiaries to 2%, but should not increase those distributions to 4%. Similarly, with respect to the trustee's duty of impartiality, it would be inappropriate for the trustee to invest primarily for income and distribute a windfall to the current beneficiaries.Duty to Keep Beneficiaries InformedIn theory, the duty to keep beneficiaries informed does not seem terribly complex. The trustee ought to send periodic statements to the beneficiaries so they are aware of the trust's assets and the transactions that are occurring in the trust's account. That way, the beneficiaries can fulfill their fundamental role of enforcing the proper administration of the trust. See also Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 81.21[4]. How does the attorney advise a client who does not want the children to know about the millions of dollars being held in trust for their benefit lest they lose all incentive to be productive members of society? The short answer is that, in Texas, there is not much that a trustee can do to shield this information from the beneficiaries because secrecy would constitute a breach of the trustee's fiduciary duty. Further, a beneficiary may demand an accounting of the trust and a trustee is obligated to provide an accounting within 90 days of receiving such a demand. See Tex. Prop. Code §§ 113.151 and 113.152.Other DutiesThere remain a few additional individual duties that arguably fall within those previously discussed yet warrant separate mention:• Duty to administer the trust. The trustee has the duty to administer the trust. Tex. Prop. Code § 113.051; First City Nat'l Bank v. Haynes, 614 S.W.2d 605 (Tex. App. 1981) (holding that a trustee breached its fiduciary duty by failing to pay taxes, failing to keep property in repair, and failing to clean property prior to its sale). See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.23[1]. While this seems obvious, it may differ from other fiduciary duty contexts, where there may not be an affirmative duty to act.• Duty to act within scope of authority. A second duty is the obligation to act within the scope of the trustee's authority. The trustee must be authorized to take an action (e.g., if the trust does not authorize distribution to a particular beneficiary until age 21, the trustee has no authority to make earlier distributions). However, circling back to the duty of care, whether a trustee can take an action is only the first stage of the analysis. Equally important is whether the trustee should take a proposed action. For example, the trust may authorize the trustee to invest the entire trust corpus in high yield bonds or a beneficiary's start-up business, but either extreme is seldom prudent. Similarly, the trustee may have the authority to distribute all of the trust assets to the beneficiary, but if the beneficiary is financially irresponsible, full distribution is likely a poor decision.Responsibilities of a TrusteeWhen administering a trust, the terms of the trust instrument, the identity of the beneficiaries, the nature of the trust assets, the law governing the trust, the tax status of the trust, and other circumstances all influence what specific tasks the trustee must undertake. Rarely will two different trust administrations be identical.There are a few basic responsibilities that will generally apply to all trustees:• Take control of trust assets. Trustees are required to take control of the trust assets. Trustees must keep the trust assets separate and avoid commingling with their own personal assets. Eaton v. Husted, 141 Tex. 349, 172 S.W.2d 493 (1943). Fortunately, trustees may employ financial firms to hold investable assets and need not maintain certificated securities in a safe, which used to be commonplace. Tex. Prop. Code § 113.007.• Safeguard trust assets. Trustees are required to safeguard trust assets. For example, trustees should exercise prudence when choosing a custodian. Trustees should also properly insure real estate or artwork owned by the trust. • Keep accurate records. Trustees are required to maintain clear, distinct, and accurate records for the trust. You should be cognizant that it is in the trustee's best interest to be an assiduous record keeper, because a beneficiary may demand the trustee to submit an accounting of the trust administration. As part of this responsibility, the trustee may need to track income and principal separately, particularly where the trust has different standards for income and principal distributions. Tex. Prop. Code § 113.151. See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 48.23 [4].• Invest trust assets. As fiduciaries, trustees have the general authority to  invest the assets of the trust. See Tex. Prop. Code § 113.006. See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 48.23 [2]. However, trustees may delegate investment discretion to professional investment managers so long as they exercise reasonable care, skill, and caution in:o Selecting the agento Establishing the scope and terms of the delegation, consistent with the purposes and terms of the trusto Reviewing the agent's actions periodically to monitor performance and compliance with the terms of the delegationTex. Prop. Code § 117.001 et seq.• Make mandated distributions and consider distribution requests. Trustees are required to make distributions that are mandated under the trust instrument. They must also consider beneficiaries' requests for discretionary distributions when the trust instrument vests the trustee with such discretion.• File tax returns. Trustees must file any tax returns that are required for the trust. See also Texas Estate Planning § 42.06. This can be a somewhat complicated undertaking. A trust may not have a separate filing obligation under federal law if it is a grantor trust, though depending upon the circumstances, the trustee of a grantor trust may have an obligation to file an informational return. In terms of state filing obligations, like individuals, trusts may have income sourced in different states and may have to file non-resident returns in those states. Moreover, because some states classify trusts as residents based on different criteria (i.e., residence of the trustee and/or residence of the beneficiaries), a trust may be a resident of multiple states. For example, a trust established by a Texas settlor with a Delaware trustee and a California beneficiary may have to file in all three states. Additionally, if there are any international aspects to the trust, a whole additional set of reporting obligations may apply. For these reasons, attorneys should advise their trustee clients to hire accountants who have experience preparing fiduciary income tax returns, not just personal income tax returns or business entity returns.• Pursue or defend claims. Trustees may be required to pursue claims on behalf of the trust (e.g., against service providers, former trustees, or even co-trustees) or defend claims against the trust. Tex. Prop. Code § 113.019.See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.22[2].Directed TrustsTexas by statute permits directed trusts, where, for example, a co-trustee (a general trustee) named in the trust instrument is vested with discretion over investments or distributions, and the role of another co-trustee (an administrative or excluded trustee) is simply to effectuate the directions it receives from the named advisors. Tex. Prop. Code § 114.0031. See also 4-84 Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 84.08[4]. So long as the excluded trustee does not participate in willful misconduct, that trustee is not responsible for a consequence resulting from compliance with the exercise of the power, and does not have a duty or an obligation to review, inquire, investigate, or make recommendations or evaluations with respect to the exercise of the power. Id.  Practitioners should be aware that Section 114.0031 of the Texas Property Code, which was amended in the 2019 Legislative session, carves out limited exceptions where trust advisors serve in a non-fiduciary capacity.  See Tex. Prop. Code § 114.0031(e).Majority DecisionsUnless otherwise specified in the trust instrument, multiple trustees who are unable to reach a unanimous decision may act by majority decision. Tex. Prop. Code § 113.085.Liability of TrusteeChapter 114 of the Texas Property Code empowers the court to remedy a breach of trust in numerous ways, including:• Compelling the trustee to perform the trustee's duties• Enjoining the trustee from committing a breach of trust• Compelling the trustee to redress a breach of trust by paying money or restoring property or by other means• Ordering a trustee to account• Appointing a special fiduciary to take possession of the trust property and administer the trust• Suspending the trustee• Removing the trustee as provided in Tex. Prop. Code § 113.082• Reducing or denying compensation to the trustee• Ordering any other appropriate reliefSee also 72 Tex Jur Trusts § 200.Generally, trustees are not personally liable for the acts of a predecessor, nor does any successor trustee have a duty to institute a proceeding or file any claim against any prior trustee, or that trustee's estate, for any of the prior trustee's actions, unless the successor trustee knew or should have known about the prior trustee's breach. Tex. Prop. Code § 114.002. See also Texas Probate, Estate and Trust Administration § 82.04[4].If the settlor is particularly concerned about reducing the trustee's liability, the trust instrument may include language exonerating a successor trustee for failing to seek redress; for example:No trustee shall be liable at any time because of, or in consequence of, any acts, omissions, or defaults with respect to the administration of the trust by any predecessor trustee, and no trustee shall have any duty to examine the accounts, records, and acts of any predecessor trustee or to institute any action or claim against any predecessor trustee to compel redress of any breach of trust or for any other reason.See also Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.24[4].Trustees will generally not be held liable for actions of a co-trustee, unless the trustee does not exercise reasonable care. Tex. Prop. Code § 114.006. See also 72 Tex. Jur. Trusts § 163. A dissenting trustee who joins in an action at the direction of the majority of the trustees and who has notified any co-trustee of the dissent in writing is not liable for the action. Tex. Prop. Code § 114.006(c).Compensation of TrusteeUnless otherwise provided in the trust instrument, Trustees are entitled to reasonable compensation for administering trusts. Silence of the trust instrument with regard to compensation will not be considered a reason to deny payment, nor will a right to compensation be implied by the naming of a trustee. In general, the intent of the settlor will govern the issue of trustee remuneration.In Texas, a court will generally get involved in trustee remuneration only in the case of a dispute over the fee. Tex. Prop. Code § 114.061. The Texas Trust Code does not provide any method for establishing trustee fees. Courts, however, have allowed that a reasonableness standard will be applied. See Texas Transaction Guide--Legal Forms § 50C.26.Related ContentResource Kits?• Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (TX)Current as of: 08/26/2024End of Document

Letter to Financial Institution 
[name of client][client address][month] [day], [year][name of financial institution][financial institution address]RE: Account Number [account number]To Whom It May Concern:[II/We] wish to change the title of the following account(s) to [my/our] revocable living trust, the [name of revocable living trust]:[Type of account (e.g., brokerage)], Account Number [account number]Please transfer title of the above listed account(s) to the following:[Name of trustee], as Trustee of the [name of revocable living trust] dated [date].Enclosed, please find a copy of the Certification of Trust for the [name of revocable living trust], which documents the establishment of this Trust and sets forth the powers for the future handling of assets by the trustee. Please continue to use the Social Security Number currently associated with the account(s) being transferred as the Tax Identification Number for the Trust. As per Treasury Regulation § 1.671-4(b), no separate Tax Identification Number is required for this type of trust.If you have any questions regarding this transfer, please contact [me/us] using the information listed below. Thank you for your assistance and prompt attention to this matter.Sincerely,__________________________[name of client][client address][client address][client phone number][client email address]End of Document

Asset Transfer Checklist (Trust) (CA)
by Nicole A. Davidson, Law Office of Nicole A. Davidson (adapted from California Wills and Trusts, John A. Hartog and Albert G. Handelman)This checklist contains California procedures for transferring specific assets to a revocable living trust (i.e., funding the trust). While not all assets are suitable for inclusion in a trust, all assets must be considered and integrated into the overall estate plan.For a full listing of key content related to estate plans for married or partnered people in California, see Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (CA).ARTICLE I. PRELIMINARY DETERMINATIONS.1.1. Identifying Assets to Be Transferred to Trust.(a) Review a list of the client's assets supplied by the client.(b) For each asset, determine whether the asset:(1) Will be transferred to the trust;(2) Will be held outside the trust; or(3) Requires a designation of beneficiary.(c) Determine whether there are any legal or contractual restrictions on transferring property to the trust, such as:(1) Buy-sell agreements affecting the disposition of partnership interests or stock in closely-held corporations.(2) Transfer restrictions on closely-held securities.(3) Limitations of disposition of property imposed by agreements between settlor and a former spouse, including:(i) Premarital agreements.(ii) Postmarital agreements.(iii) Marital settlement agreements incident to dissolution of prior marriage.(d) Determine whether any preliminary transmutations of property will be necessary prior to funding the trust.1.2. Mechanics of Transferring Assets to Trust.(a) Review the procedures for transferring each of the settlors' assets to the trust.(b) For each asset, determine whether the attorney or the settlors will be responsible for taking the steps necessary to make the transfer.(1) Will the settlors be able to understand and execute the necessary procedures?(2) Are the settlors willing to do so?(3) If the settlors want the attorney to "do all the work," do the settlors understand that this will increase the trust start-up costs?(4) What is likelihood that the settlors will fail to take the necessary steps even if they express a desire or willingness to do so?ARTICLE II. RESIDENTIAL REAL PROPERTY.2.1. Review the due-on-sale clause in the mortgage, if any. If a concern exists over the possible effect of clause, write to the mortgage lender to:(a) Obtain the lender's approval of the change of title.(b) Verify that the settlors' obligations under the existing loan provisions will remain the same.2.2. Obtain and review the deed to determine how title is held, the legal description, and the assessor's parcel number.2.3. If title is held in joint tenancy, convert the property to community property by preparing either:(a) A deed conveying the property from the parties as joint tenants to the parties as community property, or(b) A transmutation agreement converting the property from joint tenancy property to community property.2.4. Prepare the appropriate deed.(a) Determine the type of deed:(1) Grant deed; or(2) Quitclaim deed.(b) Make sure the deed conforms to the recorder's specifications.(c) Title should read as follows: [Name(s) of Trustee(s)], as Trustee(s), or their successors in interest, under [Name and Date of Trust].2.5. Have all the transferors execute the deed before a notary public.2.6. Prepare and obtain the transferee's signature on the Preliminary Change of Ownership Report.2.7. Record the deed.(a) Forward the original and one copy of the executed deed, together with the Preliminary Change of Ownership Report, and a (Documentary) Transfer Tax Affidavit – if required by the county (currently required in El Dorado, Merced, Riverside, San Bernardino, San Francisco, San Joaquin, San Mateo, and Santa Barbara) to the county recorder's office of the county where the real property is situated, and request recordation of deed.(b) Submit a check for the appropriate fee to the recorder's office.(c) Provide a stamped, self-addressed return envelope, and request that the county recorder return an endorsed recorded copy of the deed.ARTICLE III. COMMERCIAL REAL PROPERTY.ARTICLE IV. RANCH AND FARMING PROPERTY.ARTICLE V. REAL PROPERTY OUTSIDE CALIFORNIA.5.1. Determine what agency handles the recordation of deeds in state and county in which real property is situated.5.2. Contact recording agency in county where real property is located and:(a) Determine:(1) What documentation is required;(2) Amount of recording fees; and(3) Amount of time typically required to process recording request and return executed copy of deed.(b) Request that the recording agency forward all required forms to the attorney's office, or ascertain whether a California deed is acceptable.5.3. Prepare the appropriate deed.5.4. Prepare other documentation, if necessary.5.5. Have all transferors execute a deed before a notary public.5.6. Record the deed.(a) Forward the original and one copy of the executed deed, together with any other necessary documentation, to the recording agency in the county where the real property is situated, and request recordation of the deed.(b) Submit a check for the appropriate fee.(c) Provide a stamped, self-addressed return envelope, and request that the agency return an endorsed recorded copy of the deed.ARTICLE VI. TIME-SHARE INTERESTS IN UNITED STATES.6.1. Determine the type of ownership in the time-share interest:(a) Time-Share Estate; or(b) Time-Share Use.6.2. Contact the administrative offices where the settlor purchased the time-share interest to determine what documentation is required to transfer title to the client's revocable trust.6.3. Review title documentation that was recorded when the time-share interest was initially purchased (condominium conveyance for time-share unit or certificate of membership) and verify:(a) How title of ownership is held;(b) Legal description of settlor's unit;(c) Assessor's parcel number, if any; and(d) The common address.6.4. If the property in question is located in California, follow the steps specified in procedure 6.2 above to convey title to the trust.6.5. If the interest is located outside California, following the steps specified in procedure 5.5 above to convey title to the trust.ARTICLE VII. FOREIGN TIME-SHARE OWNERSHIP INTERESTS.7.1. Verify whether the settlor can record title in the country where the time-share interest is situated.7.2. Review the settlors' time-share condominium conveyance documents to determine:(a) Whether title has been recorded in the settlors' names as individuals; and (b) Whether the time-share ownership interest can be transferred to the settlors' revocable living trust.7.3. Contact the time-share development where the settlors purchased the time-share interest to determine what agency handles recordation of legal documents in the area where the property is located.7.4. Request that the agency forward any necessary legal documentation necessary, or ascertain whether a deed in the form of a United States deed will suffice.7.5. Prepare the appropriate deed, or other form of documentation.7.6. Have all transferors execute the deed before a notary public.7.7. If required, submit the pertinent pages from settlors' revocable living trust instrument, e.g.,(a) Full copy or abstract; or(b) First page; and(c) Pertinent pages pertaining to trustee powers and duties; and(d) Signature pages of settlors and trustee; and(e) Page containing all-purpose notary acknowledgment.7.8. Forward all required documentation to the appropriate agency for recordation, together with any required fee.7.9. If the proposed transfer of interest is prohibited by deed:(a) Prepare form for assignment of foreign time-share interest outside United States.(b) Have all transferors execute the assignment before a notary public.(c) Instruct the settlors to place the executed assignment with original trust instrument.ARTICLE VIII. OIL AND GAS INTERESTS.8.1. Review the settlors' document to determine whether the investment is registered in the company's records and/or recorded in the settlors' name.8.2. Ascertain from the managing company whether they can provide the settlors with a blank mineral deed and/or assignment form for completion, or whether the managing company will accept an assignment prepared by the attorney for the settlors.8.3. Prepare either:(a) Individual grant deed;(b) Mineral deed;(c) Oil and gas lease;(d) Assignment of overriding royalty;(e) Assignment of interest in oil and gas lease;(f) Assignment of oil and gas lease; or(g) Ratification of oil & gas lease.8.4. Have all transferors execute the deed, lease or assignment before a notary public.8.5. Contact the county recorder or county clerk in the state and county where the oil and gas interest is located to determine the fee for recordation.8.6. Inquire whether it is necessary to prepare and submit a preliminary change of ownership report to the county recorder or county clerk.8.7. Send the original of the appropriate deed, lease, or assignment to the county recorder or to the county clerk for recordation with the required fee and any other documentation:(a) Provide a stamped self-addressed envelope for the recorder or clerk to return an endorsed, recorded copy of the deed, lease or assignment to the attorney pending return of the original deed, lease, or assignment.(b) Provide a second stamped self-addressed return envelope and request that the county recorder or clerk return an original deed, lease, or assignment when the recordation process has been completed.8.8. Send a copy of the endorsed recorded mineral deed to the managing company.8.9. Obtain an amended division order by instructing the paying company to prepare and return an amended division order.8.10. Provide the settlors' social security numbers and have the settlors sign an amended division order as trustees.8.11. Return the amended division order to the managing company with a letter instructing the company to change its records to reflect ownership by the settlors' revocable living trust.8.12. Instruct the managing company to:(a) Initiate pay status to the settlors' revocable trust for royalty payments; and(b) Forward the royalty payments to the settlors as trustees.ARTICLE IX. FEDERALLY REGISTERED SECURITIES.9.1. Determine whether to:(a) Retain a stockbroker's services [see item 9.2, below]; or(b) Deal directly with the transfer agent [see item 9.3, below].9.2. If a stockbroker is engaged in connection with the transfer:(a) Arrange for the settlors to meet the stockbroker and complete a new account application to set up a trust account.(b) Instruct the settlors that the stockbroker will require the following documents:(1) Settlors' original stock certificates;(2) Either:(i) Stock Assignment Separate From Certificate, or(ii) Irrevocable Stock and/or Bond Power;(3) Complete copy of trust instrument, or pertinent pages (face page, trustee powers, signature and notary pages) and/or Trustee's Certification of Trust(4) Letter of instructions signed by the settlors authorizing the stockbroker to reissue new a share certificate in trust name.(c) Have the settlors deliver the original certificates, Stock Assignment Separate From Certificate and/or Irrevocable Stock and/or Bond Powers, and letter of instructions to the stockbroker.(d) Instruct the stockbroker to:(1) Send the above documentation to the transfer agent for reissue; and(2) Provide the transfer agent with an address to which the new certificates should be returned.9.3. If transfer will be made through the transfer agent directly:(a) Determine who the transfer agent is for a particular stock.(b) Locate and obtain the settlors' original stock certificates.(c) Prepare either:(1) Stock Assignment Separate From Certificate; or(2) Irrevocable Stock and/or Bond Power.(d) Provide a complete copy of the trust instrument, or pertinent pages (face page, trustee powers, signature and notary pages) and/or Trustee's Certification of Trust.(e) Prepare letter of instructions, signed by the settlors, authorizing the transfer agent to reissue new share certificate in the trust's name.(f) Mail the original stock certificates, stock power, attorney certification, and letter of instructions to the transfer agent.(1) Use registered or certified mail with return receipt requested.(2) Instruct the transfer agent to return the reissued certificates.(3) Provide a return address.ARTICLE X. RESTRICTED SECURITIES.10.1. Determine the identity of the transfer agent for stock.10.2. Assist the settlors in locating the original stock certificates.10.3. Prepare an Irrevocable Stock and/or Bond Powers for each class of stock.10.4. Provide a complete copy of the trust instrument, or pertinent pages (face page, trustee powers, signature and notary pages).10.5. Prepare a letter of instructions, signed by the settlors, authorizing the transfer agent to reissue new share certificates in the trust's name.10.6. Mail the original stock certificates, stock power, attorney certification, and letter of instructions to the transfer agent.(a) Use registered or certified mail with return receipt requested.(b) Instruct the transfer agent to return the reissued certificates.(c) Provide a return address.(d) Mark calendar to anticipate the receipt of reissued certificate within a month to six weeks.10.7. Upon the receipt of reissued certificates, examine the certificates to verify that the legend lists exactly the same restrictions that appeared on the original certificates.ARTICLE XI. INCENTIVE STOCK OPTIONS.11.1. Contact the employer that issued the stock option plan to determine if the company can provide the settlor with an assignment form.11.2. Alternatively, or if an assignment form is unavailable, prepare an assignment of the right to exercise the stock option plan after the death of the settlor.11.3. Have the settlors execute the assignment before a notary public.11.4. Place the executed assignment with the client's original estate planning documents.ARTICLE XII. CLOSELY HELD BUSINESS INTERESTS.12.1. Refer to and review all buy-sell agreements and by-laws to determine whether the settlor who owns the interest is prohibited from transferring the interest to a revocable living trust.12.2. Determine whether, after the transfer of the interest to the trust, the individual or corporation nominated as successor trustee of the trust will agree, and have the expertise, to continue the business in the event that the settlor with the business expertise dies or becomes incapacitated.12.3. Consider whether it will be necessary or desirable to appoint a "special trustee" who has expertise to continue the business.12.4. Determine whether the trustee will serve as a director, officer, and/or employee.12.5. Determine how the trustee's compensation will be arranged.12.6. Determine whether E&O (errors & omissions) insurance coverage will be provided for the trustee.12.7. Cancel the certificates for previously issued shares and place the cancelled certificates in the appropriate section of the corporate minute book of business.12.8. Reissue new certificates for the number of shares previously issued, in the name of the revocable living trust.12.9. Indicate the cancellation and reissue of shares in the corporate ledger kept in the corporate minute book.12.10. Store the reissued certificates in safekeeping or advise the settlors to place the certificates in their safe deposit box.ARTICLE XIII. PARTNERSHIP INTERESTS.13.1. Review the partnership agreement to ascertain whether the settlor partner is a general or a limited partner.13.2. For a limited partnership interest, review I.R.S. Form 1040, Schedule K-1, Beneficiary's Share, of Income, Deductions, Credits, etc., to determine the identity of the general partner.13.3. Review the partnership agreement to determine whether transfer to a revocable living trust is an authorized assignment.13.4. If a settlor is a member of professional partnership (e.g., law or medical partnership) in which each partner is a licensed member of profession, the settlor's interest cannot be transferred to a revocable living trust unless certain conditions are satisfied. The practitioner must be familiar with these conditions and advise the settlors how to proceed.13.5. Review the partnership agreement to determine who must consent to the proposed transfer.13.6. If a review of the partnership agreement reveals that an amendment is necessary before the settlor's interest can be transferred to the revocable living trust:(a) Contact the general partners and ascertain whether they will accept an amendment prepared by the attorney on behalf of the settlor.(b) If so, prepare an amendment of limited partnership agreement and assignment, assumption and consent.(c) Have the settlor who owns the partnership interest execute the document before a notary public.(d) Forward the amendment to the general partner for review and signature.(e) Request that the general partner return an executed copy of the amendment to the attorney's office.13.7. If transfer to a revocable living trust is not prohibited (and no amendment of partnership agreement is required):(a) Obtain and forward the settlor's original certificate of ownership to the general partners.(b) Provide the social security number of the settlor.(c) Prepare an assignment of partnership interest with consent.(d) Have the settlor who owns the partnership interest execute the document before a notary public.(e) Submit the pertinent articles of trust agreement, including the pages that identify the trust name, trustee powers, signatures, and notary public signature.(f) Submit the transfer fee, if required.(g) Request that the general partners:(1) Review and sign consent.(2) Return the assignment of interest form along with their signed consent.(3) Reissue the certificate of limited partnership interest to the revocable living trust.(h) Forward the reissued certificate of ownership to the settlors for safekeeping, or determine if the settlors want the attorney to retain the original assignments and consent for safekeeping.13.8. Advise the general partner to file an amendment to the certificate of limited partnership with the Secretary of State if the transfer to the trust (or any other event) results in any of the following:(a) Change in the name of the limited partnership.(b) Change in the street address of the principal executive office.(c) Change in address of a general partner or change in address of an agent for service of process.(d) Admission of a general partner.(e) Discovery by any of general partners of any faults or erroneous material statement contained in the certificate or any amendment to the certificate.ARTICLE XIV. SOLE PROPRIETORSHIPS.14.1. Consider whether the business will continue after the death of the proprietor, and if so, whether the trustee has sufficient expertise to continue the business.14.2. Review the assets and liabilities of the business to determine whether the assets are sufficient to cover any known or contingent liabilities of the business.14.3. If the sole proprietorship is transferred to the to trust, prepare and have the settlors execute an assignment of sole proprietorship.14.4. Consider using a fictitious business name for the business after the assets and liabilities have been transferred to the trust to facilitate lifetime operations of the business, under the proprietor's individual name or a name sufficient to identify the business as a separate and distinct asset of the trust.ARTICLE XV. INTERESTS IN PROFESSIONAL PRACTICE.15.1. Determine whether transfer of the interest to a revocable living trust is permissible.15.2. Cancel the certificates for previously issued shares in the professional corporation and place the cancelled certificates under the appropriate section in the corporation's corporate minute book.15.3. Reissue new certificates for the number of shares previously issued in the name of the revocable living trust.15.4. Indicate the cancellation and reissuance of shares on the corporate ledger kept in the corporate minute book.15.5. Place the reissued certificates in safekeeping or advise the settlors to place certificates in the settlors' safe deposit box.ARTICLE XVI. CORPORATE BONDS.16.1. Determine whether to:(a) Retain a stockbroker's services [see item 16.2, below]; or(b) Deal directly with the transfer agent [see item 16.3, below].16.2. If a stockbroker is engaged in connection with the transfer, advise the settlor to arrange for an appointment with the brokerage firm to complete an application to open a brokerage account in name of the trust.16.3. If the transfer will be made through a transfer agent directly, determine who the transfer agent is.16.4. Ask the settlors to assemble and deliver all original corporate bonds to the attorney.16.5. Complete either:(a) Stock Assignment Separate From Certificate, or(b) Irrevocable Stock and/or Bond Power. 16.6. Have each bond holder execute an assignment and/or a power for each transfer before:(a) A bank or trust company officer, or(b) A member firm of a nationally recognized security exchange authorized to make signature or medallion guarantee.16.7. Prepare a letter of instructions to the brokerage firm and/or transfer agent and obtain the signature of each bond holder.16.8. Mail the original bond certificate, assignment and/or power, and letter of instructions to the transfer agent or brokerage firm.16.9. Use registered or certified mail with return receipt requested.16.10. Mark the calendar to anticipate receipt of the reissued certificate within a month to six weeks.16.11. Upon receipt of the reissued bond certificates, instruct the settlors to place the reissued certificates in safekeeping (preferably in safe deposit box).ARTICLE XVII. MUNICIPAL BONDS--UNREGISTERED (BOOK ENTRY).17.1. Prepare a written assignment of bearer bond to the trust.17.2. Have the settlors sign an assignment of bearer bond to the revocable living trust in the presence of a notary public.17.3. Place the executed assignment of bearer bond with the original revocable living trust instrument.17.4. If the bearer bond is held in a stock brokerage account, instruct the stockbroker to simply change the title of the client's account to the name of the revocable living trust.17.5. Request that the stockbroker provide the attorney written confirmation of the transfer.ARTICLE XVIII. MUNICIPAL BONDS—REGISTERED.18.1. Ask the settlors to provide all original municipal bonds certificates.18.2. Complete either:(a) Stock Assignment Separate From Certificate, or(b) Irrevocable Stock and/or Bond Power.18.3. Have each bond holder execute an assignment and/or power for each transfer.18.4. Prepare a letter of instructions to the brokerage firm and/or transfer agent.18.5. Have each bond holder sign a letter of instructions.18.6. Mail the original bond certificates, stock assignment and/or stock power, and letter of instructions to the transfer agent or brokerage firm, by registered or certified mail with return receipt requested.18.7. Mark the calendar to expect to receive the reissued certificate within a month to six weeks.18.8. Instruct the settlors to place the bonds in safekeeping (preferably in a safe deposit box) upon receipt.ARTICLE XIX. UNITED STATES SAVINGS BONDS.19.1. Contact the settlors' bank or the Federal Reserve Bank in Minneapolis, MN, (800) 553-2663 or (612) 204-5000, and obtain the appropriate form to transfer the savings bonds to a revocable living trust.19.2. Have all owners sign on the reverse side of Form PD F 1851 to transfer the U.S. Savings Bonds Series E and EE, Series H and HH, and Series I.(a) Have the settlors sign before the appropriate officer from a bank, savings and loan or credit union authorized to give a signature guarantee.(b) Arrange for the appropriate signatures of any named POD beneficiary:(1) For Series E or Series H bonds, the POD beneficiary must sign on the reverse side of Form PD F 1851 to approve the transfer to the revocable living trust.(2) For Series EE, HH, and I bonds, the POD beneficiary's signature is not required on Form PD F 1851 to transfer Series EE, Series HH, or Series I bonds to the revocable living trust.19.3. Prepare a letter of instructions to the Federal Reserve Bank to transfer the settlors' U.S. Savings bonds to the trust.19.4. Mail Form PD F 1851, original savings bonds, and letter of instructions, by registered or certified mail with return receipt requested to the address shown on the form.19.5. Mark the calendar to expect to receive the reissued bonds within a month to six weeks.ARTICLE XX. TREASURY BONDS.20.1. Review the settlors' treasury bond statement to determine the account number.20.2. Obtain Form 5178, Treasury Direct Transaction Request, online at www.treasurydirect.gov or by calling Treasury Direct at 1-800-722-2678, and complete the form as follows:(a) The settlors' account number and account name must be stated exactly as they appear on the settlors' statement.(b) The request for name change must be in the following form:"[Name of Trustee(s)], Trustee(s) of [Name and Date of Trust]."(c) Under the "Taxpayer Identification Number Correction," a mistake in the taxpayer identification number may be corrected. If a transfer is being made to an account with a different taxpayer identification number, Security Transfer Request, FS Form 5179 must be used.(d) Under "telephone number change," indicate the settlors' work and home telephone numbers.(e) Under "Authorization," both owners should date and sign Form 5178 on the reverse side before an appropriate commercial bank or savings and loan officer authorized to guarantee signatures.(f) The "Certification" section should be completed by an officer of a commercial bank or an officer of a savings and loan authorized to give signature guarantees.20.3. Prepare a letter of instructions to the Federal Reserve Bank or to the settlors' local bank, and have the settlors sign the letter of instructions.20.4. Mail Form 5178 and the letter of instructions to the location indicated on the form.20.5. Instruct the settlors to review future treasury bonds statements to confirm that the transfer has been made.ARTICLE XXI. QUALIFIED ANNUITY PLANS.21.1. Contact the settlors' insurance company and obtain a change of beneficiary designation and spousal consent form for the annuity contract.21.2. Complete and have the settlors execute the change of beneficiary designation form and spousal consent.(a) For single persons, the new primary beneficiary designation should read:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."(b) For married persons, new primary beneficiary should be participant's spouse.(c) For married persons, the contingent beneficiary should read:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."21.3. Review the settlors' estate planning documents to determine whether it is necessary to execute a codicil or an agreement in conjunction with the annuity beneficiary designation to satisfy the requirements of Estate of MacDonald, (1990) 51 Cal. 3d 262, 272 Cal. Rptr. 153, 794 P.2d 911, concerning characterization of property.21.4. Have the settlors execute a codicil or enter into a joint agreement if it is necessary to comply with MacDonald.21.5. Prepare a letter of instructions to accompany the change of beneficiary designation.21.6. Request that the company send confirmation when the transfer has been made on its records.ARTICLE XXII. INDIVIDUAL RETIREMENT ACCOUNTS (IRAS).22.1. Contact the client's account holder and obtain a change of beneficiary designation form for the IRA trust account.22.2. Complete and have the settlors execute the change of beneficiary designation form and spousal consent.(a) For single persons, if the revocable living trust will be named as the primary beneficiary of the IRA to receive all benefits under the trust account, then the new primary beneficiary designation should read:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."(b) For married persons, the primary beneficiary should ordinarily be participant's spouse to allow the surviving spouse to defer the payment of income taxes by rolling over the benefit.(c) For married persons, if the revocable living trust will be named as the contingent beneficiary, the contingent beneficiary designation should read:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."22.3. Review the settlors' estate planning documents to determine whether it is necessary to execute a codicil or an agreement in conjunction with the IRA beneficiary designation to satisfy the requirements of Estate of MacDonald, concerning characterization of property.22.4. Have the settlors execute a codicil or enter into a joint agreement if it is necessary to comply with MacDonald.22.5. Prepare a letter of instructions to accompany the change of beneficiary designation.22.6. Request that the company send confirmation when the transfer has been made on its records.ARTICLE XXIII. EMPLOYEE BENEFITS.23.1. Contact the client's employer and obtain a change of beneficiary designation form for the employee benefit plan.23.2. Complete and have the settlors execute a change of beneficiary designation form and spousal consent.(a) For an unmarried person, if the revocable living trust will be named as primary beneficiary of the plan to receive all benefits under the trust account, then the new primary beneficiary designation should read as follows:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."(b) For married persons, the primary beneficiary should ordinarily be the participant's spouse, to give the surviving spouse ability to defer the payment of income taxes by rolling over the benefit.(c) For married persons, if the revocable living trust will be named as the contingent beneficiary, the contingent beneficiary designation should be the trust. Language identical to that in Paragraph 23.2(a), above, should be used to make designation.23.3. Determine if it is necessary to execute a codicil or an agreement in conjunction with the beneficiary designation to satisfy the requirements of Estate of MacDonald, concerning characterization of property.23.4. Have the settlors execute a codicil or enter into a joint agreement if necessary to comply with MacDonald.23.5. Prepare a letter of instructions to accompany the change of beneficiary designation.23.6. Request that the company send confirmation when the transfer has been made on its records.ARTICLE XXIV. PENSION PLANS.24.1. Contact the client's employer and obtain a change of beneficiary designation form for the pension plan.24.2. Complete and have the settlors execute the change of beneficiary designation form and spousal consent.(a) For an unmarried person, if the revocable living trust will be named as the primary beneficiary of the plan to receive all benefits under the trust account, the new primary beneficiary designation should read as follows:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."(b) For married persons, the primary beneficiary should ordinarily be the participant's spouse, to give the surviving spouse the ability to defer the payment of income taxes by rolling over the benefit.(c) For married persons, if the revocable living trust will be named as the contingent beneficiary, the contingent beneficiary designation should be the trust. Language identical to that in Paragraph 24.2(a), above, should be used to make designation.24.3. Determine if it is necessary to execute a codicil or an agreement in conjunction with the beneficiary designation to satisfy the requirements of Estate of MacDonald, concerning characterization of property.24.4. Have the settlors execute a codicil or enter into a joint agreement if necessary to comply with MacDonald.24.5. Prepare a letter of instructions to accompany the change of beneficiary designation.24.6. Request that the company send confirmation when the transfer has been made on its records.ARTICLE XXV. EMPLOYEE STOCK OWNERSHIP PLAN (ESOPS).25.1. Contact the client's employer and obtain a change of beneficiary designation form for the ESOP plan.25.2. Complete and have the settlors execute the change of beneficiary designation form and spousal consent.(a) For an unmarried person, if the revocable living trust will be named as primary beneficiary of the plan to receive all benefits under the trust account, then the primary beneficiary designation should read as follows:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."(b) For married persons, the primary beneficiary should ordinarily be the participant's spouse, to give the surviving spouse the ability to defer the payment of income taxes by rolling over the benefit.(c) For married persons, if the revocable living trust will be named as the contingent beneficiary, the contingent beneficiary designation should be the trust. Language identical to that in Paragraph 25.2(a), above, should be used to make designation.25.3. Determine if it is necessary to execute codicil or agreement in conjunction with beneficiary designation to satisfy requirements of Estate of MacDonald, concerning characterization of property.25.4. Have the settlors execute a codicil or enter into a joint agreement if necessary to comply with MacDonald.25.5. Prepare a letter of instructions to accompany the change of beneficiary designation.25.6. Request that the company send confirmation when the transfer has been made on its records.ARTICLE XXVI. EMPLOYEE LIFE INSURANCE.26.1. Contact the client's employer and obtain a change of beneficiary designation form for the life insurance policy.26.2. Complete and have the settlors execute the change of beneficiary designation form and spousal consent. Use the following language for the new beneficiary designation:"THE TRUSTEE THEN IN OFFICE UNDER THE TERMS OF THE [Name of Trust], DATED [Date of Trust]."26.3. Determine if it is necessary to execute a codicil or an agreement in conjunction with the beneficiary designation to satisfy the requirements of Estate of MacDonald, concerning characterization of property.26.4. Have the settlors execute a codicil or enter into a joint agreement if necessary to comply with MacDonald.26.5. Request that the insurance company send confirmation when the transfer has been made on its records.ARTICLE XXVII. INSTALLMENT OBLIGATIONS.27.1. Review the installment obligation held by settlor(s) to determine whether said obligation is:(a) Unsecured; or(b) Secured by a deed of trust or other security.27.2. If the installment obligation is unsecured, the settlors should assign the obligation to the revocable living trust to indicate an intent to fund the trust with the obligation:(a) Prepare an assignment of promissory note.(b) Arrange for the settlors to sign the assignment in front of a notary public.(c) Present the assignment to the debtor and ask debtor to sign the acknowledgment:(1) Recognizing the assignment; an(2) Indicating that future payments will be made to trustees in the following form:[Name of Trustee(s)], Trustees under the terms of [Name and Date of Trust].(d) Have the settlors date and endorse the original promissory note on the reverse side. The following language is recommended:As a conveyance without consideration to change the manner in which the interest of the parties is held, we, [Names of Settlors] assign to [Name of Trustee(s)], as Trustee(s) of [Name of Trust], dated [Date of Trust], all right, title and interest in this promissory note for $[Amount of Note], dated [Date of Note], and signed by [Name(s) of Payor(s)].____________________[Signature(s) of Settlor/Assignor(s)](e) Attach the assignment of promissory note to the client's original promissory note, once the assignment has been completed and the debtor has acknowledged the assignment.(f) Instruct the settlors to retain the original promissory note and the assignment in safekeeping.27.3.
