If the promissory note is secured, the settlors should prepare and record the assignment of deed of trust to the revocable living trust:(a) Obtain form for assignment of deed of trust from any title company or prepare the attorney's own form.(b) Have the settlors execute the assignment of deed of trust before a notary public.(c) Complete a preliminary change of ownership report.(d) Complete a (documentary) transfer tax affidavit if required by the county recorder's office (i.e., El Dorado, Merced, Riverside, San Francisco, San Bernardino, San Joaquin, San Mateo, and Santa Barbara Counties).(e) Record the assignment of deed of trust in the county where the settlors' real property is located.(f) Mail the original preliminary change of ownership report and (documentary) transfer tax affidavit (if required) with the assignment of deed of trust to the county recorder, and instruct the recorder to forward the preliminary change of ownership report and transfer tax affidavit (if required) to the county assessor.(g) Mail a copy of the assignment of deed of trust to the debtor when the assignment of deed of trust has been recorded and returned.(h) Attach the assignment of deed of trust to the original deed of trust to indicate the progression of title.(i) Instruct the settlors to retain the original promissory note and assignment of deed of trust in safekeeping.ARTICLE XXVIII. OTHER LIQUID ASSETS.28.1. Collect all passbooks and most recent financial statements to determine:(a) The names, addresses, and telephone numbers of financial institutions.(b) Account numbers.(c) How title is held.(d) The approximate value of each account.(e) Whether any accounts have automatic deposits.28.2. Request new signature cards from each financial institution, and complete them for each accountholder, showing title in following form:[Name of Trustee(s)], Trustees of [Name of Trust], Dated [Date of Trust].28.3. Have the settlors execute the new signature cards and arrange to personally deliver them to financial institutions.28.4. If it is inconvenient for the settlors to personally deliver new signature cards to a financial institution, prepare letter of instructions.28.5. Instruct the settlors to review future statements to confirm the transfer to the trust.ARTICLE XXIX. AUTOMOBILES, VEHICLES AND VESSELS REGISTERED WITH DEPARTMENT OF MOTOR VEHICLES.29.1. Comply with the California Department of Tax and Fee Administration (CDTFA) procedures to obtain use tax clearance and thereby avoid a transfer tax.(a) Contact the nearest CDTFA office and obtain Vehicle/Vessel Use Tax Information, Form BT-106.(b) Complete Form BT-106.(c) Assemble the following documentation required by the CDTFA to issue use tax clearance:(1) Certificate of ownership ("pink slip").(2) Current registration, if available.(3) Trustee Certificate with pertinent articles of revocable living trust (i.e., face page, trust powers, signature, and notary pages).(4) Provide the name of the lienholder, if any.(d) Arrange to have the documents delivered to nearest CDTFA office (see locations listed on back of Form BT-106 or refer to www.cdtfa.ca.gov.29.2. Check to determine that the CDTFA has returned a copy of Form BT-106 [see item 29.1, above] along with a Use Tax Clearance -- either Form BT-111 (internal form) for gasoline vehicles and diesel vehicles under 7,000 lbs, or Form BT-1138 (internal form) for diesel vehicles over 7,000 lbs.29.3. Have the settlors present in person or by mail Form BT-106 and Form BT-111 or Form BT-1138 (whichever is appropriate [see item 29.2, above]) to the nearest office of Department of Motor Vehicles.29.4. Arrange for the registered owner(s) to sign off on line 1 on the face of the Certificate of Ownership as follows:(a) For sole ownership, only the signature of the sole owner is required.(b) For joint ownership (when "or" appears between the names), only one signature is required.(c) If "and" appears between names, then both signatures are required.29.5. Complete the new registered owner information, with the settlors' current address, on the reverse side of the Certificate of Ownership ("pink slip") to transfer title to the trustee.  The form of the title should read as follows:[Name of Trustee(s)], Trustees, or successor(s) of the [Name and Date of Trust].29.6. Submit a check in the appropriate amount for the transfer fee.  Make the check payable to the Department of Motor Vehicles.29.7. Mark the calendar four to six weeks ahead to ascertain whether the settlors received the new certificate.ARTICLE XXX. PROPERTY REGISTERED WITH DEPARTMENT OF HOUSING AND COMMUNITY DEVELOPMENT (HCD).30.1. Have the settlors locate and provide the Certificate of Title issued by HCD, or the Certificate of Ownership ("pink slip") issued by the Department of Motor Vehicles prior to July 1, 1981.30.2. Have all registered owners sign the Certificate of Title.30.3. Have the settlors locate and provide the current registration, if possible.30.4. Prepare the Multi-Purpose Transfer Form, Form HCD 476.6G.30.5. Prepare and submit an Application for Registration, Form HCD 480.5 if the settlors' Certificate of Ownership for a mobile home was issued by the Department of Motor Vehicles prior to July 1, 1981.30.6.  Submit a check for the transfer fee.30.7. Instruct HCD to re-issue the Certificate of Title showing title in following form:"[Name of Trustee(s)], Trustee(s)."30.8. Arrange for the settlors to take documentation directly to HCD at 2020 West El Camino Avenue, Sacramento, CA 95833 or or mail the documents to P.O. Box 277820, Sacramento, CA 958-27-7820.ARTICLE XXXI. VESSELS REGISTERED THROUGH COAST GUARD.31.1. Comply with the State Board of Equalization procedures to obtain a use tax clearance and thereby avoid a transfer tax.31.2. If there is a lienholder on the vessel, the State Board of Equalization will require the lienholder's approval of the transfer to the trust.(a) If so, contact the Coast Guard and obtain an Application, Consent, and Approval for Withdrawal of Application for Documentation or Exchange of Certificate of Documentation, (Form CG-4593, Rev. 9/92).(b) Complete Form CG-4593 and submit it to the lienholder for approval and return.(c) Submit a copy of the signed Application Form CG-4593 and the Vehicle/Vessel Use Tax Information (Form BT-106) to the State Board of Equalization to obtain the use tax clearance.  Retain the original Form CG-4593 to send to Coast Guard with the executed Coast Guard forms.31.3. Check to determine that the Board of Equalization has returned a copy of Form BT-106 [see item 31.1, above] along with a Use Tax Clearance; either Form BT-111 or Form BT-1138.31.4. Telephone the Coast Guard National Vessel Documentation Center (NVDC) in Falling Waters, West Virginia at (800) 799-8362, to obtain following forms, and complete the forms:(a) Application for Initial Issue, Exchange, or Replacement of Certificate of Documentation; Redocumentation (Form CG-1258, Rev. 9-92).(b) Bill of Sale (Form CG-1340, Rev. 9-92).31.5. Instruct the Coast Guard Marine Safety Office to re-title the vessel in the name of trust, using following form of title: [Name of Trustee(s)], Trustees.31.6. Forward the State Board of Equalization Forms BT-106 and BT-111 and executed Coast Guard forms to the Coast Guard Marine Safety Office at 792 T J Jackson Drive, Falling Waters, West Virginia 25419 for registration in the name of the revocable living trust.31.7. Include a check for the transfer fee.31.8. Mark the calendar to expect to receive the new registration in the trust name within four to six weeks.ARTICLE XXXII. AIRCRAFT.32.1. Contact the FAA Aircraft Registry at (405) 954-3116 or (866) 762-9434 or go online to its Website at https://www.faa.gov/licenses_certificates/aircraft_certification/aircraft_registry/ to obtain forms or general information about the FAA and obtain the following forms:(a) Aircraft Registration Application, Form 8050-1.(b) Aircraft Bill of Sale, Form 8050-2.32.2. Complete the Application and Bill of Sale, making certain that:(a) The names are identical on each conveyance document.(b) The names are identical to the names shown on the evidence of ownership.(c) The type of registration block is checked on the Aircraft Registration Application and signer's name agrees.32.3. Obtain the signatures of all owners on the Aircraft Registration Application (Form 8050-1, 12/90) and the Bill of Sale (Form 8050-2, 9/92) where required.32.4. Submit the following documentation to FAA:(a) Aircraft Registration Application, Form 8050-1 (12/90), white and green copies.(b) Aircraft Bill of Sale, Form 8050-2 (9/92), original.(c) Evidence of ownership (i.e., conveyances reflecting chain of ownership from last owner to applicant):(1) Bill of sale.(2) Contract of conditional sale.(3) Transfer of equitable interest.(4) Transfer of interest.(5) Repossession.(6) Foreclosure.(7) Judicial sale.(8) Foreclosure or mechanic's lien.(9) Certified court order.(10) Affidavit stating why evidence of ownership cannot be produced.(d) Provide the Trustee's Certification of Trust with a full copy of trust instrument. Type or write "TRUE COPY OF ORIGINAL" across the face of the trust instrument.32.5. Prepare an affidavit stating that each beneficiary under the trust is either a United States citizen or a resident alien.32.6. Provide a check payable to the FAA for the registration fee.32.7. Instruct the FAA to reissue the new registration to a single applicant as trustee or to several trustees jointly as co-trustees.(a) For a single person, the title should read as follows: [Name of Trustee], Trustee.(b) For married persons, the title should read as follows: [Name of Trustee(s)], Co-Trustees.32.8. Forward the documentation and the fee to the FAA at following address:FAA Aircraft Registration BranchP. O. Box 25504Oklahoma City, OK 73125-050432.9. Mark the calendar to expect receipt of the new registration within six to eight weeks.ARTICLE XXXIII. TANGIBLE PERSONAL PROPERTY AND COLLECTIBLES.33.1. Complete a declaration of trust ownership.33.2. Prepare and attach list of assets of intrinsic value to the declaration of trust ownership if the settlors' estate includes personal property such as coin collections, antiques, tools, equipment, art collections, stamps, etc.33.3. For assets of intrinsic value, encourage the settlors to attach photographs of assets or expert appraisals to the original declaration of trust ownership.33.4. Contact the settlors' insurance carrier and request that the trustee be added as an additional insured on any policy covering tangible personal property.33.5. Instruct the settlors to attach the insurance rider to the original declaration of trust ownership.33.6. Place the original declaration of trust ownership with the settlors' original estate plan documents.ARTICLE XXXIV. PATENTS AND TRADEMARKS.34.1. Review legal documents evidencing the settlors' ownership to determine whether ownership terminates at death.34.2. Prepare an assignment of patent or trademark, as appropriate.34.3. Have the settlors execute the assignment before a Notary Public.34.4. Prepare one of following, as appropriate, to accompany the assignment:(a) Recordation Form Cover Sheet Patents Only, Form PTO-1595; or(b) Recordation Form Cover Sheet Trademarks Only.34.5. Include a stamped self-addressed post card for use by the PTO to confirm receipt of the assignment.34.6. Make a check for the appropriate recording fee payable to the "Commissioner of Patents and Trademarks."(a) Patents: $40.00 for each patent.(b) Trademarks: $40.00 for the first trademark; $25.00 for each additional trademark.34.7. Mail the assignment, postcard and appropriate recordation fee for each patent and/or trademark being assigned to the settlors' revocable living trust to: Mail Stop Assignment Recordation Services, Director of the USPTO, P.O. Box 1450, Alexandria, VA 22313-1450.34.8. Mark the calendar to expect receipt of the original assignment within six to eight weeks.ARTICLE XXXV. COPYRIGHTS.35.1. Prepare an assignment of copyright to the trust.35.2. Have the settlors execute the assignment of copyright to trust before a notary public.35.3. Prepare a letter of instructions to accompany each assignment submitted, instructing the Copyright Office to re-register title of the copyright(s) in the name of the settlors' revocable living trust.35.4. Mail the assignment and letter of instructions, together with the appropriate fee, to:Copyright OfficeLibrary of Congress, Copyright Office101 Independence Avenue, SEWashington, D.C. 20559Attn: Document Unit.End of Document

Letter to Client 
Table of Contents
[name of law firm][law firm address][law firm phone number][Month] [Day], [Year][name of client][address of client]RE: Funding of Revocable Living TrustDear [name of client]:Now that you have established your Revocable Living Trust (the "Trust"), you may fund it by transferring ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. An unfunded or partially funded trust does not avoid probate. While your pour over will provides that assets held in your individual name at your death will be added to the Trust assets and distributed as provided in the Trust Agreement, those assets will be subject to probate before they are added to the Trust property. To avoid that result, you may wish to retitle all assets in the name of your Revocable Trust as further outlined in this letter. Property is only considered trust property if it is titled in the name of the trust itself.Please review your assets to determine which to transfer into your Trust. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property located in any state or country, tangible personal property, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you may hold. Certain assets such as IRAs and some types of annuities should not be transferred to the Trust during your lifetime due to tax consequences or other considerations. Please consult my office for questions on whether an asset is appropriate or desirable for addition to your Trust property.To transfer assets to your Trust, you generally must execute new documents of title, deeds to real property, and signature cards for your bank accounts. Your financial advisor, accountant, and/or life insurance agent may need to help you make ownership or beneficiary changes. The instructions here will give you general knowledge of how to fund your Trust, but they cannot comprehensively address every type of transfer. Please call us if you must make transfers that are not addressed in these instructions, or if you would like assistance with any of these transfers. I can assist you in the asset transfer process, including the preparation of deeds and obtaining and filing necessary documentation. Generally, you should title all of your currently owned and newly acquired assets in the name of your Trust. We prefer the following format for titling assets in the name of your Trust:[name of client], as Trustee of the [name of revocable living trust] dated [Month] [Day], [Year], and any amendments thereto.We recommend you keep copies of all written documents that evidence the ownership interest of your Trust. Additionally, it is good practice to keep a list of all assets transferred to the Trust along with the date of transfer on Schedule A of the Trust. This schedule should be updated as Trust assets are added during your lifetime.You should carefully and continually review the assets held in your own name (both assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this is consistent with your estate planning goals. I would be glad to discuss the advisability of retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, holding assets in joint names with another person, or transferring assets to the Trustee of your Revocable Living Trust. On the following pages, I offer some general guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your Revocable Living Trust. These comments are general in nature, and if you require specific guidance, please contact my office.
Instructions for Transferring Specific Assets Cash Accounts You should sign new signature and ownership cards to retitle any sizeable bank accounts or cash equivalents, including treasury bills, money market accounts, and certificates of deposit, to name yourself as Trustee of those accounts. You need not change any small joint or other checking accounts used primarily for household expenses.Before you retitle your certificates of deposit, consult with a bank officer to make sure that the institution does not consider the change in account name to be an early withdrawal that incurs a penalty. Generally, this should not be a problem because your tax identification number for the account will remain the same.Instruct your financial institution by letter or in person to change the title to your Trust. The tax identification number (your Social Security number) on the account for withholding and reporting purposes will remain the same. Sign the new signature cards as Trustee.Investment Accounts If you hold publicly traded stocks and bonds through brokerage or investment accounts, contact your brokers or custodians and direct them to change the title of the accounts to the name of your Trust. The procedure for doing so is the same as the procedure for retitling cash accounts explained above. You may have to complete new account applications and present a copy of your Certification of Trust in order to change the title. Title to the accounts should be in the Trust name.Stocks and Bonds Not Held in Investment Accounts If you possess original stock or bond certificates, new certificates may be issued to reflect the Trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. Alternatively, you may open a brokerage or investment account in the name of your Revocable Living Trust and deposit your original certificates in the account. Be sure to keep track of the income tax basis of all shares as the Trustee will need that information if any shares are sold during your lifetime.Personal Effects Tangible personal property refers to such items as household furnishings, appliances and fixtures, works of art, motor vehicles, pictures, collectibles, personal wearing apparel and jewelry, books, sporting goods, and hobby paraphernalia. You can transfer your tangible personal property by executing an Assignment of Personal Property.Your personal vehicles can be titled in the name of your Revocable Living Trust, but we find that most of our clients prefer to leave their vehicles outside of the Trust. However, should you decide to title your vehicle in the name of the Trust, consult your casualty insurance agent and advise them of the change.Retirement Plans, Life Insurance Policies, and Annuities Generally speaking, ownership of these accounts should not be transferred to your Revocable Living Trust, but the death benefits can be made payable to the Trustee for distribution under the terms of the Trust. Designating the Trust as beneficiary of an annuity or retirement account may have unintended income tax consequences. Please contact me or your tax professional prior to making any such designation.Although life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate, you may want the proceeds from the policies paid directly to your Revocable Living Trust. In order to designate the Revocable Living Trust as the beneficiary, you will need to obtain and complete change-of-beneficiary forms. Your agent should be able to assist you with naming the Trustee of your Revocable Living Trust as the beneficiary of the policy, or if not, please contact me and I will be happy to assist you. You should receive a letter confirming the change of beneficiary from the insurance company to keep with your Trust records.If you have loaned money to anyone, you should assign your interest as lender to your Revocable Living Trust by a written document and notify your debtor of the assignment. We can prepare assignment documents for each interest for your signature.Partnership Interests If there are no restrictions in your partnership agreement, your interest in the partnership could be transferred through a written assignment of interest signed by you and acknowledged by your partners. It may be necessary to prepare a new certificate of partnership interest or an amendment to the partnership agreement.We can prepare assignment documents for each interest for your signature.Real Property Transferring real property to your Trust will require attention to ownership and tax issues based on the nature of the current title to the property. You will need to execute new deeds for each property, which you must record with the Clerk of Court in the county where the real property is located. At your request, we will assist you with this.Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer and whether the proposed transfer will trigger a "due on sale" clause in the instrument. It is important to comply carefully with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located.You should contact your homeowner's insurance provider to discuss adding your Trust as an additional endorsee on your policy. Lastly, you may wish to consult with a title agent to ensure that the property interest is eligible for transfer.Administering the Trust If a bank, financial institution, or other governing body requires evidence of the Revocable Living Trust to complete the transfer, use the Certification of Trust rather than producing a copy of the entire Trust Agreement. The Certification of Trust provides only the necessary information without disclosing confidential details.Once an asset has been transferred to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust, all transactions regarding that asset must be handled by the Trustee, rather than by you individually. For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the Trustee, it is the Trustee who has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock, or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting. Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the Trustee.As long as you are acting as Trustee of your Revocable Living Trust, you do not need to obtain a separate tax identification number for your Trust or to file a separate Trust tax return. The Internal Revenue Service (IRS) prefers that you use your own Social Security number. You should report all of the income generated by Trust assets on your personal IRS Form 1040.When you die, your Trust becomes irrevocable and the successor Trustee will need to obtain a tax identification number. We will work with your successor Trustee and your accountant to see that your heirs get the full benefit of your estate planning upon your death. Certain trust administration procedures will be required, and it is advisable for the successor Trustee to consult with an attorney at that time.You should review your estate plan with an attorney every two to three years because all estate plans require on-going maintenance. In particular, a change in your family circumstances, an increase in your net worth, or a change in the tax law could significantly affect your plan.I hope that this letter answers most of your immediate questions. Please call me if you have any additional questions about the transfer of property to your Trust or about the operation of the Trust in the future. This letter is for your guidance only and does not discuss all possible situations. Please keep it for future reference.
Sincerely yours,__________________[name of attorney]End of Document

Letter to Client 
Dear [name]:I am writing regarding the [name of beneficiary] Special Needs Trust Agreement dated [month] [day], [year], which we have prepared for you as [settlor of the trust / trustee of the trust / both settlor and trustee of the trust].The purpose of this letter is to (1) describe the process of transferring assets into the trust, and (2) address what you, as [settlor / trustee / both settlor and trustee] of the trust, need to know and think about while administering the trust. Therefore, I advise you to keep this letter for future reference.To accomplish the long-term goals for which the trust was created, the settlor and trustee must adhere to the terms of the trust agreement and the tax laws relating to trusts. If you have any questions about the tax laws relating to this trust, please do not hesitate to ask me or a tax professional.The trust describes the responsibilities and the powers of the trustee(s). Since all trust documents (also called trust instruments) do not contain the same powers, the trustee must pay special attention to the language in the trust to be certain of the trustee's authority and options.This trust is a third-party special needs trust. It is designed so that the family and friends of [name of beneficiary], the beneficiary of the trust, can make gifts to benefit [name of beneficiary] while preserving the right to receive government benefits. The gifts made to the trust can take many forms: cash, bonds, shares of stock, real property, or even personal property. Under no circumstances should any property or assets belonging to the beneficiary be transferred into the trust. If this is done, it could jeopardize the beneficiary's eligibility for government benefits. Also, the beneficiary has no right to demand or withdraw any assets or property from the trust.Irrevocability of Trust. The trust is an irrevocable trust. That means it is fixed and cannot be amended or terminated, except under very limited circumstances. Also, gifts made to the trust cannot be returned. Please call me if you need to make any changes to the trust.Federal Tax Identification Number. Whether or not the trust needs its own federal tax identification number (TIN) depends on whether the trust is a grantor trust or not. A grantor trust is a trust over which the grantor or other owner retains the power to control or direct the trust's income or assets. If the trust is a grantor trust, the trustee has the choice of reporting the annual income for federal tax purposes on either IRS Form 1041 or on the income tax return of the grantor. A grantor trust does not need its own TIN. If the trust is not a grantor trust, either the trustee or the attorney for the trust must obtain a TIN for the trust by filing IRS Form SS4. The TIN will be used to identify the trust in bank accounts and tax returns.Funding the Trust. The ownership of one or more assets must be transferred to the trustee for the trust to be in effect. The bank or brokerage account or the asset itself, such as shares of stock, should be titled in the name of the trust, as follows: "[name of trustee], Trustee of The [name of beneficiary] Special Needs Trust dated [month] [day], [year]." (Your bank may want to use a slightly different name.) If the trust is not a grantor trust, always use the trust's TIN as the identification number for bank accounts.Your stockbroker may request a copy of the signed trust instrument for examination by the firm's legal department. We generally do not provide a copy of the entire agreement. A copy of the Certification of Trust or the signature page and the trustee powers will demonstrate that the trust has been properly executed and the trustee has the necessary power to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds, and options, trade on margin, and conduct other trust business.To transfer real estate into the trust, a deed must be executed and recorded granting the property to the trust.Gift Tax Return. If any gift to the trust exceeds $19,000 in value, the donor should be instructed to  complete a gift tax return (IRS Form 709) by April 15 of the following year. Please consult me concerning  allocating some or all of your generation-skipping tax exemption to this trust.Taxation of Trust Income. The trustee is required to file IRS Form 1041 [OPTIONAL: and [applicable state fiduciary income tax return]] to report the income for any year in which the trust has taxable income. These forms are due on April 15 of the year following the taxable year. Although tax software is widely available for this purpose, we strongly recommend having these forms prepared by an accountant because of their complexity.Distributions from the Trust. The types of distributions that can be made to the disabled beneficiary are limited to those which will not jeopardize the benefits received from any county, state, federal, or other government agency. In general, distributions should not be made for basic support (e.g., housing, food, clothing, and medical treatment for which public or Medicaid funds are available). Here are some examples of permissible distributions: automobile, computer equipment, subscriptions, travel, furniture, education (tuition, books, supplies), health insurance, life insurance, sports equipment, tickets to events. Cash or cash equivalents should not be distributed directly to the beneficiary. Any distributions should be made directly to the third-party supplier of the goods or services.If you have any questions about permissible distributions, please do not hesitate to contact me.Termination of the Trust. The trust will terminate if any of the following events occur:• There are no more assets in the trust.• The disabled beneficiary dies, and the assets of the trust are distributed to the beneficiary's descendants or to the other contingent beneficiaries named in Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust.• The beneficiary has become ineligible for public benefits, per Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust.Trustee Powers. A detailed list of the trustee's powers can be found in Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust. Please read them and make sure you understand them. I will be happy to answer any questions you may have about them.Record Keeping. It is important that the trustee keep records of trust property separate from property that may not be in the trust. The settlor and the trustee will receive monthly and other account statements in the name of the trust. The Department of Health and Human Services and the Social Security Administration may audit trust expenditures, so it is crucial to have complete documentation.

Funding Revocable Trusts (MA)
Go to: The Importance of Funding a Revocable Living Trust   |   How to Transfer or Place Specific Assets into a Revocable Living Trust   |   Other Funding Issues   |   Future Trust AdministrationMaintainedThis practice note contains information and instructions on funding a revocable trust in Massachusetts. It provides a general description of the steps necessary to transfer many kinds of assets to a revocable trust; of course, the specific steps to accomplish this transfer will depend on the nature of the asset involved. One of the primary benefits of a revocable living trust is that, if fully and properly funded during the grantor's lifetime, probate can be avoided or minimized. Therefore, it is vitally important to transfer all of a settlor's assets into the trust, ideally soon after the creation of the trust, and then on an ongoing basis as needed throughout the grantor's lifetime.Trust funding must be taken seriously and professionally, as errors can result in client complaints and malpractice claims. Some attorneys choose not to involve themselves in the funding process. Others assume responsibility for certain tasks and not for others. To the extent possible, you should determine with your client in advance who will be responsible for which tasks and clearly explain that all tasks other than those you expressly agree to undertake will be the responsibility of the client unless you subsequently agree otherwise (ideally in writing). In situations where the client is to perform some or all funding tasks, you may wish to send a follow-up/reminder letter approximately 60 days after the trust is created to reiterate the importance of funding the trust and to see if they wish to involve your help with any remaining tasks. If you choose not to take on the responsibility of funding the trust for your client, or if the client declines your offer to help, you should at least provide explicit instructions about the process to the client.This practice note offers guidance to you when funding a revocable living trust for your clients, as well as when advising your clients as to how they should fund it.For a full listing of key content related to estate plans for married or partnered people in Massachusetts, see Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (MA).For related practice notes, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (MA), Requirements and Restrictions on Trust Purposes and Administration (MA), and Revocation, Amendment, and Termination of Trusts (MA).The Importance of Funding a Revocable Living TrustA grantor's assets must be transferred into the trust if it is to achieve one of its objectives: to simplify the transfer and administration of assets after the settlor's death. It is also necessary because the trustee only has control of the property if it is in the trust. If the grantor becomes incapacitated, the chosen successor trustee(s) will be able to continue managing trust assets without court involvement or other difficulty if the grantor's assets are in the trust at that time.To the extent that the settlor does not transfer assets into the trust, those assets will not escape probate. That is why a pour-over will should always be prepared along with the trust agreement to direct that any assets not held by the trust at the time of the grantor's death be transferred into the trust so that they will be distributed in accordance with the provisions of the trust. However, a probate proceeding will be necessary to accomplish this.How to Transfer or Place Specific Assets into a Revocable Living TrustThe following items are assets that are commonly held by a grantor. You should be mindful to get a clear and accurate picture of the types of assets your client owns so you can provide appropriate advice and try to ensure everything that must be transferred to the trust actually gets transferred.Personal ResidenceIn most cases, the grantor's interest in the home should be transferred from their own name to the name of the trustee (usually, the client), as trustee. This can be achieved by a quitclaim or warranty deed, and by filing any necessary transfer tax forms. Also, as seen in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 184, § 35, the grantor must file and record a Trustee's Certificate with the transaction. For a modest fee, a title insurance company can help make sure everything is done properly and efficiently. If the client undertakes this task, you should ask to be sent a copy of the recorded deed.The following are a few special points about the transfer of a residence or any other real property into a revocable living trust:• Under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 188, § 3, Massachusetts homestead residences are protected from certain creditors. It is wise to familiarize yourself and your client with these statutes. • There will be no reassessment because the transfer of real property to a revocable trust is not a "change in ownership" for property tax purposes.• There will be no transfer tax because the property has not been sold. However, appropriate transfer tax forms will need to be filed, indicating that it is a transfer for no consideration to a revocable trust.• While it is not legally required that the new deed be recorded right away, it is highly advisable to do so unless the terms of any mortgage contain language that makes it unwise. For example, most mortgage agreements have what is called an acceleration or "due on sale" clause, which means that the full balance on the mortgage becomes immediately due if there is a change in ownership. It is important to comply carefully with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located. You may consult with a title agent to ensure that the property interest is eligible for transfer. Since transferring the home into the trust isn't a change of ownership of the type a due-on-sale clause is meant to address, you or the client should also contact the lender, explain what you are trying to do, and confirm that the due-on-sale clause won't be triggered. Ideally, you or the client will get this in writing from the lender.• If a client has plans to refinance a property, advise them to complete the refinance before transferring the title to the trust. Cooperative or Condominium ApartmentsIf the client has such an ownership interest, you or the client should ask the manager of the cooperative or condominium about how it should be transferred into the trust. It is likely the management will have various formalities and fees associated with the transfer, but it is nevertheless important that the transfer be made. Please also note that if the client chooses to refinance their mortgage after transferring the cooperative apartment or condominium into the trust, they may have to transfer it out of the trust in order to refinance and then transfer it back into the trust after the refinance is complete. You or the client should always consult with the lender when making these types of changes to ensure compliance with the terms of the loan agreement.Other Real PropertyA new deed is required for every other piece of real property that the client owns. As noted above, it may be necessary to obtain the permission of the lender to transfer the property into the trust. If the other real property is located in another state, you or the client should consult an attorney in that state to assist in transferring that property, as different states have different rules and customs. If your client purchases real estate in the future, the client should generally take title in trustee capacity. If the client fails to do so, the new property must go through the probate process.Savings Accounts and Certificates of DepositThese accounts should be held by the trustee as trustee of the revocable trust. Banks and similar institutions commonly see revocable trusts and are used to helping their customers with necessary changes. A copy of the trust instrument or Memorandum of Trust is usually required. Note that a Memorandum of Trust is also sometimes called a Certificate of Trust or an Affidavit of Trust. See Certificate of Trust (MA) for a sample certificate of trust. Suggest that the bank make a copy if they require it for their records. The client should keep a copy of any new card or document that they sign to effect the change. If a particular savings account cannot be transferred before a set date without losing interest, the client may decide to wait. It may be necessary to wait until a Certificate of Deposit's maturity date before it can be retitled.Checking AccountsWhile such accounts can and, in almost all cases, should be put into the name of the trust, the client may want, for a special reason, to leave one account in one's own name as an individual. However, any such account should maintain a low balance. Bank records must indicate that accounts are held in the name of the trust, but personal checks can be printed with the client's name only, without reference to the trust. Advise your client that in Massachusetts, if an estate has less than $25,000 in value, it may not be subject to a full probate. Instead, an executor/personal representative may petition for a voluntary administration, a simplified probate procedure. For more information on voluntary administration, see Purposes and Uses of a Will (MA),Governing Law of Formal Probate, Jurisdiction, and Proceedings (MA) and Governing Law of Informal Probate, Jurisdiction, and Proceedings (MA).Life Insurance PoliciesYou or the client must contact their insurance agent or the company itself to obtain their "Change of Beneficiary" and "Change of Ownership" forms. In many cases, the owner and the beneficiary should be the trust. Because these changes are not effective until they are received and acknowledged by insurance companies, you or the client should take care of this as soon as possible. Be sure to receive written confirmation from the insurance company that the desired changes have been effected in company records and by endorsement on the policy.If the client has personal reasons for naming a particular individual as beneficiary, the client certainly may do so. This means that the proceeds of the policy will be distributed directly to the named beneficiaries, and not according to the terms of the trust. There are differing opinions and considerations on whether to name individuals or the trust as the primary beneficiary of life insurance policies, and these opinions and considerations should be discussed with the client to determine the primary beneficiary of such policies. For example, if the client wants an outright distribution of insurance proceeds to a named beneficiary who is an adult, and feels that person should have access to those proceeds quickly, then naming the individual as a beneficiary instead of the trust might be advisable. Also, while creditors often can go after insurance proceeds to satisfy any debts of the deceased grantor, it is perhaps less likely that will happen if the proceeds pay out directly to a named beneficiary as opposed to them being paid out to the trust.Insurance on Property Held by the TrustInsurance contracts that provide for payments to be made if there is loss or damage to assets held in trust (e.g., title insurance and homeowner's insurance) should be amended. You or the client should ask their insurance agent to have the policy or policies amended. The trustee(s) and the trust should be named as additional insured parties, using the "name" suggested under Other Funding Issues below.The grantor, as an individual, should continue to be named as an insured party even though the trust and trustee are added as additional insured parties.Stock/SecuritiesThe transfer of stock to the trust must be accomplished by changing title on the stock certificates themselves. The client's broker can help make this change for free or for a small charge per certificate. If your client holds shares through a brokerage firm, the client will need to change the title on only the brokerage accounts, rather than on each share certificate.The stockbroker may request copies of the certification of trust and the signed trust instrument for examination by the firm's legal department. A copy of the signature page and the trustee powers should be sufficient to demonstrate that the trust is properly executed and the trustee has the necessary power to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds, and options, trade on margin, and conduct other trust business. Alternatively, you or the client may be able to simply provide a copy of the Memorandum of Trust.If the client holds shares in a closely held corporation, the corporation's secretary should issue new certificates in the name of the trust. The existing (old) certificates should then be canceled. Before this is done, however, it should be determined whether there is a stock restriction agreement, whether an assignment might cause some sort of forfeiture, and whether the board must approve the transfer. It would be wise to consult with corporate counsel in this regard.You or the client should send any stock certificates via registered mail, return receipt requested, with enough insurance to cover the replacement bond, in case the certificates are lost in the mail. The amount of this bond can be obtained from the brokerage, and it guards against someone else trying to transfer the certificates if they somehow fall into someone else's hands.PartnershipsPartnership interests can be complicated. To register these partnership interests in the trust's name, a new certificate of partnership interest or an amendment to the partnership agreement may need to be executed. There may be restrictions against transfer in the partnership agreement, so it should be reviewed before attempting the transfer. A limited partnership agreement may indicate what is needed to transfer the interest. Such an agreement should be reviewed to determine (1) whether or not the client can transfer one's interest into the trust and, if so; (2) how to achieve this result.Promissory NotesThe client should assign each promissory note personally owned to the trust. The client can do so by typing on the back of each note the following endorsement:Pay to the order of ______________________, Trustee under ______________________ Trust Agreement Dated ______________________ ______________________.Go to table1The client should also assign the deeds of trust securing payment of the notes to the trust. You or the client need not record the assignment, but it should be prepared in the same manner as deeds to real property and should be retained in a safe place. If the trustee or a successor trustee needs to commence any action to obtain title to the property given as the security for payment of the note, the assignment can be recorded. Advise your client to notify the maker of a promissory note to make all future checks payable to the trust and not to your client.Patents and CopyrightsTo transfer patents or copyrights into a revocable trust, you or the client need to execute a written assignment of ownership document, specifically identifying the intellectual property and transferring the rights to the trust as the owner. Remember to file the necessary paperwork with the U.S. Copyright Office and the U.S. Patent and Trademark Office.IRAsIRAs must be held in the name of the individual who created the account. The client names beneficiaries on a "designation of beneficiary" form provided by the financial institution. Normally, a married person will name one's spouse as the first beneficiary and, as an alternate beneficiary, either the trust or individuals. Individuals as beneficiaries may have a few more tax elections than the trust will have. Naming the trust as beneficiary will have less such options, but would ensure that the proceeds of the account pay to beneficiaries in accordance with the terms of the trust.Retirement BenefitsRetirement benefits require special consideration since both the form of benefit and the choice of beneficiary affect the client's estate plan and can have significant tax consequences. These choices also have a major impact on married persons' lives in the event one spouse enters a nursing home. The client should consult with the plan administrator about the options available now and in the future. The necessary forms may require specific wording in order for your married clients to be eligible for the marital deduction and other benefits.If your client's employment terminated before 1985, the client may be entitled to certain important tax advantages. The client should review benefits with a pension specialist prior to making any changes to the retirement benefits.Normally a married client should first designate one's spouse as beneficiary of qualified plan benefits.AutomobilesIt may not be advisable to transfer automobiles to the trust. If, however, your client has a number of automobiles, or ones of particularly high value, these should be transferred to the trust. Automobiles should not be transferred to the trust unless the client is certain that adequate liability insurance is available to cover substantial claims, as the trust may be made a party to any lawsuits following an accident involving an automobile that the trust holds as the owner. If automobiles will be transferred to the trust, your client should contact the appropriate state licensing agency for the vehicle and seek instructions on how to transfer title to the trust. Your client should also contact the insurance carrier for the vehicle to ensure the vehicle will continue to be covered once it is placed in the trust. Advise your client that under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90D, § 15A, upon the death of a married resident owner of a motor vehicle registered as a pleasure vehicle in the commonwealth, and unless otherwise provided in a will, the motor vehicle is deemed jointly held property with right of survivorship and the interest of the decedent passes to the surviving spouse.Lease AgreementsThe lease under which your client is the lessor should be assigned to the trust using an Assignment of Lease. Your client should notify the lessee in writing that all future rental checks should be made payable directly to the trust, not to your client individually. It also may be beneficial to inform tenants that the terms of their existing lease will not change, but any renewal or new lease should reflect that the landlord is the revocable trust. There is no need to show the tenants any portion of the trust agreement.Cemetery PlotsYou or the client should transfer the client's cemetery plot to the trust. This is typically done through executing an Assignment of Burial Plots & Mausoleum. The director of the cemetery should receive a copy of this assignment.FirearmsWhile a revocable trust could own firearms, it is ill-advised to transfer into or hold firearms in the trust. In addition to it imposing significant risk and liability, most firearms are highly regulated and, under federal law, some individuals are considered prohibited persons concerning firearm ownership. Firearm laws are complex, and failure to abide by them can result in fines and imprisonment. For the purposes of holding and transferring firearms, there are special purpose gun trusts. For templates related to gun trusts, see Gun Trust, Assignment of Property to Gun Trust, and Letter to Client (Funding and Administering Gun Trust).Safe-Deposit BoxMany institutions allow a revocable trust to maintain a safe-deposit box. This avoids the need to obtain a court order or institute a probate proceeding simply to open the safe-deposit box where no alternate individual is named with access to the box. Your client can transfer the safe-deposit box by assigning the rights under the existing rental agreement to the trust or by having the trust itself enter into a new rental agreement with the bank. It is also a good idea for your client to give the successor trustee authority to access the box so that if your client should become incapacitated or even deceased, the successor trustee can get into the box.U.S. Savings BondsThe federal government requires the use of Form PD 1851, Request to Reissue United States Savings Bonds to a Personal Trust, for transferring U.S. Savings Bonds. The back of the form has instructions. After you or the client have filled out the form, the client's signature must be "guaranteed" (medallion stamped) by an officer at the bank and forwarded along with the bonds to the following. For EE or I savings bonds: Treasury Retail Securities Services, PO Box 214, Minneapolis, MN 55480-0214; for HH savings bonds: Treasury Retail Securities Services, PO Box 2186, Minneapolis, MN 55480-2186.Other Personal PropertyProperty such as airplanes, yachts, horses, artwork, royalty contracts, a family business, stock options, mobile homes, partnership interests, and other rare types of property have special considerations that should be researched to determine if and how to transfer such property into the trust.Personal property items (household furniture and furnishings, books, pictures, jewelry, hobby equipment and collections, wearing apparel, and other articles of household or personal use or ornament, etc.) can be transferred by a general assignment with particular descriptions of valuable items and a provision to cover after-acquired items.Other Funding IssuesIn What "Name" Should Trust Assets Be Held?Generally speaking, assets will be retitled in substantially the following format:[Trustee name], Trustee under [trust name] Trust Agreement Dated [month day, year].It presents no problems if a bank or any other financial institution wants to hold an asset in a slightly different name. The important thing is to be sure that the trust is not confused with any other trust and that the assets are clearly held in the trust.Income Tax ReturnsAt the time the trust is established, your client should inform the client's accountant.As long as the grantor serves as the trustee (which is usually the case), the grantor will use their Social Security number as the number for the trust. Your client will file the same income tax return previously filed. If a successor trustee takes over, a tax identification number must be obtained so that the trust can file an income tax return. The trust will, in effect, file a return with the same information and pay no more taxes than your client pays now, but the information will be reported on an IRS form designed for trustees and fiduciaries.The successor trustee will use IRS Form SS-4 to obtain the new tax identification number. When the number is received, it should be communicated to the client's accountant or tax preparer, as it will be used on the tax return to be signed by the trustee.RecordkeepingNo extraordinary recordkeeping is necessary beyond that which your client would ordinarily maintain. It is important, however, that the client keep records of trust property separate from property that may not be in the trust. Once changes in ownership to the trust are made, your client will receive monthly and other account statements in the name of the trust. Information your client receives from the stockbroker or other sources of financial information should also be adequate.Preparation of Schedule AYou or your client should prepare a Schedule A listing all assets transferred to the trust. Examples of these listings are:• 100 shares, P, G & E, common• Real property at 0000 High Street, Palo Alto, California –and–• Limited partnership interest in XXX Fund 1 unitFuture Changes in Trust AssetsIf the trust should subsequently dispose of a listed asset, it may be removed from the schedule by keeping a separate schedule and indicating its disposition and the date it was disposed of. Schedule A, as originally prepared, need not be changed, as it only indicates assets as of a particular day. As assets are removed, your client should simply keep a record of the change. Likewise, if a new asset is acquired by the trust, it may be added to a new schedule.The client should review the schedule of trust assets each year when income tax returns are prepared. Delete what has been sold. Add what has been acquired and confirm that it is held in the name of the trust.Future Trust AdministrationThe focus at the time the trust is created is funding the trust and administering it during your client's lifetime. At death, certain trust administrative procedures are required, and consultation by the successor trustee with an attorney will likely be necessary. If the trust has been properly funded, the cost of trust administration will be significantly less than probate. Furthermore, if the trust contains provisions for an A/B trust, significant tax savings and other benefits are likely. You may also wish to suggest that your client create a list of their professional advisers (e.g., you, their financial planner, their CPA/tax preparer, etc.), as well as the institutions holding the grantor's assets—along with contact information for each—and keep it with their trust records. This can be of great benefit to successor trustees who may not be familiar with the grantor's assets and who to contact about those assets.Current as of: 12/12/2024

Letter to Client 
[Month Day, 20__][name of client][address of client]RE: Funding of Revocable Living TrustDear [name of client]:Now that you have established your Revocable Living Trust (the "Trust"), you may fund it by transferring ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. An unfunded or partially funded trust does not avoid probate. While your pour over will provides that assets held in your individual name at your death will be added to the Trust assets and distributed as provided in the Trust Agreement, those assets will be subject to probate before they are added to the Trust property. To avoid that result, you may wish to retitle all assets in the name of your Revocable Trust as further outlined in this letter.Please review your assets to determine which to transfer into your Trust. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property located in any state, tangible personal property, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you may hold. Certain assets should not be transferred to the Trust during your lifetime, for example, IRAs and some types of annuities.Generally, to transfer assets to your Trust, you must execute new documents of title, deeds to real property, and signature cards for your bank accounts. Your financial advisor, accountant, and/or life insurance agent may need to help you make ownership or beneficiary changes. The instructions here will give you general knowledge of how to fund your Trust, but they cannot comprehensively address every type of transfer. Please call us if you must make transfers that are not addressed in these instructions, or if you would like assistance with any of these transfers. I can assist you in the asset transfer process, including the preparation of deeds and obtaining and filing necessary documentation.Generally, you should title all of your currently owned and newly acquired assets in the name of your Trust. We prefer the following format for titling assets in the name of your Trust:  [name of client], as Trustee of the [name of revocable living trust] dated [Month Day 20__], and any amendments thereto.We recommend you keep copies of all written documents which evidence the ownership interest of your Trust. Additionally, it is good practice to keep a list of all assets transferred to the Trust, along with the date of transfer, on Schedule A of the Trust.You should carefully and continually review the assets held in your own names (both assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this is consistent with your estate planning goals. I would be glad to discuss the advisability of retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, holding assets in joint names with another person, or transferring assets to the Trustee of your Revocable Living Trust. On the following pages, I offer some general guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your Revocable Living Trust. These comments are general in nature, and if you require specific guidance, please contact my office.
Instructions for Transferring Specific Assets1. Cash Accounts. You should sign new signature and ownership cards to retitle any sizeable bank accounts or cash equivalents, including treasury bills, money market accounts, and certificates of deposit, to name yourself as Trustee of those accounts. You need not change any small joint or other checking accounts used primarily for household expenses.Before you retitle your certificates of deposit, consult with a bank officer to make sure that the institution does not consider the change in account name to be an early withdrawal that incurs a penalty. Generally, this should not be a problem because your tax identification number for the account will remain the same.Instruct your financial institution by letter or in person to change the title to your Trust. The tax identification number (your Social Security number) on the account for withholding and reporting purposes will remain the same. Sign the new signature cards as Trustee.2. Investment Accounts. If you hold publicly traded stocks and bonds through brokerage or investment accounts, contact your brokers or custodians and direct them to change the title of the accounts to the name of your Trust. The procedure for doing so is the same as the procedure for retitling cash accounts explained above. You may have to complete new account applications and present a copy of your Certification of Trust in order to change the title. Title to the accounts should be in the Trust name.3. Stocks and Bonds Not Held in Investment Accounts.If you possess original stock or bond certificates, new certificates may be issued to reflect the Trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. Alternatively, you may open a brokerage or investment account in the name of your Revocable Living Trust and deposit your original certificates in the account.4. Personal Effects. Tangible personal property refers to such items as household furnishings, appliances and fixtures, works of art, motor vehicles, pictures, collectibles, personal wearing apparel and jewelry, books, sporting goods, and hobby paraphernalia. You can transfer your tangible personal property by executing an Assignment of Personal Property.Your personal vehicles can be titled in the name of your Revocable Living Trust, but we find that most of our clients prefer to leave their vehicles outside of the Trust. However, should you decide to title your vehicle in the name of the Trust, consult your casualty insurance agent and advise them of the change.5. Retirement Plans, Life Insurance Policies, and Annuities.Generally speaking, ownership of these accounts should not be transferred to your Revocable Living Trust, but the death benefits can be made payable to the Trustee for distribution under the terms of the Trust. Designating the Trust as beneficiary of an annuity or retirement account may have unintended income tax consequences. Please contact me or your tax professional prior to making any such designation.Although life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate, you may want the proceeds from the policies paid directly to your Revocable Living Trust. In order to designate the Revocable Living Trust as the beneficiary, you will need to obtain and complete change-of-beneficiary forms. Your agent should be able to assist you with naming the Trustee of your Revocable Living Trust as the beneficiary of the policy, or if not, please contact me and I will be happy to assist you. You should receive a letter confirming the change of beneficiary from the insurance company to keep with your Trust records.6. Partnership Interests. If there are no restrictions in your partnership agreement, your interest in the partnership could be transferred through a written assignment of interest signed by you and acknowledged by your partners. It may be necessary to prepare a new certificate of partnership interest or an amendment to the partnership agreement.We can prepare assignment documents for each interest for your signature.7. Real Property.Transferring real property to your Trust will require attention to ownership and tax issues based on the nature of the current title to the property. You will need to execute new deeds for each property, which will need to be recorded with the Clerk of Court in the county where the real property is located. At your request, we will assist you with this.Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer and whether the proposed transfer will trigger a "due on sale" clause in the instrument. It is important to comply carefully with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located.You should contact your homeowner's insurance provider to discuss adding your Trust as an additional endorsee on your policy. Lastly, you may wish to consult with a title agent to ensure that the property interest is eligible for transfer.Administering the TrustIf a bank, financial institution, or other governing body requires evidence of the Revocable Living Trust to complete the transfer, use the Certification of Trust rather than producing a copy of the entire Trust Agreement. The Certification of Trust provides only the necessary information without disclosing confidential details.Once an asset has been transferred to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust, all transactions regarding that asset must be handled by the Trustee, rather than by you individually. For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the Trustee, it is the Trustee who has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock, or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting. Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the Trustee.As long as you are acting as Trustee of your Revocable Living Trust, you do not need to obtain a separate tax identification number for your Trust or to file a separate Trust tax return. The Internal Revenue Service (IRS) prefers that you use your own Social Security number. You should report all of the income generated by Trust assets on your personal IRS Form 1040.When you die, your Trust becomes irrevocable and the successor Trustee will need to obtain a tax identification number. We will work with your successor Trustee and your accountant to see that your heirs get the full benefit of your estate planning upon your death. Certain trust administration procedures will be required, and it is advisable for the successor Trustee to consult with an attorney at that time.You should review your estate plan with an attorney every two to three years because all estate plans require on-going maintenance. In particular, a change in your family circumstances, an increase in your net worth, or a change in the tax law could significantly affect your plan.I hope that this letter answers most, if not all, of your immediate questions. Please call me if you have any additional questions about the transfer of property to your Trust or about the operation of the Trust in the future. This letter is for your guidance only and does not discuss all possible situations. Please keep it for future reference.Sincerely yours,____________________[name of attorney]End of Document

Letter to Client 
[name of law firm][law firm address][law firm phone number][date][name of client(s)][address]RE: Funding of Revocable TrustDear [name of client(s)]:FUNDING YOUR REVOCABLE TRUSTNow that you have established your Revocable Trust, it must be funded by transferring ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. If you own any assets jointly and undividedly with one or more persons and wish to transfer those assets into your Revocable Trust, all of the joint title owners will have to join in the conveyance.This memorandum provides a general description of the steps necessary to transfer many kinds of assets to your Revocable Trusts. Of course, the specific steps to accomplish this transfer will depend on the nature of the asset involved. If you would like assistance with any of these transfers, please contact my office. I can assist you in the asset transfer process, including the preparation of deeds and obtaining and filing necessary documentation.Please examine your assets to determine which assets to transfer into your Revocable Trust and contact my office if you would like advice about whether to transfer specific assets into the Trust. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property located in any state or country, mineral and royalty interests, tangible personal property, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you may hold. Assets that cannot be transferred to the trust during your lifetime(s) include IRAs, 401ks, and some types of annuities.In general, to transfer assets to your Trusts, you will need to change the title for each asset from your names individually or jointly to the Revocable Trust, as follows:[name of client 1] and [name of client 2] as TRUSTEES of the [name of Revocable Living Trust], dated [date]One of the major goals accomplished by a Revocable Trust as the main instrument in your estate plan is to avoid probate of the trust assets at the time of your deaths. In order to avoid probate, it is critically important that assets be titled in the name of the serving Trustee—as Trustee, not in the Trustee's name individually—of the Revocable Trust and not by you individually and/or jointly, as outlined above. Although your pour-over Will provides that assets held in your individual name at your death will be added to the trust assets and distributed as provided in your Trust Agreement, those assets will be subject to probate before they are added to the trust property. To avoid that result, you may wish to retitle all assets in the name of your Revocable Trust as outlined above.You should carefully and continually review the assets held in your own names (both assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this is consistent with your estate planning goals. I will be glad to discuss the advisability of retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, holding assets in joint names with another person, or transferring assets to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust. On the following pages, I offer some general guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your Revocable Trust. These comments are general in nature, and if you require specific guidance, please contact my office.Bank Accounts or Certificates Issued by a Bank or Other Savings Institution. The name on the bank account or certificate must be changed to reflect its ownership by the Trust, and a new passbook or certificate must be issued in the name of the Trustee on behalf of the Trust. Be careful to confirm with the financial institution before transferring a savings certificate that there will be no penalty for early withdrawal.Safety Deposit Box. The lessee of a safety deposit box should be changed or acquired in the name of the Trust to allow the acting Trustee access to the safety deposit box. The bank may require new signature cards.Brokerage Accounts. If you own stocks, bonds, or other securities through a brokerage account, the brokerage firm's name and records must be changed to indicate that the Trustee owns the account on behalf of the Trust and you do not own it individually. This also applies if the certificates for those assets are not issued in your name but are held instead in a street name or in the brokerage firm's name.Corporate Stocks (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations) or Mutual Fund Shares. If certificates are issued in your name(s), both individual or jointly, new certificates must be issued to reflect the Trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. You may be able to combine all certificates representing shares in one corporation into one new certificate, but you may wish to consider requesting a series of new certificates, one corresponding to each old certificate, in order to trace your income tax basis if any shares are sold by the Trustee during your lifetimes.Registered Bonds or Debentures (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations, the U.S. Government, an Agency, any State, or Subdivision thereof). These securities must also be registered in the name of the Trustee and new bonds or debentures reissued. As with stock certificates discussed above, if bonds or debentures are held in a brokerage account, title to the account must be changed. U.S. Savings Bonds series EE and series I are reissued electronically using Form 1851 found at treasurydirect.gov/forms/sav1851.pdf.Unregistered or Bearer Bonds or Debentures. These securities, typically issued by the United States government, an agency, a state, or a municipality, generally cannot be registered in the name of the Trustee, so that there must be some other evidence that these securities have been transferred. This can be accomplished by executing and delivering to the Trustee an Assignment to Revocable Trust, listing the bearer securities being transferred, which will be retained by the Trustee with the permanent records of the trust. I can prepare for you any documents necessary to accomplish the transfer. It would be helpful to keep bearer securities in a safe deposit box in the name of the Trustee with a duplicate copy of the transfer document attached.Tangible Personal Property including Tangible Investment Assets (Such as Gold Bullion, Silver Coins, Art Objects). Because there is often no proof of registration or ownership of these assets, their transfer to your Revocable Trust should be handled in a manner similar to that described for unregistered securities. Any new tangible investments of this type should be purchased directly in the name of the Trustee, as Trustee with the confirmation or other purchase slip retained with the instrument or investment to prove the Trustee's ownership.Motor Vehicles and Mobile Homes. Motor Vehicles and Mobile Homes should be transferred to the Trust unless it has very little value, or unless you plan to dispose of it imminently. The state Department of Licensing handles the transfer of vehicle and mobile home title transfers. Be prepared to present the current title certificate(s) and Certification of Trust to effectuate the transfer.Partnership Interests (Whether in a General Partnership or Limited Partnership). In order to register these partnership interests in the Trustee's name, a new certificate of partnership interest, assignment, or an amendment to the partnership agreement may need to be executed. There may be restrictions against transfer in the partnership agreement, so it should be reviewed before the transfer is attempted.Limited Liability Company (LLC). An LLC Operating Agreement may include provisions for disposition of membership interest upon the death of a member. A membership interest in an LLC may be transferred into the name of the Trust, if appropriate, which requires either amending the LLC Operating Agreement or an assignment of the membership interest if permitted by the LLC Operating Agreement.Real Property (Personal Residence, Investment Real Estate, and Mineral and Royalty Interests). Legal title must be transferred to the Trustee by means of an executed, notarized, and recorded deed, usually a Quitclaim Deed. Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer and whether the proposed transfer will trigger any "due on sale" clause in the mortgage or deed of trust. It is important to comply carefully with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located. You may wish to consult with a title agent to ensure that the property interest is eligible for transfer.Life Insurance Policies, Annuities, and Retirement Plan Benefit. Ownership of these assets generally may not be transferred to a Revocable Trust, but the death benefits can be made payable to the Trustee for distribution under the terms of the trust. Life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate unless all designated beneficiaries predecease the policyholder. You may wish to transfer ownership to your Revocable Trust to give the Trustee more control over the policies in case your designated beneficiaries predecease you or you become incapacitated.Ownership of IRAs and 401ks should not be transferred to the trust. Change-of-beneficiary forms from the insurance company or the administrator of the retirement plan must be completed and recorded with the insurer or the plan Trustee to name the Trustee of your Revocable Trust as the beneficiary of any benefits. Some annuities do not permit transfers, and others have negative tax consequences if put into the Revocable Trust, so caution is advised.If a bank, financial institution, or other governing body requires evidence of the Revocable Trust to complete the transfer, use the Certification of Trust rather than producing a copy of the entire trust agreement. The Certification of Trust provides only the necessary information without disclosing confidential details.Once an asset has been transferred to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust, all transactions regarding that asset must be handled by the Trustee, rather than by you individually and/or jointly (even if you are the initial Trustees of the Revocable Trust). For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the Trustee, it is the Trustee who has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting. Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the Trustee.Additional assets may be transferred to your Revocable Trust either by registering them in the name of the Trustee, by delivering them to the Trustee with appropriate documents to re-register the assets in the name of the Trustee, or with other transfer documents if the asset is of the nonregistered type. Withdrawals of property from your Revocable Trust must comply with the provisions of the Trust Agreement. I can prepare documents for you to accomplish such withdrawal. The Trust Agreement can be amended or revoked in its entirety, but any amendment or revocation must comply with the procedural requirements of the Trust Agreement.Federal income tax returns for a Revocable Trust are not required. If the Trustors are also the Trustee or a co-Trustee, a federal tax identification number for the trust is also not necessary, and all income is reported using the Trustors' social security numbers (which becomes the Revocable Trust's taxpayer identification number). If the Trustors are not Trustees, the Trustors' social security numbers can still be used, but the Trustee must meet certain other requirements in that case. After the Trustors' death, a federal tax identification number must be obtained for the Trust.Please call me if you have any questions about the transfer of property to your trust or about the operation of the trust in the future. This memorandum is for your general guidance only and does not encompass all possible situations.
Sincerely yours,____________________[name of attorney]End of Document
