Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 405(a).• Pet trusts. A trust for the care of an animal may be created under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 408.• Noncharitable purpose trust. A settlor may create a trust for any legal noncharitable purpose under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 409. For further discussion of Purpose Trusts, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (MA) under this subtopic.Trustees and Trust AdministrationTrusteesSelecting a TrusteeThe choice of a trustee or trustees is a critical decision. Even when a trust has very restrictive provisions, the trustee still has a great deal of discretion and responsibility. See Mass Gen. Laws ch. 203E, §§ 815, 816.A trustee must administer the trust as a prudent person would considering the purposes, terms, and other circumstances of the trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 804. A trustee must be someone who both understands the duties of a trustee and can carry out those duties. Thus, it is important to select a person or entity who can capably administer the trust and manage its assets. If there is no friend or family member capable of and interested in administering the trust, a professional trustee may be required, although doing so may entail additional cost.A trustee should have the confidence of the beneficiaries, and be fair minded, organized, and able to maintain records. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, §§ 803, 810.Ideally, the trustee should also be someone who does not have a financial interest in the trust, to avoid potential conflicts of interest. As noted previously, the same person cannot be both the sole trustee and the sole beneficiary. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 402(a).The location of the trustee may also be a factor to consider. A trustee is required to take reasonable steps to take control of and protect the trust property. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 809. If the trust assets include real estate, a trustee who is local to the property will be able to check on the property and may be more familiar with local laws.Finally, a trustee may have to make unpopular decisions. Unhappy beneficiaries may question the trustee's judgment. The trustee must be someone who will be able to handle the potential litigation and legal liability resulting from his or her decisions and management of the trust.AppointmentThe trust instrument should name a specific person or persons (or entity) to be trustee. However, no one can be compelled to act as a trustee. A person who has been designated as a trustee must accept or deny the trusteeship. Acceptance is achieved by substantially complying with a method of acceptance provided in the terms of the trust or, if the terms of the trust do not provide a method or the method provided in the terms is not expressly made exclusive, by accepting delivery of the trust property, exercising powers or performing duties as trustee, or otherwise indicating acceptance of the trusteeship. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 701(a).If a designated trustee does not accept the trusteeship within a reasonable time after knowing of the designation, he or she will be deemed to have rejected the trusteeship. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 701(b).SuccessionThe trust instrument should not only name a trustee, but it should also name a successor trustee or provide a process for naming one. See Mass Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 704. Naming a successor trustee is important because the original trustee(s) can unexpectedly become incapacitated or die. Furthermore, in some instances, the expected trust duration may be longer than the trustee's life span. For example, for a trust such as a dynasty trust, which is intended to last for multiple generations, the choice of trustees and the trustee succession provisions are particularly important to consider.If one or more co-trustees remain in office, a vacancy in a trusteeship need not be filled. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 704(b). If there are no remaining trustees to fill a vacancy in a trusteeship, then the vacancy will be filled in the following order of priority:• A person designated by the terms of the trust to act as successor trustee• A person appointed by unanimous agreement of the qualified beneficiaries• A person appointed by the courtMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 704(c).Trust AdministrationDuties and Powers GenerallyThe trustee must administer the trust in good faith. Mass Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 801. The trustee has many duties in administering the trust, including a duty of loyalty and a duty of impartiality. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, §§ 802–803. The trustee also has a duty to administer the estate as a prudent investor would, exercising exercise reasonable care, skill, and caution. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 804.The trust instrument may and should set for the scope of the trustee's duties and powers. In the absence of such provisions, though, the trustee has the duties and powers set forth in the MUTC.Fiduciary Duty GenerallyOne of the unique characteristics of a trust is the bifurcation of ownership. The trustee has legal title to the property, but the beneficiaries have an equitable interest in the property. On acceptance of a trusteeship, the trustee must administer the trust in good faith, in accordance with its terms and purposes and the interests of the beneficiaries, and in accordance with the provisions of the Massachusetts Uniform Trust Code (MUTC). Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 801; see Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 802(a) (trustee must administer trust solely in interests of beneficiaries). Furthermore, under the Massachusetts Prudent Investor Act, a trustee must invest and manage the trust assets solely in the interest of the beneficiaries. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 6.For more information about the characteristics of a trust, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (MA).Duty of LoyaltyOne of the trustee's most important duties is to administer the trust solely in the interests of the beneficiaries. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 802(a). While the trustee is entitled to reasonable compensation for his or her services in administering the trust, the trustee cannot act in his or her own interests. For example, a sale, encumbrance, or other transaction involving trust property entered into by the trustee for his or her own personal account or affected by a conflict between the trustee's fiduciary and personal interests is voidable except in certain circumstances. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 802(b)–(c). Even a transaction involving non-trust property may involve a conflict between the trustee's personal and fiduciary interests if the transaction involves an opportunity properly belonging to the trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 802(d). However, it is not considered self-dealing if a trustee makes an investment in securities of an investment company to which the trustee, or its affiliates, provide services in a capacity other than a trustee, as long as the investment complies with the prudent investor rule. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 802(e).ImpartialityA trustee must act impartially or fairly to all beneficiaries. If a trust has two or more beneficiaries, the trustee must act impartially in investing, managing, and distributing the trust property, giving due regard to the beneficiaries' respective interests. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 803.Fairly does not mean equally because different beneficiaries will have different needs. In investing, protecting, and distributing the trust estate, a trustee must act impartially and with due regard for the diverse beneficial interests created by the terms of the trust. Middlesex Sav. Bank v. Cooper, 26 Mass. L. Rep. 243, 2009 Mass. Super. LEXIS 277 (Mass. Super. Ct. Worcester Sept. 21, 2009). The duty of impartiality applies to successor trustees as well. Harrison v. Marcus, 396 Mass. 424, 486 N.E.2d 710 (1985).Prudent Administration and InvestmentThe prudent trustee rule exists in Massachusetts through common law and through the enactment of the Massachusetts Uniform Trust Code and the Massachusetts Prudent Investor Act. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 801 et seq. (MUTC); Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 1 et seq. (Prudent Investor Act); see also Chase v. Pevear, 383 Mass. 350, 362, 419 N.E.2d 1358 (1981) (common law investing with prudence). A trustee must administer the trust as a prudent person would, considering the purposes, terms, and other circumstances of the trust. In doing so, the trustee must exercise reasonable care, skill, and caution. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 804. In addition, in administering a trust, the trustee may incur only costs that are appropriate and reasonable in relation to the trust property, the purposes of the trust, and the skills of the trustee. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 805.A trustee who invests and manages trust assets owes a duty to the beneficiaries of a trust to comply with the prudent investor rule. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 2(a). However, the prudent investor rule may be expanded, restricted, eliminated, or otherwise altered by the provisions of a trust. In addition, a trustee is not liable to a beneficiary to the extent that the trustee acted in reasonable reliance on the provisions of the trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 2(b).The prudent investor standard requires a trustee to invest and manage trust assets as a prudent investor would, considering the purposes, terms, and other circumstances of the trust. In satisfying this standard, the trustee must exercise reasonable care, skill, and caution. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 3(a). The trustee's investment and management decisions regarding individual assets will be considered in the context of the trust portfolio as a part of an overall investment strategy reasonably suited to the trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 3(b).Among circumstances that a trustee must consider in investing and managing trust assets are the following, to the extent relevant to the trust or its beneficiaries:• General economic conditions• Possible effect of inflation or deflation• Expected tax consequences of investment decisions or strategies• Role that each investment or course of action plays within the overall trust portfolio• Expected total return from income and the appreciation of capital• Other resources of the beneficiaries• Needs for liquidity, regularity of income, and preservation or appreciation of capital• Asset's special relationship or special value, if any, to the purposes of the trust or to one of the beneficiariesMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 3(c).A trustee may invest in any kind of property or type of investment consistent with the standards set forth in the Prudent Investor Act. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 3(e). A trustee must reasonably diversify the investments of the trust unless, under the circumstances, it is prudent not to do so. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 4.A trustee must make a reasonable effort to verify facts relevant to the investment and management of trust assets. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 3(d). A trustee who has special skills or expertise, or is named trustee in reliance on the trustee's representation that the trustee has such special skills or expertise, has a duty to use such special skills or expertise. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 3(f); Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 806.A trustee is permitted to consult with accountants and financial advisers. After obtaining advice or consultation, the trustee may take the information or suggestions into account, but then must exercise independent, prudent, and impartial fiduciary judgment on the matters involved. The Woodward School for Girls, Inc. v. City of Quincy, 469 Mass. 151, 13 N.E.3d 579 (2014). However, as noted below, a trustee is also permitted to delegate investment and management functions if it is prudent to do so. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 10.Compliance with the prudent investor rule is determined in light of the facts and circumstances existing at the time of a trustee's decision or action. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 9.Duty to Keep Beneficiaries InformedThe trustee has a duty to keep the qualified beneficiaries of the trust reasonably informed about the administration of the trust. In addition, a trustee should promptly respond to a qualified beneficiary's request for information related to the administration of the trust, unless the request is unreasonable under the circumstances. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 813(a).The trustee must inform the qualified beneficiaries, in writing, of his or her name and address within 30 days after acceptance of the trust or the trust becomes irrevocable, whichever is later. The information should be delivered or sent by ordinary first-class mail. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 813(b).The trustee has the duty to send at least an annual account of trust income and principal to the distributees and other qualified beneficiaries who request it. A report should also be sent at the termination of the trust. While the account can be formal or informal, it should include information relating to the trust property liabilities, receipts, and disbursements, including the amount of the trustee's compensation. In addition, the account should include a listing of the trust assets, and the market value of those assets, if feasible. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 813(c).A beneficiary may waive the right to a trustee's account of trust income or principal or other information otherwise required to be furnished under § 813. This waiver can be withdrawn. A waiver does not relieve the trustee from accountability and potential liability for matters that the account or other information would have disclosed. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 813(d).Note that some states allow for a silent or quiet trust. Some settlors do not want beneficiaries to get accountings because they do not want the beneficiary to know about the amount of money they are to receive in trust because that knowledge might dampen the beneficiary's work ethic. However, Massachusetts does not currently allow for such a trust.Duty to Furnish BondIn the case of a testamentary trust, a trustee must furnish a bond for the performance of the trustee's fiduciary duties and a surety. However, this requirement can be waived by the terms of the trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 702. For inter vivos trusts, there is no bond requirement unless the terms of the trust require one.Other Responsibilities of TrusteeWhile the needs of every trust are different, there are a few basic statutory responsibilities that generally apply to all trustees.Control and Protection of Trust PropertyA trustee must take reasonable steps to take control of and protect the trust property. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 809.Recordkeeping and Identification of Trust PropertyA trustee must keep adequate records of the administration of the trust. A trustee must keep trust property separate from the trustee's own property. A trustee may, however, invest the property of two or more separate trusts as a whole, if the trustee maintains records clearly indicating the respective interests of each trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 810.Enforcement and Defense of ClaimsA trustee must take reasonable steps to enforce claims of the trust and to defend claims against the trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 811.Collecting Trust PropertyA trustee must take reasonable steps to compel a former trustee or other person to deliver trust property to the trustee and to redress a breach of trust known to the trustee to have been committed by a former trustee. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 812.Exercise of Discretionary PowersA trustee must exercise a discretionary power in good faith and in accordance with the terms and purposes of the trust and the interests of the beneficiaries. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 814(a).Distributions to BeneficiariesThe trustee must of course distribute income and/or principal to the trust beneficiaries in accordance with the terms of the trust. In the absence of a spendthrift provision, those distributions may be subject to attachment by creditors. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 501.To the extent that the trust confers on the trustee a power to make discretionary distributions, the following rules apply unless the trust terms expressly provide otherwise:• A person other than a settlor who is a beneficiary and trustee of a trust that confers on the trustee a power to make discretionary distributions to or for the trustee's personal benefit may exercise the power only in accordance with an ascertainable standard.• A trustee may not exercise a power to make discretionary distributions to satisfy a legal obligation of support that the trustee personally owes another person.Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 814(b).The rules set forth in § 814(b) do not apply, however, to:• A power held by the settlor's spouse who is the trustee of a trust for which a marital deduction was previously allowed –or–• Any trust during any period that the trust may be revoked or amended by its settlorMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 814(d). In addition, a power that is limited or prohibited by § 814(b) may be exercised by a majority of the remaining trustees whose exercise of the power is not so limited or prohibited. If the power of all trustees is so limited or prohibited, the court may appoint a special fiduciary with authority to exercise the power. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 814(c).On the occurrence of an event terminating or partially terminating a trust, the trustee must proceed expeditiously to distribute the trust property to the persons entitled to it, subject to the right of the trustee to retain a reasonable reserve for the payment of debts, expenses, and taxes. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 817(b). Prior to distributing the trust property on termination of the trust, the trustee may send to the beneficiaries a proposal for distribution. The right of any beneficiary to object to the proposed distribution shall terminate if the beneficiary does not notify the trustee of an objection within 30 days after the proposal was sent, but only if the proposal (1) informed the beneficiary of the right to object and of the time allowed for objection, and (2) provided the beneficiary with sufficient material facts to enable the beneficiary to evaluate the proposal. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 817(a).Powers of TrusteeGenerally, the powers of the trustee are specified by the terms of the trust and may be exercised by the trustee without court authorization. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 815(a)(1). In addition, except as limited by the terms of the trust, the trustee may, without court authorization, exercise:• All powers over the trust property that an unmarried competent owner has over individually owned property• Any other powers appropriate to achieve the proper investment, management, and distribution of the trust property –and–• Any other powers conferred by the MUTCMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 815(a)(2).In addition to the general powers set forth above and those set forth in the trust, the MUTC lists a number of specific powers that trustees may exercise, subject to any limits set forth in the trust. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 816.The trustee's exercise of any power is subject to the fiduciary duties prescribed by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 801 et seq., as discussed above. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 815(b).Delegation of FunctionsUnder the Prudent Investor Act, a trustee may delegate investment and management functions to an agent if it is prudent to do so. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 10(a). In doing so, the trustee must exercise reasonable care, skill, and caution in:• Selecting an agent• Establishing the scope and terms of the delegation, consistent with the purposes and terms of the trust –and–• Periodically reviewing the agent's actions in order to monitor the agent's performance and compliance with the terms of the delegationMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 10(a).A trustee who complies with the above requirements is not liable to the beneficiaries or to the trust for the decisions or actions of the agent to whom the function was delegated. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 10(c).In performing a delegated function, an agent owes a duty to the trust to exercise reasonable care to comply with the terms of the delegation. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 10(b). By accepting the delegation of trust functions from the trustee of a trust that is subject to the laws of the Commonwealth of Massachusetts, an agent submits to the jurisdiction of the Massachusetts courts. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203C, § 10(d).Liability of TrusteeLiability to BeneficiaryA violation by a trustee of a duty the trustee owes to a beneficiary is a breach of trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1001(a). To remedy a breach of trust that has occurred or may occur, the court may:• Compel the trustee to perform the trustee's duties• Enjoin the trustee from committing a breach of trust• Compel the trustee to redress a breach of trust by paying money, restoring property, or other means• Order a trustee to account• Appoint a special fiduciary to take possession of the trust property and administer the trust• Suspend the trustee• Remove the trustee• Reduce or deny compensation to the trustee• Subject to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1012 (protection of third parties dealing with trustee), void an act of the trustee, impose a lien or a constructive trust on trust property, or trace trust property wrongfully disposed of and recover the property or its proceeds• Order any other appropriate reliefMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1001(b).There are several situations where the trustee will not be held liable. First, a trustee who acts in reasonable reliance on the terms of the trust as expressed in the trust instrument is not liable to a beneficiary for a breach of trust to the extent the breach resulted from the reliance. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1006.Second, a trustee will not be held liable for a breach of trust if the beneficiary while having capacity, in writing, consented to the conduct constituting the breach, released the trustee from liability for the breach, or ratified the transaction constituting the breach. However, a release, consent, or ratification will not relieve the trustee of liability if (1) it was obtained by improper conduct by the trustee; or (2) at the time of the release, consent, or ratification, the beneficiary did not know of the material facts relating to the breach of trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1009.In addition, if the happening of an event or change of status, including, but not limited to, birth, adoption, marriage, divorce, performance of educational requirements, or death, affects the administration or distribution of a trust, a trustee who has exercised reasonable care to ascertain the happening of the event or change of status is not liable for a loss resulting from the trustee's lack of knowledge. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1007.A term of a trust relieving a trustee of liability for breach of trust is unenforceable to the extent that it:• Relieves the trustee of liability for breach of trust committed in bad faith or with reckless indifference to the purposes of the trust or the interests of the beneficiaries –or–• Was inserted as the result of an abuse by the trustee of a fiduciary or confidential relationship to the settlorMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1008(a).In addition, an exculpatory term drafted or caused to be drafted by the trustee may be invalid as an abuse of a fiduciary or confidential relationship unless the trustee proves that its existence and contents were adequately communicated to the settlor. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1008(b).Note that once a beneficiary has received a final account or other statement fully disclosing the matter and showing termination of the trust relationship between the trustee and the beneficiary, any claim against a trustee for breach of trust will be barred unless the claim was commenced within six months after receipt of the final account or statement. Furthermore, a claim for breach of trust will be barred, notwithstanding the lack of full disclosure, if the beneficiary fails to commence the claim within three years after the beneficiary was informed by the trustee of the location and availability of the final records for examination. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1005(a). In addition, for claims not barred by § 1005(a), a beneficiary may not commence a proceeding against a trustee for breach of trust more than three years after the date the beneficiary or a representative of the beneficiary knew or reasonably should have known of the existence of a potential claim for breach of trust. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1005(b). Finally, if § 1005(a) and (b) do not apply, a judicial proceeding against a trustee for breach of trust must be commenced within five years after the first of the following to occur:• The removal, resignation, or death of the trustee• The termination of the beneficiary's interest in the trust• The termination of the trustMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1005(c).Liability to Third PartiesExcept as otherwise provided in the contract, a trustee is not personally liable on a contract properly entered into in the trustee's fiduciary capacity in the course of administering the trust if the trustee, in the contract, disclosed the fiduciary capacity. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1010(a).A trustee is personally liable for torts committed in the course of administering a trust or for obligations arising from ownership or control of trust property, including liability for violation of environmental law, only if the trustee is personally at fault. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1010(b).A claim based on a contract entered into by a trustee in the trustee's fiduciary capacity, on an obligation arising from ownership or control of trust property, or on a tort committed in the course of administering a trust, may be asserted in a judicial proceeding against the trustee in the trustee's fiduciary capacity, whether or not the trustee is personally liable for the claim. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1010(c).Liability of a trustee who is a general partner in a partnership owned by the trust is governed by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1011.Compensation of TrusteeThe terms of the trust should specify the trustee's compensation. However, the court may allow more or less compensation than that provided in the trust instrument if:• The duties of the trustee are substantially different from those contemplated when the trust was created.• The compensation specified by the terms of the trust would be unreasonably low or high.Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 708(b).If the terms of the trust do not specify the trustee's compensation, a trustee is entitled to compensation that is reasonable under the circumstances. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 708(a).In addition to compensation, the trustee is also entitled to be reimbursed out of the trust property, with interest as appropriate, for expenses that were:• Properly incurred in the administration of the trust –or–• Not properly incurred in the administration of the trust, to the extent necessary to prevent unjust enrichment of the trustMass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 709(a).Note that an advance by the trustee of money for the protection of the trust creates a lien against trust property to secure reimbursement with reasonable interest. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 709(b).Resignation or Removal of TrusteeAt any point in the existence of a trust, a trustee may resign. However, in the case of a revocable trust, the trustee must give 30 days' notice to the settlor and all co-trustees. In the case of any other trust, the trustee must give 30 days' notice to the qualified beneficiaries and all co-trustees of the trust. The trustee can also ask the court for approval. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 705(a). In approving a resignation, the court may issue orders and impose conditions reasonably necessary for the protection of the trust property. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 705(b). Any liability of a resigning trustee or of any sureties on the trustee's bond for acts or omissions of the trustee are not discharged or affected by the trustee's resignation. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 705(c).The trustee can also be removed. The settlor, a co-trustee, or a beneficiary may request the court to remove a trustee, or a trustee may be removed by the court on its own initiative. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 706(a). The court may remove a trustee if:• The trustee committed a serious breach of trust.• A lack of cooperation among co-trustees substantially impairs the administration of the trust.• The trustee is unfit or unwilling, or persistently fails, to administer the trust effectively, and the court determines that removal of the trustee best serves the interests of the beneficiaries.• There has been a substantial change of circumstances or removal is requested by all of the qualified beneficiaries, the court finds that removal of the trustee best serves the interests of all of the beneficiaries and is not inconsistent with a material purpose of the trust, and a suitable co-trustee or successor trustee is available.Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 706(b).Pending a final decision on a request to remove a trustee, or in lieu of or in addition to removing a trustee, the court may order such appropriate relief under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 1001(b) (discussed above under Liability of Trustee) as may be necessary to protect the trust property or the interests of the beneficiaries. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 706(c).A trustee who has resigned or been removed must expeditiously deliver any trust property within his or her possession to the co-trustee, successor trustee, or other person entitled to it. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 707.Rules of ConstructionIn interpreting a trust, the intent of the settlor is paramount. See Hillman v. Hillman, 433 Mass. 590, 593, 744 N.E.2d 1078 (2001); Walker v. Walker, 433 Mass. 581, 587, 744 N.E.2d 60 (2001). In determining the settlor's intention with respect to the trustee's powers, the courts regard as particularly significant the language used by the donor viewed in light of the rule of law in effect at the time the powers in question were created. Morse v. Kraft, 466 Mass. 92, 98, 992 N.E.2d 1021 (2013). The Massachusetts courts ''tend to disfavor'' interpretations that would resolve ambiguities by attributing to the settlor ''an intention which as a practical matter is likely to benefit the taxing authorities and no one else." Morse v. Kraft, 466 Mass. 92, 98, 992 N.E.2d 1021 (2013), citing Hillman v. Hillman, 433 Mass. 590, 593, 744 N.E.2d 1078 (2001).Under MUTC, the rules of construction that apply to wills also apply to the interpretation of revocable trusts and the disposition of trust property. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 112; see Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 190B, § 2-601 et seq. (rules of construction applicable to wills); see also Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 190B, § 2-701 et seq. (rules of construction applicable to wills and donative dispositions in other governing instruments). For purposes of § 112, a revocable trust means a trust that is (1) revocable by the settlor until the time of the settlor's death; (2) created or amended by the settlor after the effective date of the MUTC chapter; and (3) intended to dispose of the settlor's property at death, whether under will or otherwise and whether or not the trust was funded at the time of the settlor's death. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 203E, § 112.To avoid the possibility of courts applying default rules of interpretation that may differ from the client's actual intent, counsel should carefully draft the trust language to reflect the client's intent as clearly as possible.Time Limitations on Trust DurationThe rule against perpetuities was developed to prevent transferors from limiting the transferability of property beyond a specified period and thus avoiding dynastic property and trusts. In the context of trusts, the rule is implicated when the trust terms create future interests that may not vest within 21 years after the death of a life in being at the time the interest was created. The rule originated in the common law, but Massachusetts has adopted a statutory rule against perpetuities. See Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 190B, § 2-901.Related ContentResource Kits• Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (MA)Current as of: 07/01/2025End of Document

Requirements and Restrictions on Trust Purposes and Administration (IL)
Go to: Formal Requirements   |   Trustees and Trust Administration   |   Related ContentMaintainedby William Dyrcz, The Law Offices of William Dyrcz (updated by Amina Saeed, River Valley Law Firm)This practice note discusses formal trust requirements and restrictions for an Illinois client and also includes topics such as appointment of trustees and successor trustees; and trust administration, including trustee duties, liabilities, and compensation, in Illinois. For the purposes of this practice note, a trust is an estate planning tool that can be used to accomplish basic and sophisticated wealth preservation and succession planning.Effective January 1, 2020, the Illinois Trust and Trustee's Act 760 ILCS 5/1 et seq. is repealed. A new Illinois Trust Code, 760 ILCS 3/101 et seq. (the Code) is added to the statutes. While the Act applies to trusts created after January 1, 2020, there are some retroactive provisions. 760 ILCS 3/813.2(b), (c).For a full listing of key content on creating an estate plan for an individual with a spouse or partner residing in Illinois, see Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (IL).For more background information on trusts, including characteristics of a trust and the reasons an individual might want to create one, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (IL). For information on trust terminations and modifications, see Revocation, Amendment, and Termination of Trusts (IL).Formal RequirementsIntentIt is axiomatic that a settlor cannot create an express trust without the intent to do so. Given the general requirement for trusts to be in writing (see exception discussed below), it is impossible, or nearly so, to contrive of a fact pattern where a settlor is alleged to have created an express trust by implication. When construing a trust, Illinois courts apply the same rules that apply to the construction of wills. See Harris Trust and Savings Bank v. Donovan, 145 Ill. 2d 166, 163 Ill. Dec. 854, 582 N.E. 2d 120, 123 (1991).Identifiable PropertyAn analogy to having a well drafted but unfunded trust would be similar to purchasing a new luxury car. Until the owner puts gas in, it will only sit in the driveway. An old common law rule is that for a valid trust to exist, it must hold assets (i.e., there must be a trust res or property delivered to the trustee). However, unfunded revocable trusts are increasingly common. How are these two concepts reconciled? Put simply, the unfunded revocable trust, or for that matter an unfunded irrevocable trust, is not yet a valid trust; it only becomes a valid trust upon funding. Could it be problematic to have a not-yet-valid trust? There is some concern that a trust that is not yet valid could not be effectively named as a beneficiary under a pour-over will. To be safe, it has been common practice to recite in the schedule of trust assets some nominal amount of money (e.g., $10) to satisfy the requirement for a res. In practice, the res requirement is highly unlikely to be an issue, but if a contest is anticipated, steps may be taken to reduce the risk of contest by funding the trust to a more-than-nominal degree promptly after creation.Ascertainable BeneficiariesThe Code defines beneficiaries as having a "present or future beneficial interest… vested or contingent, assuming nonexercise of powers of appointment." 760 ILC 3/103(A)(3). The term also includes nontrustees who have a power of appointment over trust property. 760 ILCS 3/103(3)(B). The Code defines a current beneficiary as a recipient or potential recipient of trust income or principal and also includes charities, as well as those with a power of appointment over trust property. 760 ILCS 3/103(9). The Code also defines "qualified beneficiaries" as current beneficiaries, excluding charities, those with a power of appointment, and all presumptive remainder beneficiaries. 760 ILCS 3/103(30).Methods of Creating a TrustA trust may be created by:• Transfer of property to another person as trustee during the settlor's lifetime or by will or other disposition taking effect upon the settlor's death• Declaration by the owner of property that the owner holds identifiable property as trustee –or–• Exercise of a power of appointment in favor of a trustee760 ILCS 3/401Creation RequirementsA trust is created only if:• The settlor has capacity to create a trust• The settlor indicates an intention to create the trust• The trust has a definite beneficiary or is: (a) a charitable trust; (b) a trust for the care of an animal as provided in Section 408 of the Code, or a trust as provided in Section 409 of the Code• The trustee has duties to perform –and–• The same person is not the sole trustee and sole beneficiary760 ILCS 3/402A trust need not be in writing; however, to prove that an oral trust exists, it must be established by clear and convincing evidence. 760 ILCS 3/407.Trustees and Trust AdministrationTrusteesAppointmentIn general, any individual, or a corporate trustee such as a bank or trust company, may serve as trustee. The selection of a trustee depends on a number of factors, including the settlor's preference; the time commitment that will be required of a prospective trustee; conflicts that may exist between a potential trustee and one or more beneficiaries of the trust; and the size, complexity, and potential risk of the assets of the trust.While the settlor may desire an independent trustee (e.g., a corporate trustee), the prospective trustee may be reluctant to serve, for example, where the trust assets consist of real estate holdings which have not been evaluated for potential risks (e.g., environmental hazards) and such an undertaking would be cost prohibitive. More often, the size of the trust estate may not justify the fee structure of an institutional trustee. If the trust instrument nominates as trustee a bank that no longer exists, no longer accepts trust business, or refuses to administer the particular trust, and no other mechanism of filling the vacancy in the office of trustee is provided, the only solution may be to seek court appointment of a trustee. To avoid this scenario, the trust instrument should either appoint a successor trustee or provide a means for the last trustee, beneficiaries, or another third party to appoint a new trustee.Notably, designating a trustee does not compel that person or entity to serve as such. The trustee must voluntarily qualify (i.e., formally accept appointment) before actually commencing to serve. The initial trustees of an inter vivos trust qualify by signing the trust instrument. Successor trustees of an inter vivos trust accept by signing the instrument under which they are appointed, or they sign a separate instrument drafted for that specific purpose (i.e., an acceptance of trust). In all cases, proper documentation is essential to forestall any claims that a trustee is acting without due authority. Best practices further suggest preparing documentation for the acceptance of trusteeship under testamentary trusts, including any sub-trusts that may spring into existence under the trust instrument after the death of the settlor.SuccessionIf a trust instrument names a successor trustee, or provides for a process of naming a successor trustee, there is generally no problem. However, even where a successor is not named in the trust instrument, Illinois law contains a flexible mechanism for the appointment of successor trustees to permit the beneficiaries to avoid court proceedings in many cases where a successor trustee is necessary. For the order and procedure for successor trustees, see 760 ILCS 3/704. Regardless, when drafting an Illinois trust, it is recommended that estate planners incorporate multiple layers of trustee succession provisions. To be thorough, the practitioner may empower the grantor to name successors; next authorize the grantor's spouse to designate successors; then nominate a series of successor trustees, in order of priority listed, to serve as trustee; provide that the last acting trustee may appoint a further successor; allow a majority of the adult beneficiaries of the trust to fill any vacancy; and finally, as a last resort, identify a corporate trustee. While estate planners do not typically provide for all of these fallback options, some subset of them is advisable. One cannot force a person to serve as an agent so a provision for resignation by a trustee is important as well. See 760 ILCS 3/705 for the resignation of a trustee.Trust AdministrationPowers and Duties of a TrusteeIllinois leaves open the powers that a trustee has in administering a trust. The statute does specifically list various duties of the trustee. A person establishing a trust may specify in the instrument the rights, powers, duties, limitations, and immunities applicable to the trustee, beneficiary, and others and those provisions where not otherwise contrary to law shall control, notwithstanding the rights, powers, duties, limitations, and immunities provided in the statute. Even when a duty is imposed by statute on a trustee, the settlor may expand, restrict, eliminate, or otherwise alter the duty by express provisions of the trust instrument, and the trustee is not liable to a beneficiary for the trustee's reasonable and good faith reliance on those express provisions. 760 ILCS 3/1006.  A trustee shall give bond to secure performance of the trustee's duties only if the court finds that a bond is needed to protect the interests of the beneficiaries or is required by the terms of the trust and the court has not dispensed with the requirement. See 760 ILCS  3/702(a). The court may specify the amount of a bond, its liabilities, and whether sureties are necessary. Also, the court may modify or terminate a bond at any time. A corporate fiduciary is qualified to do trust business, need not give bond, even if required by the terms of the trust. 205 ILCS 620/1-5.05.Illinois does go into great detail regarding investment management, delegation of investment decisions, and specific statutory powers that a trustee may exercise unless prohibited by the settlor's directives.Prudent Investor RuleA trustee administering a trust has a duty to invest and manage the trust assets as follows:The trustee has a duty to invest and manage trust assets as a prudent investor would considering the purposes, terms, distribution requirements, and other circumstances of the trust. This standard requires the exercise of reasonable care, skill, and caution and is to be applied to investments not in isolation, but in the context of the trust portfolio as a whole and as a part of an overall investment strategy that should incorporate risk and return objectives reasonably suitable to the trust.760 ILCS 3/901 et seq.While the statute above states what a trustee must do, a settlor may not wish to name a bank or trust company to handle the management and distributions of trust assets either due to the impersonal nature of a trust company, the cost financial institutions charge for trust services, or because they prefer to have a family member handle the family's financial matters. An individual that a settlor wishes to name as a trustee may not feel comfortable picking stocks, bonds, and other investments for family beneficiaries and may wish to seek advice in managing other people's wealth and still serve as trustee for the family. Illinois provides a statutory method for an individual trustee to obtain financial advice and continue on as the trustee free from personal liability to the beneficiaries.Duty Not to DelegateA trustee has a duty not to delegate to others the performance of any acts involving the exercise of judgment and discretion, except acts constituting investment functions that a prudent investor of comparable skills might delegate under the circumstances. The trustee may delegate those investment functions to an investment agent. See 760 ILCS 3/807(a)-(b). In order for the trustee to delegate the investment functions:• The trustee must exercise reasonable care and skill.• The trustee must conduct an inquiry into the experience, performance history, professional licensing or registration, if any, and financial stability of the investment agent.• The investment agent shall be subject to the jurisdiction of the courts of the state of Illinois.• The investment agent shall be subject to the same standards that are applicable to the trustee.• The investment agent shall be liable to the beneficiaries of the trust and to the designated trustee to the same extent as if the investment agent were a designated trustee in relation to the exercise or nonexercise of the investment function.• The trustee shall send written notice of its intention to begin delegating investment functions to the beneficiaries eligible to receive income from the trust on the date of initial delegation at least 30 days before the delegation.If all these requirements are satisfied, the trustee shall not be responsible for the investment decisions of the investment agent. See 760 ILCS 3/808(f).Trustee PowersIllinois law grants the trust creator (settlor, grantor, trustor, trustmaker) a broad range of terms that may be included or excluded in a trust. A person establishing a trust may specify in the instrument the rights, powers, duties, limitations, and immunities applicable to the trustee, beneficiary, and others, and those provisions, where not otherwise contrary to law, shall control. 760 ILCS 3/815(a)(1). For any powers that the trust creator does not specifically state or exclude in the trust document, the statute provides a list of powers (by default) that allows the trustee to act. These powers include but are not limited to:• To sell, contract to sell, or exchange, or all or part of the trust estate at public or private sale, for cash or on credit• To borrow money and mortgage or pledge all or part of the trust estate• To grant easements and subdivide or improve real estate• To appoint a trustee to act in other jurisdictions• To open bank accounts and safe deposit boxes• To exercise voting rights or give proxies on securities• To pay taxes and expenses incurred in administering the trust estate• To appoint attorneys, accountants, and financial advisors and pay reasonable compensation to the appointees• To delegate to a co-trustee for any period of time any or all of the trustee's rights, powers, and duties• To compromise, contest, prosecute, or abandon claims in favor of the trust estate• To sign contracts, notes, conveyances, and other instruments• To receive additional trust property• To invest in or hold undivided interests in property• To deal with the executor, trustee, or other representative of any other trust or estate where the trust is a beneficiary or has an interest in• To make distributions in cash or in kind• To rely on an affidavit, certificate, or letter reasonably believed to be genuine to make payments or distributions in good faith without liability• To have all the rights, powers, and duties as trustee after the trust is terminated and until distributions or pending litigation is completed• To purchase insurance• To distribute income and principal as the trustee believes to be for the best interests of any beneficiary who is under legal disability or in the trustee's opinion is unable to properly manage his or her affairs• To plant and harvest crops and to breed, raise, purchase, and sell livestock, own and manage farm equipment, buildings, and farmland• To drill for or otherwise own oil, gas, or mineral rights• To operate a business proprietorship• To operate a partnership or corporation owned by the trust • To terminate the trust and distribute the trust estate if the trustee determines, in the trustee's sole discretion, with the consent of the recipients, that the value of the trust is less than $100,000 and that the costs of continuing the trust will substantially impair accomplishment of the purpose of the trustSee 760 ILCS 3/816.In addition to general powers, trustees are granted discretionary powers. The statute provides that a beneficiary who is not the settlor, is acting as a trustee, must make distributions to the beneficiary under ascertainable standards and not to creditors of the beneficiary. 760 ILCS 3/814(b). This allows a beneficiary to serve as a trustee and still be able to protect his or her own trust share under a spendthrift clause.Powers and Duties of a Successor TrusteeIn the event of the death, resignation, refusal, or inability to act of any trustee: • The remaining trustee, if any, shall continue to act, with all the rights, powers, and duties, of all of the trustees –or–• If there is no remaining trustee, a successor trustee may be appointed by a majority in interest of the beneficiaries then entitled to receive the income from the trust estate 760 ILCS 3/812.A successor trustee has all the rights, powers, and duties that are granted to or imposed on the prior trustee. A successor trustee is under no duty to inquire into the acts or doings of a predecessor trustee and is not liable for any act or failure to act of a predecessor trustee. With the approval of a majority in interest of the beneficiaries then entitled to receive the benefit of the trust income, a successor trustee may accept the account rendered and the property received as a full and complete discharge to the predecessor trustee without incurring any liability for doing so. See 760 ILCS 3/812.Duty to Keep Beneficiaries InformedIn theory, the duty to keep beneficiaries informed does not seem terribly complex. The trustee ought to send periodic statements to the beneficiaries so they are aware of the trust's assets and the transactions that are occurring in the trust's account; that way, the beneficiaries can fulfill their fundamental role of enforcing the proper administration of the trust. So how does the attorney advise a client who does not want his or her children to know about the millions of dollars being held in trust for their benefit lest they lose all incentive to be productive members of society? The short answer is that, in Illinois, there is not much that a trustee can do to shield this information from the beneficiaries because secrecy would constitute a breach of the trustee's statutory fiduciary duty.Timeliness of accounting to the beneficiaries is important as by statute, beneficiaries, and heirs of beneficiaries have two years to object to the accounting by a trustee. Once a settlor dies, the successor trustee must inform all beneficiaries of the trust within 90 days, the terms and conditions of the trust. 760 ILCS 3/813.1(b). A trustee must provide an annual accounting to certain beneficiaries 760 ILCS 3/813.1(b)(2). It is important that the trustee keep track of the receipts and disbursements from the time he or she takes over the trust until the trust is finally terminated. Every trustee must, at least annually, furnish a current account showing the receipts, disbursements, and inventory of the trust estate to the beneficiaries then entitled to receive or receiving the income from the trust estate. A current account is binding on the beneficiaries receiving the account and on such beneficiaries' heirs and assigns unless an action against the trustee is instituted by the beneficiary or such beneficiary's heirs and assigns within three years from the date the current account is furnished. Every trustee must, on termination of the trust, furnish to the beneficiaries then entitled to distribution of the trust estate a final account for the period from the date of the last current account to the date of distribution showing the inventory of the trust estate, the receipts, disbursements, and distributions and shall make available to such beneficiaries copies of prior accounts not theretofore furnished. See 760 ILCS 3/813.1(b)(4).If a beneficiary is disabled, the trustee must report to the representative of the estate of a beneficiary under legal disability or a spouse, parent, adult child, or guardian of the person of a beneficiary for whose estate no representative has been appointed, may act for the beneficiary in receiving and approving any account of the trustee appointing a successor trustee and executing any receipt and receiving any notice from the trustee. 760 ILCS 3/813.2(d).Compensation of TrusteeTrustees are entitled to be compensated for administering trusts. For Illinois trusts and trustees, the trustee may be reimbursed for all proper expenses incurred in the management and protection of the trust and is entitled to reasonable compensation for services rendered. See 760 ILCS 3/708(a). For individual trustees, one should consider the nature of the work involved including the time, effort, difficulty, and skills required; compensation customarily charged in the community for similar services; the size of the estate and the results obtained by the trustee's efforts; any time limitations on settling the trust estate or the making of distributions; and the experience and capabilities of the trustee. It is standard practice for corporate trustees to charge commissions at their published rate schedule unless there is an agreement to the contrary. A trustee is also allowed reimbursement of advancement of costs and expenses to administer the trust and protect trust property. 760 ILCS 3/709(a).Related ContentResource Kits• Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (IL)Current as of: 07/01/2025End of Document

Letter to Client 
[name of law firm][law firm address][law firm phone number][date][name of client(s)][address]RE: Funding of Revocable TrustDear [name of client(s)]:FUNDING YOUR REVOCABLE TRUSTNow that you have established your Revocable Trust, it must be funded by transferring ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. If you own any assets jointly and undividedly with one or more persons and wish to transfer those assets into your Revocable Trust, all of the joint title owners will have to join in the conveyance.This memorandum provides a general description of the steps necessary to transfer many kinds of assets to your Revocable Trusts. Of course, the specific steps to accomplish this transfer will depend on the nature of the asset involved. If you would like assistance with any of these transfers, please contact my office. I can assist you in the asset transfer process, including the preparation of deeds and obtaining and filing necessary documentation.Please examine your assets to determine which assets to transfer into your Revocable Trust and contact my office if you would like advice about whether to transfer specific assets into the Trust. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property located in any state or country, mineral and royalty interests, tangible personal property, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you may hold. Assets that cannot be transferred to the trust during your lifetime(s) include IRAs, 401ks, and some types of annuities.In general, to transfer assets to your Trusts, you will need to change the title for each asset from your names individually or jointly to the Revocable Trust, as follows:[name of client 1] and [name of client 2] as TRUSTEES of the [name of Revocable Living Trust], dated [date]One of the major goals accomplished by a Revocable Trust as the main instrument in your estate plan is to avoid probate of the trust assets at the time of your deaths. In order to avoid probate, it is critically important that assets be titled in the name of the serving Trustee—as Trustee, not in the Trustee's name individually—of the Revocable Trust and not by you individually and/or jointly, as outlined above. Although your pour-over Will provides that assets held in your individual name at your death will be added to the trust assets and distributed as provided in your Trust Agreement, those assets (except most real estate) will be subject to probate before they are added to the trust property. To avoid that result, you may wish to retitle all assets in the name of your Revocable Trust as outlined above. Please note, however, that transferring assets into your Revocable Trust will not prevent them from being subject to Federal estate tax, if applicable.You should carefully and continually review the assets held in your own names (both assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this is consistent with your estate planning goals. I will be glad to discuss the advisability of retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, holding assets in joint names with another person, or transferring assets to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust. On the following pages, I offer some general guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your Revocable Trust. These comments are general in nature, and if you require specific guidance, please contact my office.Bank Accounts or Certificates Issued by a Bank or Other Savings Institution. The name on the bank account or certificate must be changed to reflect its ownership by the Trust, and a new passbook or certificate must be issued in the name of the Trustee on behalf of the Trust. Be careful to confirm with the financial institution before transferring a savings certificate that there will be no penalty for early withdrawal.Safety Deposit Box. The lessee of a safety deposit box should be changed or acquired in the name of the Trust to allow the acting Trustee access to the safety deposit box. The bank may require new signature cards.Brokerage Accounts. If you own stocks, bonds, or other securities through a brokerage account, the brokerage firm's name and records must be changed to indicate that the Trustee owns the account on behalf of the Trust, and you do not own it individually. This also applies if the certificates for those assets are not issued in your name but are held instead in a street name or in the brokerage firm's name.Corporate Stocks (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations) or Mutual Fund Shares. If certificates are issued in your name(s), both individually or jointly, new certificates must be issued to reflect the Trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. You may be able to combine all certificates representing shares in one corporation into one new certificate, but you may wish to consider requesting a series of new certificates, one corresponding to each old certificate, in order to trace your income tax basis if any shares are sold by the Trustee during your lifetimes. In the case of private stock in a closely held business, there may be restrictions against transfer in the shareholders agreement, so it should be reviewed before the transfer is attempted.Registered Bonds or Debentures (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations, the U.S. Government, an Agency, any State, or Subdivision thereof). These securities must also be registered in the name of the Trustee and new bonds or debentures reissued. As with stock certificates discussed above, if bonds or debentures are held in a brokerage account, title to the account must be changed. U.S. Savings Bonds series EE and series I are reissued electronically using Form 1851 found at treasurydirect.gov/forms/sav1851.pdf.Unregistered or Bearer Bonds or Debentures. These securities, typically issued by the United States government, an agency, a state, or a municipality, generally cannot be registered in the name of the Trustee, so there must be some other evidence that these securities have been transferred. This can be accomplished by executing and delivering to the Trustee an Assignment to Revocable Trust, listing the bearer securities being transferred, which will be retained by the Trustee with the permanent records of the trust. I can prepare for you any documents necessary to accomplish the transfer. It would be helpful to keep bearer securities in a safe deposit box in the name of the Trustee with a duplicate copy of the transfer document attached.Tangible Personal Property including Tangible Investment Assets (Such as Gold Bullion, Silver Coins, Art Objects). Because there is often no proof of registration or ownership of these assets, their transfer to your Revocable Trust should be handled in a manner similar to that described for unregistered securities. An Assignment of Tangible Personal Property to Revocable Trust can be used to transfer all tangible personal property to the Trust. While the assignment may cover current and later acquired tangible personal property, any new tangible investments of this type should be purchased directly in the name of the Trustee, as Trustee with the confirmation or other purchase slip retained with the instrument or investment to prove the Trustee's ownership.Motor Vehicles and Mobile Homes. Motor Vehicles and Mobile Homes could be transferred to the Trust. It is recommended to consider transferring such assets to the Trust unless it has very little value, or unless you plan to dispose of it imminently. The state Department of Motor Vehicles handles the transfer of vehicle and mobile home title transfers. Be prepared to present the current title certificate(s) and Certification of Trust to effectuate the transfer. If the vehicle is subject to a loan, you should check with the lender regarding their requirements for a transfer of the vehicle securing the loan.Partnership Interests (Whether in a General Partnership or Limited Partnership). In order to register these partnership interests in the Trustee's name, a new certificate of partnership interest, assignment, or an amendment to the partnership agreement may need to be executed. There may be restrictions against transfer in the partnership agreement, so it should be reviewed prior to initiating a transfer.Limited Liability Company (LLC). An LLC Operating Agreement may include provisions for disposition of membership interest upon the death of a member. A membership interest in an LLC may be transferred into the name of the Trust, if appropriate, which requires either amending the LLC Operating Agreement or an assignment of the membership interest, or both, if permitted by the LLC Operating Agreement.Real Property (Personal Residence, Investment Real Estate, and Mineral and Royalty Interests). To transfer real property into the Trust, legal title must be transferred to the Trustee by means of an executed, notarized, and recorded deed, usually a Quitclaim Deed. Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer and whether the proposed transfer will trigger any "due on sale" clause in the mortgage or deed of trust. It is important to comply carefully with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located. You may wish to consult with a title agent to ensure that the property interest is eligible for transfer.Please note that title to real property vests immediately upon death in the name of the beneficiary under the will or heir. Therefore, unless such property is needed to pay debts of the estate, the real property does not pass through probate.Life Insurance Policies, Annuities, and Retirement Plan Benefits. Ownership of these assets should not be transferred to a Revocable Trust, but the death benefits can be made payable to the Trustee for distribution under the terms of the trust. Life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate unless all designated beneficiaries predecease the policyholder depending on the terms of the policy. You may wish to transfer ownership to your Revocable Trust to give the Trustee more control over the policies in case your designated beneficiaries predecease you or you become incapacitated.Ownership of IRAs and 401ks should not be transferred to the Trust. Change-of-beneficiary forms from the insurance company or the administrator of the retirement plan must be completed and recorded with the insurer or the plan Trustee to name the Trustee of your Revocable Trust as the beneficiary of any benefits. Some annuities do not permit transfers, and others have negative tax consequences if put into the Revocable Trust, so caution is advised.If a bank, financial institution, or other governing body requires evidence of the Revocable Trust to complete the transfer, use the Certification of Trust rather than producing a copy of the entire trust agreement. The Certification of Trust provides only the necessary information without disclosing confidential details.Once an asset has been transferred to the Trustee of your Revocable Trust, all transactions regarding that asset must be handled by the Trustee, rather than by you individually and/or jointly (even if you are the initial Trustees of the Revocable Trust). For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the Trustee, it is the Trustee who has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting. Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the Trustee.Additional assets may be transferred to your Revocable Trust either by registering them in the name of the Trustee, by delivering them to the Trustee with appropriate documents to re-register the assets in the name of the Trustee, or with other transfer documents if the asset is of the nonregistered type. Withdrawals of property from your Revocable Trust must comply with the provisions of the Trust Agreement. I can prepare documents for you to accomplish such withdrawal. The Trust Agreement can be amended or revoked in its entirety, but any amendment or revocation must comply with the procedural requirements of the Trust Agreement.Federal income tax returns for a Revocable Trust are not required. If the Settlor or Settlors are also the Trustee or a co-Trustee, a federal tax identification number for the trust is also not necessary, and all income is reported using the Settlor's or Settlors' social security numbers (which becomes the Revocable Trust's taxpayer identification number). If the Settlor or Settlors are not Trustees, the Settlor's or Settlors' social security numbers can still be used, but the Trustee must meet certain other requirements in that case. After the Settlor's or Settlors' death, a federal tax identification number must be obtained for the Trust.Please call me if you have any questions about the transfer of property to your trust or about the operation of the trust in the future. This memorandum is for your general guidance only and does not encompass all possible situations.
Sincerely yours,____________________[name of attorney]End of Document

Funding Revocable Trusts (NY)
Go to: The Importance of Funding a Revocable Living Trust   |   How to Transfer or Place Specific Assets into a Revocable Living Trust   |   Other Funding IssuesMaintainedby Michael Bond, Law Office of Michael BondThis practice note contains information and instructions on funding a revocable trust in New York. One of the primary benefits of a revocable living trust is that, if fully and properly funded during the grantor's lifetime, probate can be avoided. Therefore, it is vitally important to transfer all of a settlor's assets into the trust, ideally soon after the creation of the trust, and then on an ongoing basis as needed throughout the grantor's lifetime.Trust funding must be taken seriously and professionally, as errors can result in client complaints and malpractice claims. Some attorneys choose not to involve themselves in the funding process. Others assume responsibility for certain tasks and not for others. To the extent possible, you should determine with your client in advance who will be responsible for which tasks and clearly explain that all tasks other than those you expressly agree to undertake will be the responsibility of the client unless you subsequently agree otherwise (ideally in writing). In situations where the client is to perform some or all funding tasks, you may wish to send a follow up/reminder letter approximately 60 days after the trust is created to reiterate the importance of funding the trust and to see if they wish to involve your help with any remaining tasks. If you choose not to take on the responsibility of funding the trust for your client, or if the client declines your offer to help, you should at least provide explicit instructions about the process to the client.This practice note offers guidance to you when funding a revocable living trust for your clients, as well as when advising your clients as to how they should fund it.For a full listing of key content related to estate plans for married or partnered people in New York, see Estate Plan for Individual with Spouse or Partner Resource Kit (NY).For related practice notes, see Characteristics and Uses of Trusts (NY); Requirements and Restrictions on Trust Purposes and Administration (NY); and Revocation, Amendment, and Termination of Trusts (NY).The Importance of Funding a Revocable Living TrustA grantor's assets must be transferred into the trust if it is to achieve one of its objectives: to simplify the transfer and administration of assets after the settlor's death. It is also necessary because the trustee only has control of the property if it is in the trust. If the grantor becomes incapacitated, the chosen successor trustee(s) will be able to continue managing trust assets without court involvement or other difficulty if the grantor's assets are in the trust at that time.To the extent that the settlor does not transfer assets into the trust, those assets will not escape probate. That is why a pour-over will should always be prepared along with the trust agreement to direct that any assets not held by the trust at the time of the grantor's death be transferred into the trust so that they will be distributed in accordance with the provisions of the trust. However, a probate proceeding will be necessary to accomplish this.How to Transfer or Place Specific Assets into a Revocable Living TrustThe following items are assets that are commonly held by a grantor. You should be mindful to get a clear and accurate picture of the types of assets your client owns so you can provide appropriate advice and try to ensure everything that must be transferred to the trust actually gets transferred.Personal ResidenceIn most cases, the grantor's interest in his or her home should be transferred from their own name to the name of the trustee (usually, the client), as trustee. Recording a bargain and sale deed and filing any necessary transfer tax forms will achieve this purpose. For a modest fee, a title insurance company can help make sure everything is done properly and efficiently. If the client undertakes this task, you should ask to be sent a copy of the recorded deed.A few special points about the transfer of a residence or any other real property into a revocable living trust:• There will be no reassessment because the transfer of real property to a revocable trust is not a "change in ownership" for property tax purposes.• There will be no transfer tax because the property has not been sold. However, appropriate transfer tax forms will need to be filed, indicating that it is a transfer for no consideration to a revocable trust.• While it is not legally required that the new deed be recorded right away, it is highly advisable to do so unless the terms of any mortgage contain language that makes it unwise. For example, most mortgage agreements have what is called an acceleration or "due on sale" clause, which means that the full balance on the mortgage becomes immediately due if there is a change in ownership. Since transferring the home into the trust isn't a change of ownership of the type a due on sale clause is meant to address, you or the client should contact the lender, explain what you are trying to do, and confirm that the due on sale clause won't be triggered. Ideally, you or the client will get this in writing from the lender.Cooperative or Condominium ApartmentsIf the client has such an ownership interest, you or the client should ask the manager of the cooperative or condominium about how it should be transferred into the trust. It is likely the management will have various formalities and fees associated with the transfer, but it is nevertheless important that the transfer be made. Please also note that if the client chooses to refinance their mortgage after transferring the cooperative apartment or condominium into the trust, they may have to transfer it out of the trust in order to refinance and then transfer it back into the trust after the refinance is complete. You or the client should always consult with the lender when making these types of changes to ensure compliance with the terms of the loan agreement.Other Real PropertyA new deed is required for every other piece of real property that the client owns. As noted above, it may be necessary to obtain the permission of the lender to transfer the property into the trust. If the other real property is located in another state, you or the client should consult an attorney in that state to assist in transferring that property, as different states have different rules and customs. If your client purchases real estate in the future, he or she should generally take title in his or her trustee capacity. If the client fails to do so, the new property must go through the probate process.Savings Accounts and Certificates of DepositThese accounts should be held by the trustee as trustee of the revocable trust. Banks and similar institutions commonly see revocable trusts and are used to helping their customers with necessary changes. A copy of the trust instrument or Memorandum of Trust is usually required. Note that a Memorandum of Trust is also sometimes called a Certificate of Trust or an Affidavit of Trust. See Certificate of Trust (NY) for a sample certificate of trust form. Suggest that the bank make a copy if they require it for their records. The client should keep a copy of any new card or document that they sign to effect the change. If a particular savings account cannot be transferred before a set date without losing interest, the client may decide to wait. It may be necessary to wait until a Certificate of Deposit's maturity date before it can be retitled.Checking AccountsWhile such accounts can and, in almost all cases, should be put into the name of the trust, the client may want, for a special reason, to leave one account in his or her name as an individual. However, any such account should maintain a low balance. Bank records must indicate that accounts are held in the name of the trust, but personal checks can be printed with the client's name only, without reference to the trust.Life Insurance PoliciesYou or the client must contact their insurance agent or the company itself to obtain their "Change of Beneficiary" and "Change of Ownership" forms. In many cases, the owner and the beneficiary should be the trust. Because these changes are not effective until they are received and acknowledged by insurance companies, you or the client should take care of this as soon as possible. Be sure to receive written confirmation from the insurance company that the desired changes have been effected in company records and by endorsement on the policy.If the client has personal reasons for naming a particular individual as beneficiary, he or she certainly may do so. This means that the proceeds of the policy will be distributed directly to the named beneficiaries, and not according to the terms of the trust. There are differing opinions and considerations on whether to name individuals or the trust as the primary beneficiary of life insurance policies, and these opinions and considerations should be discussed with the client to determine the primary beneficiary of such policies. For example, if the client wants an outright distribution of insurance proceeds to a named beneficiary who is an adult, and feels that person should have access to those proceeds quickly, then naming the individual as a beneficiary instead of the trust might be advisable. Also, while creditors often can go after insurance proceeds to satisfy any debts of the deceased grantor, it is perhaps less likely that will happen if the proceeds pay out directly to a named beneficiary as opposed to them being paid out to the trust.Insurance on Property Held by the TrustInsurance contracts that provide for payments to be made if there is loss or damage to assets held in trust (e.g., title insurance and homeowners insurance) should be amended. You or the client should ask their insurance agent to have the policy or policies amended. The trustee(s) and the trust should be named as additional insured parties, using the "name" suggested under Other Funding Issues below.The grantor, as an individual, should continue to be named as an insured party even though the trust and trustee are added as additional insured parties.Stock/SecuritiesThe transfer of stock to the trust must be accomplished by changing title on the stock certificates themselves. The client's broker can help make this change for free or for a small charge per certificate. If your client holds shares through a brokerage firm, the client will need to change the title on only the brokerage accounts, rather than on each share certificate.The stockbroker may request a copy of the signed trust instrument for examination by his or her firm's legal department. A copy of the signature page and the trustee powers should be sufficient to demonstrate that the trust is properly executed and the trustee has the necessary power to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds, and options, trade on margin, and conduct other trust business. Alternatively, you or the client may be able to simply provide a copy of the Memorandum of Trust.If the client holds shares in a closely held corporation, the corporation's secretary should issue new certificates in the name of the trust. The existing (old) certificates should then be canceled. Before this is done, however, it should be determined whether there is a stock restriction agreement, whether an assignment might cause some sort of forfeiture, and whether the board must approve the transfer. It would be wise to consult with corporate counsel in this regard.You or the client should send any stock certificates via registered mail, return receipt requested, with enough insurance to cover the replacement bond, in case the certificates are lost in the mail. The amount of this bond can be obtained from the brokerage, and it guards against someone else trying to transfer the certificates if they somehow fall into someone else's hands.PartnershipsPartnership interests can be complicated. A limited partnership agreement may indicate what is needed to transfer the interest.
