Such an agreement should be reviewed to determine (1) whether or not the client can transfer his or her interest into the trust and, if so, (2) how to achieve this result.Promissory NotesThe client should assign each promissory note he or she owns to the trust. The client can do so by typing on the back of each note the following endorsement:Pay to the order of ______________________, Trustee under ______________________ Trust Agreement Dated ______________________ ______________________.Go to table1The client should also assign the deeds of trust securing payment of the notes to the trust. You or the client need not record the assignment, but it should be prepared in the same manner as deeds to real property and should be retained in a safe place. If the trustee or a successor trustee needs to commence any action to obtain title to the property given as the security for payment of the note, the assignment can be recorded.IRAsIRAs must be held in the name of the individual who created the account. The client names beneficiaries on a "designation of beneficiary" form provided by the financial institution. Normally, a married person will name his or her spouse as the first beneficiary and, as alternate beneficiary, either the trust or individuals. Individuals as beneficiaries may have a few more tax elections than the trust will have. Naming the trust as beneficiary will have less such options, but would ensure that the proceeds of the account pay to beneficiaries in accordance with the terms of the trust.Retirement BenefitsRetirement benefits require special consideration since both the form of benefit and the choice of beneficiary affect the client's estate plan and can have significant tax consequences. These choices also have a major impact on married persons' lives in the event one spouse enters a nursing home. The client should consult with the plan administrator about the options available now and in the future. The necessary forms may require specific wording in order for your married clients to be eligible for the marital deduction and other benefits.If your client's employment terminated before 1985, he or she may be entitled to certain important tax advantages. The client should review his or her benefits with a pension specialist prior to making any changes to the retirement benefits.Normally a married client should first designate his or her spouse as beneficiary of qualified plan benefits.AutomobilesIt may not be advisable to transfer automobiles to the trust. If, however, your client has a number of automobiles, or ones of particularly high value, these should be transferred to the trust. Automobiles should not be transferred to the trust unless the client is certain that adequate liability insurance is available to cover substantial claims, as the trust may be made a party to any lawsuits following an accident involving an automobile that the trust holds as the owner. If automobiles are to be transferred to the trust, your client should contact the appropriate state licensing agency for the vehicle and seek instructions on how to effectuate the transfer of title to the trust. He or she should also contact the insurance carrier for the vehicle to ensure the vehicle will continue to be covered once it is placed in the trust.Lease AgreementsThe lease under which your client is the lessor should be assigned to the trust using an Assignment of Lease. Your client should notify the lessee in writing that all future rental checks should be made payable directly to the trust, not to your client individually.Cemetery PlotsYou or the client should transfer his or her cemetery plot to the trust. This is typically done through executing an Assignment of Burial Plots & Mausoleum. The director of the cemetery should receive a copy of this Assignment.Safe Deposit BoxYour client can transfer his or her safe deposit box by assigning the rights under the existing rental agreement to the trust or by having the trust itself enter into a new rental agreement with the bank. It is also a good idea for your client to give the successor trustee authority to access the box so that if your client should become incapacitated or even deceased, the successor trustee can get into the box.U.S. Savings BondsThe federal government requires the use of Form PD 1851 for transferring U.S. Savings Bonds. The back of the form has instructions. After you or the client have filled out the form, the client's signature must be "guaranteed" (medallion stamped) by an officer at his or her bank and forwarded along with the bonds to the following. For EE or I savings bonds: Treasury Retail Securities Services, PO Box 214, Minneapolis, MN 55480-0214; for HH savings bonds: Treasury Retail Securities Services, PO Box 2186, Minneapolis, MN 55480-2186..Other Personal PropertyProperty such as airplanes, yachts, horses, art work, copyrights, patents, or royalty contracts, a family business, stock options, mobile homes, partnership interests, and other rare types of property have special considerations that should be researched to determine if and how to transfer such property into the trust.Personal property items (household furniture and furnishings, books, pictures, jewelry, hobby equipment and collections, wearing apparel, and other articles of household or personal use or ornament, etc.) can be transferred by a general assignment with particular descriptions of valuable items and a provision to cover after-acquired items.Other Funding IssuesIn What "Name" Should Trust Assets Be Held?Generally speaking, assets will be retitled in substantially the following format:[Trustee name], Trustee under [trust name] Trust Agreement Dated [month day, year].It presents no problems if a bank or any other financial institution wants to hold an asset in a slightly different name. The important thing is to be sure that the trust is not confused with any other trust and that the assets are clearly held in the trust.Income Tax ReturnsAt the time the trust is established, your client should inform his or her accountant.As long as the grantor serves as the trustee (which is usually the case), he or she will use their Social Security Number as the number for the trust. Your client will file the same income tax return he or she has always filed. If a successor trustee takes over, a tax identification number must be obtained so that the trust can file an income tax return. The trust will, in effect, file a return with the same information and pay no more taxes than your client pays now, but the information will be reported on an IRS form designed for trustees and fiduciaries.The successor trustee will use IRS Form SS-4 to obtain the new tax identification number. When the number is received, it should be communicated to the client's accountant or tax preparer, as it will be used on the tax return to be signed by the trustee.RecordkeepingNo extraordinary recordkeeping is necessary beyond that which your client would ordinarily maintain. It is important, however, that the client keep records of trust property separate from property that may not be in the trust. Once changes in ownership to the trust are made, your client will receive monthly and other account statements in the name of the trust. Information your client receives from his or her stockbroker or other sources of financial information should also be adequate.Preparation of Schedule AYou or your client should prepare a Schedule A listing all assets transferred to the trust. Examples of these listings are:• 100 shares, P, G & E, common• Real property at 0000 High Street, Palo Alto, California –and–• Limited partnership interest in XXX Fund 1 unitFuture Changes in Trust AssetsIf the trust should subsequently dispose of a listed asset, it may be removed from the schedule by keeping a separate schedule and indicating its disposition and the date it was disposed of. Schedule A as originally prepared need not be changed, as it only indicates assets as of a particular day. As assets are removed, your client should simply keep a record of the change. Likewise, if a new asset is acquired by the trust, it may be added to a new schedule.The client should review the schedule of trust assets each year when income tax returns are prepared. Delete what has been sold. Add what has been acquired and confirm that it is held in the name of the trust.Future Trust AdministrationThe focus at the time the trust is created is funding the trust and administering it during your client's lifetime. At death, certain trust administrative procedures are required, and consultation by the successor trustee with an attorney will likely be necessary. If the trust has been properly funded, the cost of trust administration will be significantly less than probate. Furthermore, if the trust contains provisions for an A/B trust, significant tax savings and other benefits are likely. You may also wish to suggest that your client create a list of their professional advisers (e.g., you, their financial planner, their CPA/tax preparer, etc.), as well as the institutions holding the grantor's assets—along with contact information for each—and keep it with their trust records. This can be of great benefit to successor trustees who may not be familiar with the grantor's assets and who to contact about those assets.Current as of: 03/21/2025

Memorandum to Client 
  Thank you for the opportunity to be of service to you as the trustee of the [Trust Name] Special Needs Trust [dated] ("Trust"). The first thing you must do as Trustee is fund the Trust. Any asset transferred to the Trust should be titled as [Trustee Name(s)], Trustee(s) of the [Trust Name] Special Needs Trust, Dated [Day], [Month], [Year].1. Funding.(a) California Real Property. To transfer California real property into the Trust a deed must be recorded with the County Recorder's Office. We prepared the deed for your property, which you signed when you signed the Trust and agreed to serve as Trustee. We will record the deed. Please note that because no exclusion from reassessment for property tax applies, there will be a reassessment of property tax basis by the county and applicable property taxes will be due and payable. We also suggest you contact the homeowner's insurance company to add the Trust as an additional insured on the homeowner's insurance policy.(b) Tax Identification. This Trust is irrevocable and will be required to file tax returns, to that end, you will need to obtain a tax identification number for the Trust. You may obtain the tax number by going to the IRS website and completing the form for an EIN, requesting the assistance of a competent tax advisor of your choice, or we can obtain the number for you. The IRS does not charge a fee to obtain the tax id number. Beware of websites that offer to obtain the number for you for a fee. The tax id will also be needed for banks and investment accounts. Do not use your own social security number, the beneficiary's social security number, and do not use [a/the] settlor's social security number, as the Trust needs to have its own tax id number.(c) Personal Property – Checking Accounts, Savings Accounts and Investment Accounts. You will need to provide a Certification of Trust [which we have provided for you] and tax identification number to the banks to transfer and/or open bank accounts for the Trust. Provide the banks with a copy of the Certification and be sure to keep the original or provide the original Certification of Trust to be inspected by the financial institution and ask the financial institutions to make a copy for their records and to return the original to you. Although the banks are obligated by California law to accept the Certification of Trust we provided to you, the banks often have their own trust certification form for you to complete. If you elect to use their form (for administrative ease) and you need assistance with their form or with funding the trust, please do not hesitate to contact our office.2. Administration.This Trust was created to provide [beneficiary name] a comfortable life, while not putting their governmental benefits in jeopardy. As long as they are receiving any governmental benefits which are needs-based (i.e., have income and resource limitations) you should not pay any of the following expenses from the Trust:(a) Food(b) Shelter – including mortgage, real property taxes, and rent(c) Utilities – gas, electricity, water, sewer, and garbage removalThe governmental benefits are meant to cover these expenses and any payment of these expenses by the Trust could result in the loss or reduction of benefits.You may pay from the Trust the following:(a) Telephone, cable, and internet services(b) Entertainment, such movies, museums, theater(c) Furniture(d) Medical equipment not covered by any other benefit(e) Caregiver/companion not covered by any other benefit, but certain rules must be complied with.  If you are considering hiring caregivers, please discuss with our office prior to doing so in order to better understand the applicable rules.Please do not hesitate to reach out to our office if you have any questions regarding a specific disbursement from the trust for the benefit of the beneficiary.Federal and State income tax returns must be filed annually. Under the terms of the Trust you may hire a CPA/tax accountant to prepare the returns for you. The annual income tax returns are specialized returns and we recommend that you consult with a competent tax advisor of your choice.If you have any questions regarding the funding or administration of the Trust, please do not hesitate to call us. We look forward to working with you during the administration of the [Trust Name] Special Needs Trust.End of Document

Letter to Client 
[name of law firm][law firm address][law firm phone number][Month Day, 20__][name of client][address]RE: Funding of Revocable TrustDear [name of client(s)]:  Now that you have established your Revocable Trust(s), the Trust should be funded by transferring ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. If you own any assets jointly with one or more persons and wish to transfer those assets into your Trust, all of the joint title owners will have to join in the conveyance. After such transfer, only the person(s) who are Trustees of the Trust are considered owners.  You should examine your asset list to determine which assets to transfer. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property in any and all states, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, savings accounts, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you hold. Assets that cannot be transferred to the Trust during your lifetime include IRAs and some types of annuities.  In general, to transfer assets to your Trust, you will need to change the title for each asset from your names individually (or jointly) to each Trust, as follows:"[Name of Trustee], as Trustee of the [name of Grantor] Revocable Living Trust, dated [month day, 20__]."  The enclosed memorandum describes in detail the steps necessary to transfer many kinds of assets to your Trusts. Of course, the specific steps to accomplish this transfer will depend on the nature of the asset involved. If you would like assistance with any of these transfers, please call me. I can then contact the appropriate entities to obtain the paperwork and transfer requirements from them and prepare deeds and other documents that must be recorded.  Now that we have completed your planning, I am enclosing my firm invoice for services rendered in that regard. I believe that the statement is self-explanatory, and merely reflects the quoted fee and costs, but certainly if you have any questions about it, please let me know.  Thank you for the opportunity to work with you on your planning. Again, if you have any questions about the Trusts, or the transfer of assets to them, please call.Funding Your Revocable Trust:A Note to My ClientFrom [Attorney Name], Esq.  A major purpose of using a revocable trust as the main instrument in your estate plan is to avoid probate on the trust assets at your death. In order to avoid probate, it is critically important that assets be owned by the trustee of the revocable trust and not by you individually. Although your pour-over will provides that assets held in your individual name at your death will be added to the trust assets and distributed as provided in your trust, those assets will be subject to probate before they are added to the trust. To avoid that result, assets you intend to place in the trust should be titled in the name of the trustee, for example, "Jane Doe, as trustee of the Jane Doe Revocable Living Trust, dated August 23, 2017."  You should carefully and continually review the assets held in your own names (both assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this is consistent with your desire to avoid probate. As a married couple, one major consideration in transferring a jointly held asset into one of your revocable trusts is that the asset will lose creditor protection afforded by Florida law to assets held jointly by married couples, known as tenancy by the entireties. I will be glad to discuss the advisability of retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, holding assets in joint names with another person, or transferring assets to the trustee of your revocable trust. Generally speaking, unless there are compelling reasons to do otherwise, I do not recommend that your personal checking or small savings accounts, or tangible personal property (jewelry, automobiles, etc.) be transferred to the trust. Please feel free to call me to discuss whether my general recommendation applies to your particular situation.  On the following pages, I offer some general guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your trust. Those comments are general in nature, and I suggest that you consult me before funding your trust.1. Accounts or Certificates Issued by a Bank or Other Savings Institution  The name on the account or certificate must be changed to reflect its ownership by the trustee, and a new passbook or certificate must be issued in the name of the trustee. Be careful to confirm with the financial institution before transferring a savings certificate that there will be no penalty for early withdrawal.2. Brokerage Accounts  If you own stocks, bonds, or other securities through a brokerage account, and if the certificates for those assets are not issued in your name but are held instead in a street name or in the brokerage firm's name, the brokerage firm's records must be changed to indicate that the trustee owns the account and not you individually.3. Corporate Stocks (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations) or Mutual Fund Shares  If certificates are issued in your name, new certificates must be issued to reflect the trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. You may be able to combine all certificates representing shares in one corporation into one new certificate, but you might consider requesting a series of new certificates, one corresponding to each old certificate, in order to trace your income tax basis in any shares sold by the trustee during your lifetime.4. Registered Bonds or Debentures Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations, the U.S. Government, an Agency, any State, or Subdivision thereof)  These securities must also be registered in the name of the trustee and new bonds or debentures reissued. As with stock certificates discussed in paragraph 3., if bonds or debentures are held in a brokerage account, title to the account must be changed.5. Unregistered or Bearer Bonds or Debentures  These securities, typically issued by the United States government, an agency, a state, or a municipality, generally cannot be registered in the name of the trustee, so that there must be some other evidence that these securities have been transferred. This can be done by executing and delivering to the trustee a transfer document listing the bearer securities being transferred, which document will be retained by the trustee with the permanent records of the trust. I can prepare for you any documents necessary to accomplish the transfer. It would be helpful to keep bearer securities in a safe deposit box in the name of the trustee with a duplicate copy of the transfer document attached.6. Tangible Investment Assets (Such as Gold Bullion, Silver Coins, Art Objects, Etc.)  Because there is no proof of registration or ownership of these assets, their transfer to the trust should be handled in a manner similar to that described in paragraph 5. for bearer securities. Any new investments of this type should be purchased directly in the name of the trustee with the confirmation or other purchase slip retained with the instrument or investment to prove the trustee's ownership.7. Partnership Interests (Whether in a General Partnership or Limited Partnership)  In order to register these property interests in the trustee's name, a new certificate of partnership interest or an amendment to the partnership agreement may need to be executed. There may be restrictions against transfer in the partnership agreement, so it should be reviewed before transfer.8. Interests in Real Property (Personal Residence or Investment Real Estate)  Legal title must be transferred to the trustee by means of an executed, notarized, and recorded deed, usually a special warranty deed. Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer and whether the proposed transfer will trigger any "due on sale" clause in the mortgage or deed of trust. It is important to comply carefully with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located. A land trust deed under Fla. Stat. § 689.071 may be advisable. You should consult with me before deeding any real property (especially a residence in Florida) into the trust. I do not recommend transferring your primary Florida residence into the trust while both spouses are living.9. Life Insurance Policies, Annuities, and Retirement Plan Benefits  Ownership of these assets is generally not transferred to a revocable trust, but the death benefits can be made payable to the trustee, if desired, for distribution under the terms of the trust. Although life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate, ownership can be transferred to the trust to give the trustee more control over the policies in case you become disabled. Ownership of IRAs should not be transferred to the trust. Change-of-beneficiary forms from the insurance company or the administrator of the retirement plan must be completed and recorded with the insurer or the plan trustee to name the trustee of your trust as the beneficiary of any benefits. Some annuities do not permit transfers, and others have negative tax consequences if put into the trust, so caution is advised.  Once an asset has been transferred to the trustee of your revocable trust, all transactions regarding that property must be handled by the trustee, rather than by you individually (even if you are the trustee of the trust). For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the trustee, it is the trustee who has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting. Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the trustee.  Additional assets may be transferred to the trust either by registering them in the name of the trustee, by delivering them to the trustee with appropriate documents to re-register the assets in the name of the trustee, or with other transfer documents if the asset is of the nonregistered type. Withdrawals of property from the trust must comply with the provisions of the trust agreement. I can prepare documents for you to accomplish such withdrawal. The trust can be amended, of course, or revoked in its entirety, but any amendment or revocation must comply with the procedural requirements of the trust agreement.  Federal income tax returns for a revocable trust are not required during the grantor's lifetime. During the grantor's lifetime, a federal tax identification number for the trust is also not necessary, and all income is reported using the grantor's social security number (which becomes the trust's taxpayer identification number). After the grantor's death, a federal tax identification number must be obtained for the trust.  Please call me if you have any questions about the transfer of property to your trust or about the operation of the trust in the future. This memorandum is for your guidance only and does not encompass all possible situations.
Sincerely yours,_____________________[name of attorney]End of Document

Letter to Client 
[Month] [Day], [Year][name of client][address]Re: Transferring Assets to Your Revocable TrustDear [name of client(s)]:Now that you, as Settlor or Grantor, have established your Revocable Trust, it is time for the next step: transferring the ownership of some or all of your assets to the Trust. Keep in mind that the terms of the Trust only apply to assets that have been transferred to it. Thus, the assets that are  "owned by the trust" are the only assets that the trustee has control over.This letter provides a general description of the steps necessary to transfer various kinds of assets to your Revocable Trust. The specific steps necessary to accomplish these transfers will depend on the nature of the asset involved. If you would like assistance with any of these transfers, please contact my office. We can guide and assist you in the asset transfer process, including the preparation of deeds and obtaining and filing necessary documentation.First, review your assets to determine which ones you would like to transfer into your Revocable Trust. The types of assets that may be transferred include stocks, bonds, real property located in any state or country, tangible personal property, bank accounts, brokerage accounts, certificates of deposit, life insurance policies, corporate or partnership interests, outstanding notes, and any other type of ownership interests you may hold. Assets that cannot be transferred to the Trust during your lifetime include IRAs and some types of annuities.If you own any assets jointly with one or more persons, and wish to transfer those assets into your Revocable Trust, all of the joint title owners will have to join in the conveyance.In addition to being the Settlor or Grantor of your Revocable Trust, you may also be a trustee. The trustee is considered to be the legal owner of the assets in the Trust. Therefore, to transfer assets to your Trust, you will need to change the title for each asset from your individual name (or names, if the asset is owned jointly) to the name of the trustee of the Revocable Trust. Here is the model for new title of an asset:[name of client 1] [and [name of client 2]] as TRUSTEE[S] of the [name of revocable living trust], dated [Month] [Day], [Year] and any amendments theretoFor example:Individual Trust: John Jones and Jane Smith, as TRUSTEES of the John Jones Revocable Living Trust, dated September 24, 2022, and any amendments theretoJoint Trust: John Jones and Jane Smith, as TRUSTEES of the John Jones and Jane Smith Revocable Living Trust, dated September 24, 2022, and any amendments thereto.It bears repeating that the only assets not subject to probate at your death are the ones you have transferred to your Revocable Trust during your lifetime (in addition to assets which have their own beneficiary designations, such as insurance policies, annuities and retirement plans). Although your Will may state that any assets held in your individual name at your death (such as tangible personal property) will be added to the Trust by your executor and distributed as provided in your Trust Agreement, those assets will be subject to probate before they can be added to the trust property. Although probate in Pennsylvania is not as expensive or as arduous as in many other states, you may nevertheless wish to retitle as many assets as possible in the name of your Revocable Trust during your lifetime.You should carefully review the assets you continue to hold in your own name(s) (i.e., assets that you now own and any that you acquire later) to be certain that this approach is consistent with your estate planning goals. I would be glad to discuss the advisability of (a) retaining ownership of various assets in your name alone, (b) holding assets jointly with another person, or (c) transferring assets to the trustee of your Revocable Trust. In the following paragraphs, I offer guidance on transferring specific types of assets to your Revocable Trust. These comments are general in nature and do not cover every possible circumstance. If you require specific guidance, please contact my office.Instructions on Transferring Assets to A Revocable Trust:• Accounts or Certificates of Deposit Issued by a Bank or Other Savings Institution. The name on the account or certificate of deposit must be changed to reflect its ownership by the trustee, and, if applicable, a new passbook (or its equivalent) or certificate of deposit must be issued in the name of the trustee. Most financial institutions will require the use of their own forms. Before transferring a certificate of deposit to your trust, confirm with the financial institution that there will be no penalty for early withdrawal.• Brokerage Accounts or Mutual Fund Shares. If you own stocks, bonds, mutual funds or other securities through a brokerage account, the brokerage firm's records must be changed to indicate that it is the trustee who now owns the account, not you individually. This also applies to shares that are not issued in your name but are held instead in a street name or in the brokerage firm's name. The financial institution will probably require the use of its own forms to effect this change.• Corporate Stocks (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations). If certificates are issued in your individual or joint name(s),  new certificates must be issued to reflect the trustee's ownership of the stock or shares. You may be able to combine all certificates representing shares in one corporation into one new certificate, but you may wish to consider requesting a series of new certificates, one corresponding to each old certificate, in order to trace your income tax basis if any shares are sold by the trustee during your lifetime(s).• Registered Bonds or Debentures (Issued by Publicly or Privately Held Corporations, the U.S. Government, an Agency, any State, or Subdivision thereof). These securities must also be registered in the name of the trustee and new bonds or debentures reissued. As with the stock certificates discussed above, if bonds or debentures are held in a brokerage account, title to the account must be changed.• Unregistered or Bearer Bonds or Debentures. These securities, typically issued by the United States government, an agency, a state, or a municipality, generally cannot be registered in the name of the trustee, so there must be some other evidence that these securities have been transferred. This can be accomplished by executing and delivering to the trustee an Assignment to Revocable Trust, listing the bearer securities being transferred, which will be retained by the trustee with the permanent records of the Trust. I can prepare for you any documents necessary to accomplish the transfer. To ensure safekeeping, keep bearer securities in a safe deposit box in the name of the trustee with a duplicate copy of the transfer document attached.• Tangible Investment Assets (such as Gold Bullion, Silver Coins, Art Objects, etc.). Because there is often no proof of registration or ownership of these assets, their transfer to your Revocable Trust should be handled in a manner similar to that described above for unregistered bonds. Any new investments of this type should be purchased directly in the name of the trustee, with the confirmation or other purchase slip retained with the instrument or investment to prove the trustee's ownership.• Partnership Interests (General Partnership or Limited Partnership). In order to register partnership interests in the trustee's name, a new certificate of partnership interest or an amendment to the partnership agreement may need to be executed. The partnership agreement should be reviewed before the transfer is attempted to make sure that it does not contain restrictions against transfer.• Interests in Real Property (Personal Residence and Other Real Estate Interests). Legal title must be transferred to the trustee by means of an executed, notarized, and recorded deed, usually a Special Warranty Deed. Be careful to determine whether any existing mortgage or deed of trust secured by the transferred real estate will be affected by the proposed transfer, and whether the proposed transfer will trigger any due on sale clauses in the mortgage or deed of trust. It is important to comply with the requirements of the law of the state where the property is located. You may wish to consult with a title agent or attorney in that state to ensure that the property interest is eligible for transfer.• Beneficiary-Designated Assets, Such As Life Insurance Policies, Annuities, and Retirement Plan Benefits. The death benefits of these assets can be made payable to the trustee, if desired, for distribution under the terms of the Trust. However, please consult with me before taking this step, because these transfers may involve unexpected complications.• Life insurance policies generally pass outside of probate, just as revocable trusts do. Thus, in most cases life insurance policies are not transferred to revocable trusts. However, if there is a need to grant the trustee of your Revocable Trust control over the policy distributions, or if you fear you may become disabled and unable to administer your life insurance policy, you may wish to transfer ownership of the policy to the trustee of your Revocable Trust.• IRAs and 401Ks should never be transferred to a revocable trust during the Settlor's lifetime. You may, however, wish to consult with me about the feasibility of establishing a trust to hold retirement assets inherited by your beneficiaries.• Some annuities do not permit transfers, and others have negative tax consequences if transferred to a revocable trust. I would be happy to review your annuity contract and, if necessary, consult with the insurance company or plan trustee to determine whether your annuity is transferrable.Change-of-beneficiary forms obtained directly from the insurance company or the administrator of the retirement plan must be completed and registered with the insurer or the plan trustee to name the trustee of your Revocable Trust as the beneficiary of any eligible benefits.Any document evidencing the transfer of assets must be signed by the trustee of your Revocable Trust to indicate that the trustee has accepted the transfer. Once an asset has been transferred to the trustee, all transactions regarding that asset must be handled by the trustee, rather than by you in your individual or joint name(s), even if you are the initial trustee(s) of the Revocable Trust. For example, after corporate stock has been transferred to the trustee, the trustee alone has the power to sell, mortgage, or assign those shares of stock or to vote them at a shareholders' meeting.Additional assets may be tranvcsferred to your Revocable Trust either by registering them in the name of the trustee, by delivering them to the trustee with appropriate documents to re-register the assets in the name of the trustee, or by means of other transfer documents if the asset is of the nonregistered type.You may be asked by financial firms to provide a copy of the signed trust instrument for examination by their legal departments. In most cases, it is not necessary to provide a copy of the entire trust agreement. A copy of the Certification of Trust or the signature page and the trustee powers provisions should demonstrate to the financial firm's satisfaction that the trust has been properly executed and that the trustee has the necessary powers to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds and options, trade on margin and conduct other trust business.Withdrawals of property from your Revocable Trust must comply with the provisions of the Trust Agreement. I can prepare documents for you to accomplish such withdrawals.Amendment and Revocation of the Trust Agreement. As long as one Grantor is still living, the Trust Agreement can be amended or revoked in its entirety, but any amendment or revocation must comply with the procedural requirements of the Trust Agreement. When the last Grantor dies, the entire trust becomes irrevocable.Taxation of the Trust. As long as one Grantor is living, it will not be necessary to file a separate federal income tax return for the Revocable Trust. All income is reported on a Grantor's individual tax return under their social security number, which is used as the trust's taxpayer identification number. Upon the death of the Grantor (or the deaths of both Grantors, in the case of a joint trust), a federal tax identification number must be obtained for the Trust. This federal tax identification number (TIN) must be used on all tax information related to the Trust.Record Keeping. The trustee has a duty to keep records of trust property separate from property that is not in the Trust. You should keep a list of all assets transferred to the Trust, along with their date of transfer, on a copy of Schedule A of the Trust. If the trustee disposes of an asset, a note to that effect should be made on Schedule A, along with the date of disposition.I hope that this letter answers your most pressing questions. Please call me if you have any additional questions about the transfer of property to your Trust or about the operation of the Trust in the future. Upon the death of a Grantor or a trustee, certain trust administration procedures will be required, and the trustee should consult with an attorney at that time. Bear in mind that this memorandum is for your guidance only and does not discuss all possible situations. Please keep it for future reference.Sincerely yours,[name of attorney]End of Document

Letter to Client 
Table of Contents
Dear [name of client]:I am writing regarding the [name of disabled beneficiary]'s Special Needs Trust Agreement dated [month] [day], [year] (the "Trust"), which we [have prepared / reviewed upon your request].The purpose of this letter is to (1) describe the process of transferring assets into the trust; and (2) discuss what you, as trustee of the Trust, need to address during administration of the Trust. Please keep this letter for future reference.To accomplish the long-term goals for which the trust was created, the trustee must adhere to the terms in the trust document and the tax laws relating to trusts. If you have any questions about the tax laws relating to this trust, please do not hesitate to ask me or the person who prepares the tax returns for the Trust.The Trust describes the powers and duties of the trustee(s). Since not all trust documents (also called trust instruments) contain the same powers, closely review and familiarize yourself with the Trust terms to be certain of your authority, requirements imposed upon you, and options for taking action on behalf of the Trust. Reach out to me with questions about the Trust terms as you review them.The Trust is a First-Party Special Needs Trust, designed to hold assets while preserving [name of disabled beneficiary]'s ability to receive government benefits. The principal government benefit in North Carolina to which [name of disabled beneficiary] is entitled is called Medicaid. Transfers to the Trust can consist of cash, bonds, shares of stock, real property, or personal property, etc., but it is important to note that no additions to the trust can be made on or after the 65th birthday of [name of disabled beneficiary]. Also, once assets are transferred to the Trust, [name of disabled beneficiary] has no control of, right to demand, or ability to withdraw any assets from the Trust.1. Irrevocability of Trust. The trust is an irrevocable trust and cannot be amended, except under limited circumstances to conform with federal or state law. Please contact me if you believe this may be an issue at any time during the administration of the trust.2. Federal Tax Identification Number. Obtain a taxpayer identification number (TIN) for the Trust from the Internal Revenue Service by filing IRS Form SS-4. The TIN serves to keep the trust assets separate from those of the settlor and identifies the trust in bank accounts and tax returns. Do not use your own social security number or the disabled beneficiary's social security number. The Trust must have its own TIN.3. Funding the Trust. The ownership of one or more assets must be transferred to the trustee for the trust to be in effect. The bank or brokerage account or the asset itself, such as shares of stock, should be titled in the name of the trust, as follows: "[Name of trustee], Trustee of the [name of disabled beneficiary] Special Needs Trust dated [month] [day] [year]." (Some banks may want to use a slightly different name.)To transfer funds from checking, savings, and brokerage accounts, the TIN should be provided. A bank or brokerage firm may request a copy of the signed trust instrument for examination by the firm's legal department. We generally do not provide a copy of the entire agreement. A copy of the signature page and the trustee powers will serve to demonstrate that the trust has been properly executed and the trustee has the necessary power to borrow money, buy and sell stocks, bonds, and options, trade on margin, and conduct other trust business. A certification of trust prepared in accordance with North Carolina law may also sometimes be used in lieu of the entire trust agreement.To transfer real estate into the trust, a deed must be executed and recorded granting the property to the trust. We can prepare the deed for the property for signature by [name of disabled beneficiary / name of guardian]. Upon obtaining the necessary signatures, we will record the deed at the county Register of Deeds office. You should contact the homeowner's insurance company to notify them of the title change.4. Administration of the Trust. (a) Taxation of Trust Income. File the IRS Form 1041 and NC Form D-407 to report the income for any year in which the trust has taxable income. These forms are due on April 15 of the year after receipt of the taxable income. We strongly recommend retaining an accountant for preparation of tax returns.(b) Distributions from the Trust. The Trust was created to provide [name of disabled beneficiary] with supplemental means of support in addition to, not as replacement of, [name of disabled beneficiary]'s government benefits. Accordingly, the types of distributions that can be made to the disabled beneficiary are limited. In general, distributions should not be made for basic support, which includes (1) housing (e.g., mortgage, real property taxes, rent, etc.); (2) utilities (e.g., gas, electricity, water, sewer, waste service, etc.) (3) food; (4) clothing; and (5) medical treatment for which public or Medicaid funds are available. The government benefits are meant to cover these expenses and any payment of them using the Trust's funds could result in the loss of benefits. All distributions from the Trust are at the discretion of the trustee. Here are some examples of items for which distributions are permissible:(i) phone, cable, and internet service(ii) entertainment (e.g., concerts, museums, theater, movies, tickets to sports events, subscriptions)(iii) furniture(iv) computer hardware and software(v) education expenses unrelated to room and board (i.e., tuition, books, computers, supplies)(vi) medical equipment not covered by any other benefit(vii) travel(viii) nonfood grocery items (e.g., cleaning supplies, personal hygiene products, tissues)(ix) eyeglasses –and–(x) automobile expensesCash or cash equivalents should not be distributed directly to the beneficiary, even if they are reimbursements or prepayments for acceptable expenditures. Instead, payments should be made directly to third parties. Distributions also should not be made to or for the benefit of anyone other than the beneficiary.If you have any questions about permissible distributions, please do not hesitate to contact me.(c) Recordkeeping. Please keep detailed records of the trust assets, income, and expenditures (including the date, amount, payee, and purpose). You may be required to provide a detailed accounting of the Trust to the North Carolina Department of Health and Human Services (NCDHHS) or others entitled to an accounting upon the termination of the Trust.(d) Reimbursement to the NCDHHS. Under North Carolina law, the Trust is required to contain a provision that provides for reimbursement to the NCDHHS, upon the death of the disabled beneficiary, of an amount equal to the total amount of Medical Assistance paid on behalf of the trust beneficiary. You, as trustee, should contact the local NCDHHS office to obtain information on the amount due. The NCDHHS may file a claim against the estate for Medicaid estate recovery for the amounts due as well.5. Termination of the Trust. The trust will terminate if any of the following events occur:(a) There are no more assets in the trust.(b) The disabled beneficiary dies and the assets of the trust, after any required reimbursement to the NCDHHS, are distributed to the beneficiary's descendants or the other contingent beneficiaries named in Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust.6. Trustee Powers. A detailed list of the trustee's powers can be found in Paragraph [paragraph number] of the trust. Please make sure you understand them. I am happy to answer any questions you may have about them.
I hope that this letter has answered your immediate questions. If you would like further explanation of any aspect of funding or administering the trust, please do not hesitate to contact me.Sincerely,____________________[name of attorney]End of Document

Estate Plan for Single Individual Resource Kit (FL)
Go to: Initial Client Meeting   |   Wills   |   Trusts   |   Disability Planning   |   Powers of AttorneyMaintainedThis resource kit provides guidance on creating an estate plan for a single individual residing in Florida. An estate plan is defined as an arrangement providing for the use, management, investment, conservation, and transfer of an individual's property during the individual’s lifetime or after death. The documents utilized by counsel in creating an estate plan will vary depending on the client's circumstances, family needs, estate size, and wishes, among other things. For example, there may be reasons for a client to create a trust on behalf of their beneficiaries rather than gift assets outright to them, or vice versa. In addition to wills and trusts, there may be a need for medical powers of attorney, financial powers of attorney, or various other estate planning tools. This resource kit includes Practical Guidance materials on these potential options, including detailed practice notes, templates, and checklists.Initial Client MeetingMost estate planning matters begin with information gathering at or immediately before the initial client meeting. The details shared by the client at the beginning of the matter help to determine the appropriate and best estate planning tools for them. It is crucial for counsel to ascertain a client's objectives as well as estate information, and it is good practice for this information to be sought out prior to the initial client meeting by way of an estate planning questionnaire. An estate planning questionnaire will inquire into the client's assets; personal information about things like their children, citizenship, any prior marriages, etc.; and their desired fiduciaries.The following Practical Guidance resources will guide you through the early stages of crafting an estate plan for your client:Go to table1WillsOnce the client's estate planning objectives are ascertained and the necessary information is gathered, the attorney must consider the proper tools to carry out those objectives. In many instances, more than one estate planning tool can be used to achieve a desired objective. A will is the most basic tool in the estate planner's kit. A will enables the client to designate how their assets should be distributed after death and who should oversee that process. A will may also address certain post-death concerns, such as caring for a minor child. You must remember that this tool works only for assets held in the client's name alone and does not work for assets for which there are beneficiary designations, such as life insurance, or for jointly owned property with a right of survivorship.The following Practical Guidance resources will guide you through drafting a will or a codicil for your client:Go to table2TrustsA trust is a legal arrangement pursuant to which a grantor transfers property to a trustee to administer on behalf of one or more beneficiaries in accordance with the terms of the governing instrument. The trustee is the legal owner of the trust assets and is tasked with administering them but does so only on behalf of the beneficiaries. Although trusts can be classified according to a number of criteria, the most important to note are inter vivos trusts or living trusts, which go into effect during the grantor's lifetime and can be either revocable or irrevocable, and testamentary trusts, which go into effect after the grantor dies. There are several reasons why a client may want to include a trust in their estate plan (e.g., avoid probate, hold out of state property, plan for future incapacity, and/or keep asset information private).The following Practical Guidance resources will guide you through drafting or amending a trust for your client:Go to table3Disability PlanningA comprehensive estate plan will likely include provisions outlining what should take place in the event of a client's disability. A health care surrogate designation can be used to authorize a health care agent to make medical or other health care decisions on an individual's behalf. A living will is an advance health care directive that details the medical measures a person does or does not want taken in a variety of potential circumstances. Both instruments serve to ensure an individual's health care wishes are followed if the individual cannot enforce those decisions due to a disability or other circumstance. In addition, certain clients may benefit from guidance concerning elder law planning.The following Practical Guidance resources will provide more information and guide you through this aspect of a client's estate plan:Go to table4Powers of AttorneyPowers of attorney are often included as part of a client's estate plan. A power of attorney is the legal instrument by which an individual (the principal) authorizes another individual (the attorney-in-fact or agent) to make certain business, financial, and personal decisions on their behalf.The following Practical Guidance resources will provide more information on powers of attorney in Florida:Go to table5Current as of: 05/20/2025

Voting Trust Agreement Extension 
Table of Contents
This Extension of Voting Trust Agreement is entered into on [date], extends the Voting Trust Agreement relating to the common shares of [name of corporation], a New York Corporation, hereinafter called the Corporation.Whereas a voting trust agreement, hereinafter called the “Voting Trust Agreement,” dated [date], relating to [applicable description, e.g., the no par value common shares] of the Corporation, was entered into by and among the shareholders becoming parties thereto in the manner therein provided, with [name of trustee] and [name of trustee] as voting trustees, andWhereas [name of deceased trustee] is now deceased, and under the terms of the Voting Trust Agreement [name of remaining trustee], hereinafter called the Voting Trustee, is the sole Voting Trustee, andWhereas paragraph [Ninth] of the Voting Trust Agreement provides, in part, pursuant to the provisions of Section 621(d) of the New York Business Corporation Law, as follows:“Ninth: At any time within six months prior to the time of expiration of this Voting Trust Agreement as originally fixed or as extended as herein provided, one or more holders holding voting trust certificates issued hereunder may, by agreement in writing extend the duration of this Voting Trust Agreement for an additional period not to exceed ten years.”andWhereas the undersigned holders of the trust certificates issued under the Voting Trust Agreement desire to extend the duration of the Voting Trust Agreement for an additional period of ten years with respect to the numbers of no par value common shares of the Corporation, set forth opposite their names,It is therefore agreed:1. Extension of Term. The Voting Trust Agreement is hereby extended for an additional period of ten years, so that the trust thereby created shall terminate on [new date of termination], instead of [prior date of termination].2. Shares Affected. The extension of the Voting Trust Agreement shall apply only with respect to the numbers of common shares of [name of corporation], set forth opposite the names of the undersigned voting trust certificate holders. After [date], the Voting Trustee shall distribute to each voting trust certificate holder the number of common shares of the Corporation, if any, presently held by the Voting Trustee for the benefit of such voting trust certificate holder with respect to which the Voting Trust Agreement is not extended, upon the surrender to the Voting Trustee of the voting trust certificate or certificates representing such number of shares with respect to which the Voting Trust Agreement is not extended.3. Shareholders Affected. This Extension Agreement shall not affect the rights or obligations of persons not parties hereto. Accordingly, after [date], the Voting Trustee shall distribute to each voting trust certificate holder who is not a party hereto all of the common shares of the Corporation represented by the voting trust certificate or certificates held by such voting trust certificate holder, upon the surrender to the Voting Trustee of such voting trust certificate or certificates.4. Amendment of Voting Trust Agreement. The form of voting trust certificate set forth in the Voting Trust Agreement shall be deemed amended to reflect the fact that the duration of the Voting Trust Agreement has been extended for an additional period of ten years as herein provided.5. Endorsement. Each of the undersigned voting trust certificate holders agrees to make all of their voting trust certificates available to the Voting Trustee so that the Voting Trustee may endorse thereon a legend to the following effect:By this Extension Agreement of Voting Trust dated [date of Agreement], the Voting Trust Agreement referred to in this voting trust certificate was extended for an additional period of ten years, ending [date].
In witness whereof the undersigned voting trust certificate holders have executed this Extension Agreement on the day and year first above written, and George Hastings, the Voting Trustee, has affixed their written consent.Go to table2ConsentThe undersigned, being the sole Voting Trustee under a Voting Trust Agreement relating to the no par value common shares of the Corporation, more particularly described in the foregoing Extension Agreement, hereby consents and agrees to the foregoing Extension Agreement and to the extension of the duration of the Voting Trust Agreement provided for therein.Dated: [date]_____________________[Name]

Appointment of Successor Trustee 
1. I, [name of the individual(s) holding the power to appoint successor trustees]; [capacity of appointing individual (e.g., settlor's spouse)] of the [name of trust] dated [Month] [Day], [Year] (the "Trust"), pursuant to the power granted to me in [applicable section/article of trust agreement] of the Trust, hereby appoint the following, in order of priority listed below, to serve as successor [trustee(s)/co-trustees] of the Trust:[name of successor trustee(s)/co-trustees(s)]2. This appointment shall take effect upon [his/her/their] acceptance of the office of trustee.Executed this [day] day of [Month], [Year]._____________________________[signature(s)][print name(s) and title if applicable]
ACKNOWLEDGMENTA notary public or other officer completing this certificate verifies only the identity of the individual who signed the document to which this certificate is attached, and not the truthfulness, accuracy, or validity of that document.Go to table1On [Month] [Day], [Year], before me, [name of notary public], notary public, personally appeared [name(s) of settlor(s) or other individual holding the power to appoint successor trustee], who proved to me on the basis of satisfactory evidence to be the person(s) whose name(s) [is/are] subscribed to the within instrument and acknowledged to me that [he/she/they] executed the same in [his/her/their] authorized [capacity/capacities] and that by [his/her/their] signature(s) on the instrument the person(s), or the entity upon behalf of which the person(s) acted, executed the instrument.I certify under PENALTY OF PERJURY under the laws of the State of California that the foregoing paragraph is true and correct.WITNESS my hand and official seal._____________________________Signature
